### ⭐ [Tensor network study of deconfined quantum criticality in a one-dimensional spin-phonon model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06605v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Johannes Knolle  
**Authors:** Anton Romen, Josef Willsher, David Hofmeier, Johannes Knolle, Michael Knap  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06605v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

Numerical data displays a weakly first-order transition with length scale set by the MPS correlation length ξ. Upon increasing the bond dimen- sion χ, order parameter residuals at the transition vanish as ˆOJ2c ∼ξ(χ)−β/ν ∼ξ−K (inset), indicating a continuous tran- sition in the thermodynamic limit, see main text for details. b) Order parameter correlations at the transitions decay al- gebraically, with exponent set by the Luttinger parameter K of the critical theory.</figcaption>

Spin-Peierls Instability of DQC in a 1D spin chain: a) Static lattice distortions drive the DQCP to a strong first-order transition, displayed by a clear jump in the order parameter. b) Inside the VBS phase, the system favours a sudden distortion of the underlying lattice δ ̸= 0 to lower the ground state energy. c) The lattice instability is captured by a sudden jump of the optimal lattice distortion δopt at the transition. d) The correlation length displays clear first-order character.</figcaption>

DQCP in the spin-phonon model: a) When coupling to dynamical Einstein phonons with frequency ω0, the con- tinuous transition between N´eel and VBS phase persists above a critical phonon frequency ωc. b) Order parameter correlations at the transition decay algebraically, with power law exponent −2K governed by the Luttinger parameter of the critical theory. c) The order parameters develop a finite residual at the critical point, which vanishes with increasing correlation length ξ of the MPS ground state. The residuals are controlled using a sweep protocol initialized in one of the two ordered phases. Finite-size scaling predicts a power-law decay ˆOJ2c ∼ξ−β/ν = ξ−K, which we use to...</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06605_figures/2606.06605_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. Strong first-order transition in the spin- phonon model: Below a critical phonon frequency ωc, the transition between N´eel and VBS phase turns strongly first order. a) This is indicated by a clear jump in the order pa- rameters, b) rapid saturation of the correlation length, and c) a discontinuity in the energy derivative at the transition. d) Further evidence for a strong-first order transition is ob- tained from the VBS Binder Cumulant UB, which develops a diverging negative cusp with increasing system size. This behavior arises from a multi-peak distribution of the order pa- rameter in the presence of phase coexistence. Data obtained for ∆1 = ∆2 = 4, g = 0.25J1, ω0 = 0.4J1.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper uses tensor network simulations to study DQCP in a 1D spin-phonon model. It finds that while the critical point exists at high phonon frequencies, coupling to lattice vibrations drives the transition into a strongly first-order regime below a critical frequency. This work provides quantitative signatures like spectral function anomalies for experimental detection.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete example of how lattice degrees of freedom can fundamentally alter the nature of quantum phase transitions predicted by purely electronic models, which is crucial for understanding real materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how lattice vibrations (phonons) affect Deconfined Quantum Critical Points (DQCPs) in one-dimensional spin systems, determining if the transition remains continuous or becomes first-order.

**Main result.** The DQCP persists for high phonon frequencies but turns strongly first-order below a critical frequency ($\omega_c$), leading to an effective description by the double sine-Gordon model.

**Method.** Tensor network simulations (iDMRG/MPS) and bosonization techniques are employed across various limits, including static and dynamical spin-phonon coupling.

**Model / system.** The system is a 1D antiferromagnetic $J_1-J_2$ spin chain coupled to lattice vibrations via a spin-phonon interaction term ($H_{sp}$). The low-energy physics is mapped onto the double sine-Gordon model.

**Key observables.** Luttinger parameter (K), order parameters (Néel/VBS), phonon spectral function $A(q, \omega)$, and Binder cumulant ($U_B$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Phonon frequency ($\omega_0$), critical frequency ($\omega_c$), coupling constants ($J_1, J_2$), and the effective coupling strength $ho$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mapping to continuum theories (sine-Gordon) and using tensor network methods with finite bond dimensions ($\chi$) for numerical calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the phase diagram showing DQCP above $\omega_c$ terminating at a Potts endpoint, and demonstrating the transition changing from continuous (power-law decay) to strongly first-order (discontinuous jumps in order parameters).

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the spin model via bosonization, then incorporates phonon coupling by examining static ($\omega_0 	o 0$) vs. dynamical ($\omega_0 > 0$) limits, culminating in quantitative measurements of critical exponents and spectral functions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Deconfined quantum critical points (DQCPs) describe continuous transitions between ordered phases beyond the Landau paradigm. A simple example is the Néel antiferromagnet (AFM) to valence bond solid (VBS) transition in a 1D antiferromagnetic $J_1-J_2$ model. In analogy to the spin-Peierls instability of critical spin chains, DQCPs are predicted to be unstable towards lattice distortions below a critical phonon frequency. In this work, we use tensor network simulations to investigate this instability in the antiferromagnetic $J_1-J_2$ model coupled to lattice vibrations. We confirm the stability of DQCP for large phonon frequencies and demonstrate that the transition turns strongly first-order below a critical frequency. The instability is caused by a reduction of the Luttinger parameter due to spin-phonon interactions and we identify the effective theory of the behavior as the double sine-Gordon model. The same effective theory is known to describe the classical Ashkin-Teller model, which enables us to show that the critical endpoint is in the four-state Potts universality class. Furthermore, we provide quantitative numerical scaling results for the phonon spectral function, offering an experimental signature to probe DQCP-phonon coupling in low-dimensional materials.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.07509"></a>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that introducing controlled disorder via Se substitution into the superconductor $4	ext{Hb-Ta}(	ext{S}_{1-x}	ext{Se}_x)_2$ is necessary to induce bulk superconductivity. The mechanism proposed is a disorder-driven delocalization of carriers within a Mott-like layer, which fundamentally alters the electronic band structure and stabilizes the superconducting state.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides a detailed experimental roadmap for linking structural disorder directly to the emergence of unconventional electronic phases in strongly correlated materials, which is highly relevant for understanding emergent quantum phenomena.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the primary mechanism driving unconventional superconductivity in $4	ext{Hb-Ta}(	ext{S}_{1-x}	ext{Se}_x)_2$, specifically determining if disorder-induced carrier delocalization is responsible.

**Main result.** Bulk superconductivity emerges only when Se doping introduces quenched disorder, suggesting that the fragility of the Mott state and subsequent carrier delocalization in the T-layer are key drivers for pairing.

**Method.** The research combines multiple experimental techniques including ARPES, STM, transport measurements ($ho_{xx}, \kappa$), and theoretical modeling (Tight-Binding) to probe electronic structure changes across doping levels.

**Model / system.** The system is the layered superconductor $4	ext{Hb-Ta}(	ext{S}_{1-x}	ext{Se}_x)_2$, which features interleaved Mott-like ($1T$) and metallic ($1H$) layers. The physics centers on charge transfer, flat band behavior, and correlation effects.

**Key observables.** Superconducting Volume Fraction (SCVF), Hall coefficient $R_H(H)$, ARPES spectral weight/band structure changes, Thermal Conductivity ($\kappa_{xx}, \kappa_{xy}$), and Lorenz number ($L$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Se concentration ($x$), temperature ($T$), magnetic field ($H$), and the ratio of Coulomb repulsion to bandwidth ($U/W$) related to Mott physics.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that Se substitution primarily acts by introducing local disorder, which drives electronic transitions rather than solely controlling carrier density via charge transfer alone.

**Figures summary.** Figures show ARPES evidence of new metallic states upon doping; STM correlates SC gaps with CDW domain structure; transport measurements track the suppression of thermal and electrical properties as Se is added.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes how Se substitution modifies electronic structure (ARPES/XPS) leading to a delocalization transition, which is then correlated across multiple diagnostics: superconductivity onset (SCVF), charge transport ($ho_{xy}(H)$), and heat transport ($\kappa$, $L$).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The unconventional superconductor 4Hb-TaS$_2$ is a natural heterostructure that can be broadly understood as interleaving Mott-like and metallic layers. We study the properties of this material as a function of quenched disorder in the form of Se/S substitution and find that while disordered samples show bulk superconductivity, clean samples do not. We show that a disorder-driven delocalization of carriers in the Mott-like ($1T$-) layer forms a new Fermi surface that is absent in the cleanest samples. This suggests that one of the primary drivers for superconductivity is the fragility of the Mott state, whose delocalization brings to life a sea of strongly correlated electrons.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper models quantum phenomena in triple-layer graphene placed inside an optical microcavity, utilizing vacuum fluctuations as the mediating interaction. By calculating coherence, entanglement, and non-Markovianity using advanced theoretical tools, the authors demonstrate that these quantum resources can be precisely controlled by tuning geometric parameters like layer spacing and rotation angles.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses quantum resource engineering using solid-state platforms (graphene) coupled to photonic environments, which is a major theme at the intersection of AMO physics and quantum information theory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how quantum coherence, entanglement, and non-Markovian dynamics can be generated and controlled in a triple-layer graphene system interacting with vacuum fluctuations within a microcavity.

**Main result.** The analysis establishes cavity-confined TLG as a highly tunable platform where quantum resources are sensitive to geometric parameters like layer separation and interlayer rotation angles.

**Method.** The authors use time-dependent perturbation theory to derive an exact analytic solution for the system's reduced density matrix, followed by calculating specific quantum information measures.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a triple-layer graphene (TLG) structure embedded in a planar microcavity. The dynamics are governed by a Hamiltonian involving free electron terms and coupling to quantized cavity electromagnetic fields.

**Key observables.** Relative Entropy of Coherence (REC), Tangle ($T_g$) for tripartite entanglement, and Non-Markovianity ($	ext{NREC}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of cutoff modes ($n_{\max}$), spatial positioning/separation ratios ($d_i/L$), interlayer rotation angles ($\phi, \phi'$), and momentum transfer ($K$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the low-energy Dirac approximation for graphene and is performed perturbatively (up to second order in time).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of REC on layer separation and cutoff modes, and show the temporal evolution of non-Markovianity ($	ext{NREC}$) versus $n_{\max}$ and angular configurations.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the coupled Hamiltonian to deriving the time evolution operator via perturbation theory. It then calculates the reduced density matrix by tracing out the cavity field, culminating in the quantitative analysis of coherence, entanglement, and memory effects based on calculated traces.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We examine the non-Markovian dynamics and the generation of quantum coherence and entanglement within a triple-layer graphene (TLG) system embedded in a planar microcavity. Using time-dependent perturbation theory, we derive an exact analytic solution for the system and demonstrate how the confined electromagnetic field mediates quantum correlations between the graphene layers. We employ three complementary measures; the relative entropy of coherence (REC) to quantify quantum coherence, the tangle to assess tripartite entanglement, and a non-Markovianity measure derived from the REC to characterize quantum memory effects. Our analysis reveals that these quantum resources exhibit remarkable sensitivity to various control parameters. Specifically, we demonstrate that the number of cutoff modes, the spatial positioning of the layers, the momentum parameter, and the interlayer rotation angles provide effective control over coherence, entanglement, and memory effects. We further show that these measures exhibit an exceptional sensitivity to the rotation angle between the layers. Ultimately, our results establish cavity-confined TLG as a highly tunable platform for exploring vacuum-mediated quantum phenomena, providing a framework for the precise manipulation of quantum correlations in graphene-based photonic and optoelectronic devices.

</details>

### [Collective decay of interacting bosons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06621v1)

**Summary.** The paper explores the collective decay of interacting bosons, modeling it as an analog to the Dicke model. It identifies distinct physical regimes based on interaction strength, showing crossovers from superradiant to subradiant emission. The methodology successfully reduces complex many-body dynamics into manageable rate equations, providing deep insights applicable to circuit QED experiments.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed theoretical understanding of how bosonic statistics modify fundamental quantum phenomena like superradiance, offering predictive scaling laws ($eta = 2\alpha$ vs $eta=2$) applicable to cavity QED implementations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the collective decay dynamics of interacting bosonic modes, serving as a bosonic analog to the Dicke model of superradiance.

**Main result.** Distinct regimes are found based on interaction strength: strong interactions yield Dicke-like superradiance, while weak interactions cause a crossover to subradiant emission that surprisingly retains an analogy to rate equations.

**Method.** The analysis combines analytical arguments (e.g., adiabatic elimination, variational methods) with large-scale numerical simulations leveraging the problem's permutational symmetry.

**Model / system.** The system consists of $N$ bosonic resonators interacting via a repulsive on-site interaction ($U$) and undergoing collective decay governed by a Lindblad master equation. The dynamics are analyzed in various limits, including non-interacting ($U=0$), large-$U$, and small-$U$.

**Key observables.** Collective emission rate $R(t)$, peak emission rate $R_{pk}$, and the scaling exponent $eta$ of $R_{pk}$ with system size $N$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength ($U$), decay rate ($\gamma$), system size ($N$), and excitation density ($
u$). Key regimes are defined by the ratio $U/(\gamma N)$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis frequently restricts itself to the permutation-invariant subspace, and in some limits (small-$U$), it relies on adiabatic elimination or approximations based on the quantum de Finetti theorem.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate emission rate dynamics for varying interaction strengths, plot scaling exponents ($eta$ vs $\alpha$), and visualize the structure of the effective dark subspace states and associated rate equations.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the decay in different coupling regimes ($U \gg N\gamma$, $U \ll N\gamma$) by mapping the full dynamics onto simplified, yet physically accurate, sets of coupled rate equations or restricted subspaces.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a bosonic analog of the paradigmatic Dicke model of superradiance, comprising interacting bosonic modes subject to fully symmetric collective decay. Depending on the interaction strength, we uncover qualitatively distinct regimes of emission. For strong interactions, the emission closely resembles Dicke superradiance, with perturbative corrections arising from the presence of additional levels. For weaker interactions, the bosonic statistics qualitatively changes the dynamics, leading to a crossover to subradiant emission. Remarkably, we show that the dynamics in this regime can be described by rate equations analogous to those of the Dicke model despite the large accessible bosonic Hilbert space. Our findings are based on a combination of analytical arguments and large-scale numerics enabled by the permutational symmetry of the problem and may be probed in circuit QED experiments.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06653"></a>
### [Higher-order Symmetric Quantum Mpemba Effect in Fragmented Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06653v1)

**Authors:** Sreemayee Aditya, Sara Murciano, Xhek Turkeshi

**Summary.** This work explores the survival of the quantum Mpemba effect in highly fragmented quantum systems governed by multiple conserved quantities. By analyzing random circuits, deterministic Hamiltonians, and toy models, the authors demonstrate that fragmentation does not eliminate the effect but rather reinterprets it as a combination of memory retention (frozen sector) and active relaxation dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a general theoretical framework showing how fundamental non-equilibrium phenomena like the Mpemba effect persist even when quantum dynamics are severely constrained by multiple conservation laws and fragmentation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether the quantum Mpemba effect, related to symmetry restoration, survives when a system's Hilbert space is fragmented by multiple conservation laws.

**Main result.** A higher-order symmetric quantum Mpemba effect (SQME) is found, which reshapes the standard QME into contributions from 'frozen memory' and 'active-fragment relaxation.'

**Method.** The research employs replica tensor-network formulations for random circuits, Chebyshev polynomial expansions for Hamiltonians, and analytical solutions for dissipative toy models.

**Model / system.** The analysis covers three settings: a charge/dipole conserving random circuit; a pair-hopping Hamiltonian; and an analytically tractable dissipative pair-flip model. These systems are characterized by strong Hilbert-space fragmentation due to conserved charges (U(1) charge and dipole moment).

**Key observables.** Entanglement asymmetry ($\Delta S^{(n)}_O$), which serves as the diagnostic for the Mpemba phenomenon.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size ($L$, up to 128 in RTN), conservation laws (charge $Q$ and dipole moment $P$), and dissipation rate ($\gamma$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The random circuit analysis relies on replica tensor networks for averaging; the dissipative model uses a Markovian approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the comparison of results across three settings (RTN, Hamiltonian simulation, Lindblad dynamics), showing QME crossings and the separation into frozen/active dynamic sectors.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the QME in increasingly complex models: first random circuits using RTNs, then deterministic Hamiltonians via Chebyshev methods, and finally a simple dissipative toy model to isolate the mechanism.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A quantum system can restore a broken symmetry faster the more strongly it initially breaks it, an anomaly known as the quantum Mpemba effect. Whether this effect survives once conservation laws fragment the Hilbert space into exponentially many disconnected Krylov sectors has remained open. We address this question for circuits and Hamiltonians with simultaneous charge and dipole conservation, the paradigmatic setting for strong Hilbert-space fragmentation. Combining a replica tensor-network formulation for charge and dipole-conserving gates, which reaches the annealed Rényi-2 entanglement asymmetry up to $L=128$, with Hamiltonian simulations and an exactly solvable dissipative model, we uncover a higher-order symmetric quantum Mpemba effect: the charge and dipole asymmetries each display Mpemba-like crossings on parametrically distinct timescales. Resolving the state into frozen and active Krylov sectors reveals the mechanism: frozen fragments retain a finite asymmetry that obstructs full restoration, while active fragments host the relaxation responsible for the crossings. Fragmentation thus does not preclude the quantum Mpemba effect but reshapes it into frozen memory and active-fragment relaxation, providing a framework for the Mpemba phenomenology of higher-moment symmetries.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Quantum correlations and coherence in a two-qubit anisotropic $XY$ under magnetic field](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07051v1)

**Summary.** The paper analyzes thermal degradation of quantum resources—including entanglement and nonlocality—in an anisotropic two-qubit XY model. It establishes a hierarchy showing that coherence ($\mathcal{C}_l$) is the most resilient resource against temperature fluctuations, while anisotropy parameters allow for active control over these quantum correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a quantitative framework for understanding how environmental noise (temperature) degrades different forms of quantum information in structured spin systems, which is crucial for designing robust quantum memories or processors.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how thermal quantum correlations, entanglement, and coherence behave in a two-qubit anisotropic XY model subjected to an external magnetic field.

**Main result.** A clear hierarchy of resource degradation is established: nonlocality vanishes first, followed by entanglement, while quantum coherence persists the longest against increasing temperature. Anisotropy parameters offer tunable control over these resources.

**Method.** The analysis uses the canonical density operator $\rho(T)$ derived from the system's Hamiltonian and calculates various quantum measures using explicit matrix elements.

**Model / system.** The physical model is a two-qubit anisotropic Heisenberg XY model incorporating Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya (DM) interaction, analyzed under thermal equilibrium conditions defined by temperature $T$ and magnetic field $B$.

**Key observables.** Concurrence ($C$, entanglement), Local Quantum Uncertainty (LQU), Bell–CHSH nonlocality ($\mathbb{B}$), and $\ell_1$-norm coherence ($\mathcal{C}_l$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic anisotropy ($\delta_m$), coupling anisotropy ($\delta_c$), DM interaction strength ($D$), Temperature ($T$), and external magnetic field ($B$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system's state is described by the thermal density operator $\rho(T)$, which is analyzed using X-state approximations for certain calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of LQU, Bell nonlocality ($\mathbb{B}$), and coherence ($\mathcal{C}_l$) on temperature ($T$), magnetic field ($B$), and anisotropy parameters ($\delta_m$, $\delta_c$).

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes four key quantum indicators—entanglement, LQU, nonlocality, and coherence—by varying external fields and internal anisotropies to map out the hierarchy of resource robustness.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study thermal quantum correlations and coherence in Heisenberg $XY$ model with anisotropic interactions under a uniform magnetic field $ B $. Using concurrence $C$, local quantum uncertainty (LQU), Bell-Clauser-Horne-Shimony-Holt (CHSH) nonlocality $ \mathbb{B}$, and coherence $C_l$ as quantifiers, we analyze how magnetic anisotropy $ δ_m $, coupling anisotropy $ δ_c $, Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya (DM) interaction $ D $, temperature $ T $, and magnetic field $ B $ modulate quantum resources. At low temperatures and relevant magnetic fields, the entanglement is maximized, but exhibits sudden death for $ δ_m = 0 $, which turns into a smooth decay as $ δ_m $ increases, highlighting its stabilizing role. LQU shows that stronger anisotropy suppresses quantum correlations, while $ \mathbb{B} $ induces a non-monotonic response peaking at a critical field $ B_c $. Bell-CHSH nonlocality violations ($ \mathbb{B} > 2 $) persist below $ B_c $, but thermal noise ($ T \geq 1 $) suppresses them. Coherence $ C_l $ is most robust to thermal fluctuations, especially for high \( δ_m \), which also dampens abrupt quantum phase transitions. The DM interaction is essential for entanglement generation, with $ D $ and anisotropy synergistically enhancing correlation resilience. We identify a hierarchy of thermal degradation: nonlocality ($ \mathbb{B} $) vanishes first, followed by entanglement ($ C $), then general quantum correlations (LQU), while coherence $ C_l $ persists the longest. These results demonstrate tunable control of quantum resources via anisotropy and external parameters, providing insights for the design of robust spin-based quantum technologies.

</details>

### [A superconducting surface-code processor with lattice-surgery logical operations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06598v1)

**Summary.** This paper reports an experimental realization of lattice-surgery operations on a superconducting surface code processor. By performing syndrome extraction cycles and utilizing advanced techniques like magic-state injection, the researchers demonstrated fault-tolerant logical computation. The results establish lattice surgery as a practical method for achieving high-fidelity quantum gates necessary for scalable quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** For a theoretical physicist, this work provides crucial experimental benchmarks for fault tolerance thresholds and validates specific physical protocols (like lattice surgery) that can be modeled theoretically to predict scaling behavior towards quantum advantage.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is demonstrating fault-tolerant logical operations, specifically lattice-surgery, between superconducting surface-code logical qubits to advance scalable quantum computation.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated lattice-surgery, achieving a high logical gate fidelity of 0.943 for an RX(pi/4) rotation, validating this paradigm for fault-tolerant circuits in superconducting platforms.

**Method.** The work involves repeated syndrome extraction cycles (SECs), deterministic state preparation via lattice splitting, executing algorithms like Deutsch-Jozsa, and implementing universal gates using magic-state injection and gate teleportation.

**Model / system.** The experiment is performed on a planar superconducting processor utilizing transmon qubits configured into distance-three surface-code patches. The system supports nearest-neighbor couplings and uses techniques like CPMG decoupling to protect data qubits.

**Key observables.** Logical error rates (e.g., 0.0365 per cycle), logical Bell state fidelity (up to 0.925 post-selection), and the gate fidelity for non-Clifford rotations ($	ext{RX}(\pi/4)$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Distance of the code (d=3), physical error probabilities (e.g., CZ gate fidelity $\approx 0.996$), and coherence times ($T_1 \approx 45 \mu	ext{s}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The high logical fidelities are conditioned on the absence of detected errors, and the current implementation relies heavily on post-selection rather than fully active error correction.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental layout, conceptual lattice-merge/split operations, decay curves showing logical memory performance over SECs, and circuit diagrams detailing algorithm execution and gate teleportation protocols.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from characterizing physical hardware fidelity (gate errors, coherence times) to demonstrating basic error correction via syndrome extraction cycles, then implementing complex logical tasks like Bell state generation and Deutsch-Jozsa, culminating in the realization of universal gates via lattice surgery.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fault-tolerant logical operations are fundamental for scalable quantum computation. Here, we report the experimental realization of lattice-surgery operations between a pair of distance-three surface-code logical qubits on a planar superconducting processor. During repeated syndrome extraction cycles, the logical qubits exhibit per-cycle error rates of $0.0365(2)$ and $0.0282(1)$, respectively, after leakage events are rejected. By leveraging joint initialization and lattice splitting, we deterministically prepare a logical Bell state, confirming genuine bipartite entanglement via the error-corrected logical state fidelity. We further execute a two-qubit Deutsch-Jozsa algorithm at the logical level to demonstrate algorithmic utility in a fault-tolerant framework. Finally, to achieve universal control, we implement magic-state injection and gate teleportation to realize continuous non-Clifford rotations about the logical $X$ axis. For the logical $R_{X}(π/4)$ gate, we achieve a logical gate fidelity of $0.943_{-9}^{+10}$ conditioned on the absence of detected errors. These results establish lattice surgery as a practical and versatile paradigm for logical computation in near-term surface-code architectures, representing a critical milestone toward scalable fault-tolerant quantum advantage in superconducting circuits.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07458"></a>
### [Collective emission of subwavelengths atom-like emitter arrays in the presence of inhomogeneous broadening](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07458v1)

**Summary.** The authors report observing robust collective emission from arrays of silicon-vacancy centers in diamond, overcoming the theoretical hurdle posed by strong inhomogeneous broadening. By engineering 'superatoms' at each site, they demonstrate that solid-state emitter arrays can function as highly controllable quantum-emitter metasurfaces. This paves the way for integrating quantum light sources directly into nanophotonic circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses how to engineer coherent quantum light sources using solid-state defects in nanophotonic environments. The ability to maintain collective coherence despite large disorder is a major breakthrough for realizing scalable quantum emitters.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge addressed is demonstrating collective emission from subwavelength emitter arrays despite strong inhomogeneous broadening, a factor previously thought to suppress photon-mediated interactions in solid-state systems.

**Main result.** Collective emission was successfully observed, showing characteristic resonance shifts and decay rates that confirm the array acts as a functional quantum-emitter metasurface. This proves that collective effects survive significant disorder in solid-state emitters.

**Method.** The work combines experimental measurements of directional emission spectra (momentum mapping) with theoretical analysis using effective Hamiltonians and simulations to model superradiance probability.

**Model / system.** The system utilizes subwavelength arrays of silicon-vacancy (SiV) centers in diamond, which are engineered into 'superatoms' via high-density ion implantation. The physics is modeled by diagonalizing an effective Hamiltonian describing collective dipole interactions within the array structure.

**Key observables.** Collective resonance frequency shift ($\Delta$), photonic band $\omega(k_{\perp})$, directional emission intensity mapped vs. transverse momentum ($k_x, k_y$), and superradiance probability.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The natural linewidth of SiV centers is much smaller than the inhomogeneous broadening (tens of GHz); collective effects are observed when the effective linewidth ($\Gamma_{	ext{eff}}$) reaches specific thresholds.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximations like Born–Markov, and the initial success hinges on the 'superatom' concept created by ion implantation to overcome frequency mismatch.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate spectral dependence on momentum (Fourier space), showing characteristic dispersion relations for collective modes; other figures map emission intensity vs. momentum and show spatial mode distributions.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the problem of inhomogeneity, presents experimental measurements confirming collective shifts and band structures across multiple SiV transitions, supports these findings with theoretical modeling of superradiance, and concludes by demonstrating the viability of this platform for integrated quantum photonics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum metasurfaces comprised of subwavelength atomic arrays emerged as a promising platform for enhanced atom-photon interaction. However, realizing such a system with solid-state emitters has been considered impractical due to strong inhomogeneous broadening, which was expected to suppress the photon-mediated interactions that underpin collective emission. Here we report the observation of collective emission from subwavelength arrays of silicon-vacancy centres in diamond -- solid-state emitters whose inhomogeneous broadening exceeds the natural linewidth by two orders of magnitude -- demonstrating that collective effects such as resonance shifts, modified decay rates and directional coherent emission survive this disorder. A crucial enabling element is the implantation of a high density of silicon ions at each array site. This creates so-called superatoms, local ensembles that probabilistically achieve frequency matching across the array and enhance the collective response. We support our observations   with a theoretical analysis explaining the mechanisms that preserve the collective effects even in the presence of inhomogeneity. These observations have direct implications for the realization of subwavelength arrays in any solid-state system, paving the way for quantum-emitter metasurfaces that are naturally integrated into nanophotonic environments.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07378"></a>
### [Ferroelectrical Switching as a Probe of Quantum Damping in Magnetic Spin Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07378v1)

**Summary.** The research proposes using ferroelectric control over magnetic dimers to probe quantum corrections to spin damping. By linking polarization switching to the transition between FM and AFM exchange regimes, the authors demonstrate that magnetization dynamics can reveal signatures of quantum entanglement through advanced simulation techniques comparing classical and quantum descriptions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly bridges first-principles electronic structure calculations (DFT) with open quantum system dynamics simulations (q-LLG), providing a concrete, tunable solid-state platform to observe fundamental quantum mechanical effects like entanglement in magnetic materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to develop a method to experimentally distinguish genuine quantum corrections from classical Gilbert damping in magnetic spin systems.

**Main result.** Ferroelectric polarization switching can be used to control the inter-spin exchange coupling between ferromagnetic and antiferromagnetic regimes, providing a tunable platform for studying quantum entanglement dynamics in magnetism.

**Method.** The study combines ab initio DFT calculations to derive spin Hamiltonian parameters with time evolution simulations using both classical (LLG) and quantum (q-LLG) Landau-Lifshitz-Gilbert equations.

**Model / system.** The system involves magnetic dimers adsorbed on ferroelectric substrates ($	ext{In}_2	ext{Se}_3$). The magnetism is controlled by the substrate's polarization state, which modulates orbital hybridization and thus the exchange coupling ($J$) between ferromagnetic (FM) and antiferromagnetic (AFM) states.

**Key observables.** Magnetization traces ($	ilde{m}$), entanglement entropy ($S_{	ext{vN}}$), energy difference ($\Delta E$), and the comparison between classical LLG vs. quantum q-LLG dynamics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hubbard $U_{	ext{eff}}$, polarization state ($	ext{P}_{	ext{dn}}$ vs $	ext{P}_{	ext{up}}$), damping parameter ($\alpha = \kappa = 0.1$), and switching time intervals (e.g., 5 ps, 20 ps).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The exchange rearrangement due to polarization reversal is treated as instantaneous; the DMI calculation assumes a fixed spin canting angle of $90^\circ$.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how FE switching modulates $\Delta E$ between FM and AFM phases (Fig. 1), and show time evolution comparisons of entanglement entropy ($S_{	ext{vN}}$) and magnetization ($	ilde{m}$) between classical LLG and quantum q-LLG simulations during polarization cycling (Fig. 2).

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the physical control mechanism using DFT to link FE polarization to exchange coupling strength ($\Delta E$). It then extends this by simulating spin dynamics, comparing classical vs. quantum approaches, showing that magnetization traces carry a unique signature of entanglement-dependent dynamics only captured by the q-LLG model.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

While damped spin dynamics is important for the understanding of magnetic materials, clear signatures of \emph{quantum corrections} to the Gilbert damping mechanism remain elusive. We propose a route to distinguish quantum and classical Gilbert spin damping using ferroelectric control of a magnetic dimer. Ab initio calculations for dimers on ferroelectric substrates show that polarization reversal switches the inter-spin exchange between ferromagnetic and antiferromagnetic regimes. We formulate a magnetization-based diagnostic that relates magnetization traces to entanglement dynamics, which enables ferroelectrical on/off control of dimer entanglement. Material-informed quantum Landau-Lifshitz-Gilbert simulations illustrate how the signature of magnetization dynamics can, in principle, be used to infer the existence of quantum Gilbert spin damping. This minimal and non-volatile platform connects first-principles modeling to experimentally accessible observables and provides a starting point for voltage-controlled quantum entanglement in magnetic spin networks.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07247"></a>
### [Theory of learning of high-dimensional controlled non-linear dynamical systems (I): models and methods](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07247v1)

**Authors:** Pierfrancesco Urbani  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07247v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This paper applies advanced tools from statistical physics, particularly Dynamical Mean Field Theory, to model the learning process in Neural ODEs. It treats the training dynamics as a coupled, high-dimensional stochastic control problem between a 'teacher' and a 'student' system. The work provides theoretical predictions for how deep neural networks learn by analyzing the evolution of correlation functions in the infinite-dimensional limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on ML, the use of mean-field theory, renormalization group concepts (implied by high-dimensional limits), and non-equilibrium statistical mechanics techniques to analyze system evolution is highly relevant to AMO physics studying complex interacting many-body systems or open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The work aims to theoretically understand feature learning dynamics within high-dimensional, non-linear dynamical systems trained using online stochastic gradient descent.

**Main result.** The authors develop a framework using Dynamical Mean Field Theory (DMFT) to derive self-consistent equations governing the evolution of summary statistics for both inference and training processes in the high-dimensional limit.

**Method.** The core methodology involves recasting the learning problem into an optimal control setting, applying path integral techniques, and solving the resulting coupled dynamics using Dynamical Mean Field Theory (DMFT).

**Model / system.** The model centers on Neural Ordinary Differential Equations (Neural ODEs) within a teacher-student framework. The system involves coupled stochastic processes for state variables ($x$, $y$) and adjoint fields ($\pi$), governed by non-linear dynamics.

**Key observables.** Learning curves, correlation functions ($C_{oo'}$), response functions ($R_{oo'}$), and the training objective/loss function derived from these correlations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The analysis is performed in the high-dimensional limit, considering parameters like the learning rate ($\eta$), noise levels ($eta$), and time scales ($T_s, T_t$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key assumptions include adopting a teacher-student setup, assuming noiseless dynamics for initial DMFT derivations (though noise is incorporated later), and relying on the high-dimensional limit to close the system via summary statistics.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare DMFT predictions against numerical simulations, showing how observables like $C_{	heta	heta}$ and $m(\alpha)$ approach unity as training progresses, indicating successful student alignment with the teacher.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the coupled dynamical system (teacher/student) to formulating the objective via a Lagrangian. It then applies path integrals and DMFT to derive self-consistent equations for summary statistics, culminating in predictions for learning curves across various complex regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Neural ordinary differential equations (neural ODEs) have rapidly gained prominence as a powerful and unifying framework for conceptualizing artificial neural networks, elegantly connecting the continuous-time modeling of dynamical systems with the discrete, data-driven paradigm of modern deep learning. Beyond their practical advantages they offer fresh theoretical insights into the training and generalization properties of neural networks. The distinctive feature of this framework is its dual dynamical nature: inference dynamics, which govern the ODE evolution during forward computation, and training dynamics, which control the optimization of model parameters. This makes neural ODEs a particularly well-suited theoretical framework for studying a large variety of settings such as multi-layer neural networks (ResNets for example), autoregressive models (with next-token generation dynamics), generative models, and recurrent neural networks in theoretical neuroscience. In this work, we introduce a theoretically grounded class of models for studying neural ODEs trained via online stochastic gradient descent. We solve the training dynamics of these models via dynamical mean field theory and derive learning curves in the high-dimensional limit.

</details>

### [Complex-gauge control of anomalous Floquet corner responses in a non-Hermitian physical-synthetic photonic lattice](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07038v1)

**Summary.** The research investigates anomalous Floquet corner responses in a complex non-Hermitian photonic lattice by coupling physical and synthetic dimensions. It rigorously separates the concept of topological existence (predicted by winding numbers) from actual local optical visibility, showing three possible measurement regimes. Furthermore, it details how tuning parameters can induce an exceptional point, causing the response to adopt a unique algebraic envelope structure.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a sophisticated theoretical framework for understanding how non-Hermiticity, synthetic dimensions, and topological invariants govern measurable optical responses, offering concrete predictions about mode localization and spectral signatures that are distinct from simple existence criteria.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to decouple topological existence from local optical visibility of anomalous Floquet corner responses in a non-Hermitian photonic lattice.

**Main result.** The paper demonstrates that the same topological sector can exhibit three distinct measurement regimes (bright, skin-dark, or flux-dark), and furthermore, an exceptional point causes the response to acquire an algebraic envelope rather than simply being absent.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis involving Floquet theory, non-Bloch winding number calculations, and constructing effective two-period propagators for corner subspace dynamics.

**Model / system.** A non-Hermitian Floquet photonic lattice incorporating both a physical resonator coordinate and a synthetic frequency dimension. The system is controlled by complex gauge fields (real flux $\Phi$ and imaginary gauges $\gamma_{x,w}$).

**Key observables.** Right-state corner visibility amplitude ($S_{RR}^C$), non-Bloch winding number ($N_{	ext{corner}}$), local mode weights ($W^{(\epsilon)}_C$), and the stroboscopic signal $A_{RR}^{2T}(C)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Real synthetic magnetic flux ($\Phi$), imaginary gauge fields ($\gamma_x, \gamma_w$), and parameters governing the exceptional point ($\Delta_{	ext{EP}}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on projecting dynamics onto a corner subspace and analyzing the effective two-period propagator matrix.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate lattice structures, generalized Brillouin zone contours showing winding numbers, and time-domain traces comparing bright, skin-dark, and flux-dark regimes, culminating in visualizations of defective vs. diagonalizable responses near an exceptional point.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the physical model and its Hamiltonian structure to calculating topological invariants (winding numbers), then analyzing local measurement observables ($S_{RR}^C$), establishing the decoupling between topology and visibility, and finally investigating the role of exceptional points in modifying the time-domain response envelope.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a non-Hermitian Floquet photonic lattice formed by a physical resonator coordinate and a synthetic frequency coordinate. A two-step modulation protocol realizes a chiral walk in this physical-synthetic plane, with a real synthetic flux controlling loop interference and imaginary gauge fields controlling non-reciprocal envelopes. We show that anomalous corner pairs at quasienergies zero and \(π/T\) exhibit three distinct layers of physics. A non-Bloch higher-order construction predicts whether the \(0/π\) corner pair exists under open boundaries. The imaginary gauge fields select where the right eigenmodes accumulate. The real flux controls the local interference matrix element that determines whether the doubled-period optical response is visible. As a result, the same topological coexistence sector can be bright, skin-dark, or flux-dark in a local optical measurement. We further show that the complex gauge can tune an exceptional point of the two-period corner propagator. At this point the anomalous response keeps its doubled-period sign alternation, but its envelope becomes algebraic because of a Jordan block. These results provide a photonic route to separate topological existence, skin-selected localization, optical visibility, and defective two-period dynamics in a non-Hermitian synthetic dimension.

</details>

### [Long-range interactions assisted shortcuts to adiabaticity and battery charging in open quantum critical systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07221v1)

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that long-range interactions are a valuable resource for controlling open quantum critical systems. By analyzing energy costs and ergotropy, the authors show that LRIs significantly enhance both Shortcuts to Adiabaticity implementation and the charging capability of quantum batteries compared to nearest-neighbor models.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work bridges non-equilibrium thermodynamics, quantum control theory, and condensed matter physics by showing how interaction topology (long-range vs. short-range) dictates the feasibility and efficiency of advanced quantum protocols like STA.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how long-range interactions (LRIs) can enhance quantum control protocols, specifically Shortcuts to Adiabaticity (STA), in open quantum critical systems and improve the charging efficiency of quantum batteries under dissipation.

**Main result.** LRIs provide a significant advantage over short-range interactions by reducing the cost of STA control and enhancing the resultant ergotropy during quantum battery charging near certain critical points.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving counterdiabatic Hamiltonians in momentum space, calculating energy costs (heat/power), and modeling dissipation using Lindblad master equations for open systems.

**Model / system.** The model is a Long-Range Kitaev (LRK) chain of spinless fermions coupled to a fermionic bath. The dynamics are governed by the Hamiltonian $H_0(t)$ which includes long-range coupling terms decaying as $|i-j|^{-\alpha}$.

**Key observables.** Heat current ($	ext{I}_{	ext{STA}}$), power ($P_{	ext{STA}}$), ergotropy ($W_{	ext{tot}}(t)$), and the counterdiabatic coupling strength ($h_m$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction decay exponent $\alpha$ (defining long-range vs. short-range regimes), critical points ($\mu = \pm 1$), and temperature/inverse temperature ($eta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is coupled to a fermionic bath, leading to an instantaneous thermal state; calculations often rely on low-momentum expansions near criticality.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the analytical and numerical results for the CD coupling strength $h_m(\mu, \alpha)$ across different interaction regimes ($\alpha$) and critical points ($\mu=-1$ vs. $\mu=+1$).

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes STA protocols in open systems, first deriving the necessary counterdiabatic terms ($h_m$), then calculating energy costs (heat/power), and finally applying these concepts to optimize quantum battery charging via ergotropy enhancement.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work we show that long-range interactions can be significantly beneficial for implementing shortcuts to adiabaticity (STA) in many-body open quantum critical systems driven out of equilibrium, as well as for charging quantum batteries in the presence of dissipation. In sharp contrast to short range interactions where passage through criticality may demand STA control with non-zero interactions between infinitely distant spins, using the example of a Kitaev chain with long-range couplings, we find that the corresponding control may involve involve interaction strength with decays algebraically with distance. In case of non-unitary control, the advantage of long-range interactions manifest through reduction in the cost of STA. We further propose a modified STA technique aimed at charging a quantum battery in the presence of dissipation, in which case long-range interactions may enhance the resultant ergotropy. Our results establish long-range interactions as a valuable resource for quantum control, with direct implications for quantum technologies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07268"></a>
### [Modelling time-irreversible avalanches](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07268v1)

**Summary.** This paper develops a theoretical model based on the Brownian Gyrator to study time-irreversible fluctuations observed in phenomena like friction and magnetic avalanches. By analyzing the average shape of these 'avalanches,' the authors quantify the degree of asymmetry. They establish a quantitative link between this measured asymmetry (skewness) and the rate of entropy production, providing a powerful diagnostic tool for non-equilibrium physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous statistical mechanical framework for quantifying non-equilibrium signatures (like time-asymmetry) in complex physical processes, linking microscopic dynamics to macroscopic irreversibility measures.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the time reversal symmetry of fluctuations, particularly by analyzing the average shape of avalanches in non-equilibrium systems.

**Main result.** The proposed Brownian Gyrator model successfully reproduces experimentally observed asymmetric average avalanche shapes, correlating this asymmetry with measures of entropy production rates.

**Method.** The authors use analytical extensions to paradigmatic models (like ABBM) and compute average bridge/avalanche shapes using propagator techniques in stochastic thermodynamics.

**Model / system.** The core model is an extension of the ABBM model, related to the Brownian Gyrator, which describes motion under anisotropic thermal fluctuations. It can be framed within rate-and-state friction models.

**Key observables.** Average avalanche/bridge shape $\langle v(t) angle_	au$, asymmetry (skewness), and entropy production rates ($S_{	ext{prod}}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Anisotropy parameters ($\gamma_x, \gamma_y$), bath temperatures ($T_x, T_y$), and the duration of the avalanche/bridge ($	au$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on extending known models to capture non-equilibrium features; numerical methods require careful handling of noise terms and integration steps.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare model predictions (e.g., average bridge shapes vs. duration $	au$) against granular friction experiments, showing characteristic time-reversal asymmetry and correlating maximal skewness with entropy production.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the theoretical framework by generalizing existing models to include anisotropy, deriving equations of motion for the generalized process, calculating the average shape using propagators, and finally correlating the resulting asymmetry (skewness) with thermodynamic quantities like entropy production.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the problem of the time reversal symmetry of fluctuations, as witnessed by the average shape of avalanches. This quantity has been measured in a variety of systems, ranging from magnetic materials to earthquakes. Although an asymmetric shape is often observed, which is a signature of a non-equilibrium dynamics, there is no general theoretical control of this feature. In this paper, we propose a non equilibrium extension of a paradigmatic model for ``crackling-noise'', the so called ABBM model. Our model is strictly related to the Brownian Gyrator, which has been previously introduced in stochastic thermodynamics as the simplest model for thermal anisotropy, but it can also be framed in the context of rate-and-state models. It reproduces the phenomenology observed in experiments on granular friction, and allows for a systematic theoretical study of the asymmetry. We manage to correlate a measure of asymmetry, that can be easily computed in experiments, with the entropy production rates of the dynamics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** The authors report an experimental realization of a two-color dipole trap using optical nanofibers to confine cold atoms near the fiber surface. They utilized machine learning to optimize the trapping process, achieving high optical densities and estimating $\sim 1400$ trapped atoms. The key finding is that while strong coupling is achieved, the measured lifetime is limited by technical noise rather than fundamental quantum processes.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it pushes the boundaries of atom-photon interfaces by demonstrating high coupling strengths (high OD) in a shallow trap regime. The combination of ML optimization with precise quantum characterization makes it a model for advanced open quantum system experiments.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The goal was to create a robust, high optical-depth nanofiber trap for coupling quantum information from cold atoms over long distances.

**Main result.** The experiment successfully achieved an optical depth exceeding 15 and trapped approximately 1400 Cesium atoms with a measurable lifetime of 28 ms. The study concluded that technical noise, rather than fundamental processes, limits the atom loss rate.

**Method.** The researchers employed machine learning optimization on the cooling sequence parameters and used transmission spectroscopy to characterize the optical depth and trap lifetime.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a two-color evanescent-field dipole trap using Cesium atoms near an optical nanofiber. The trapping mechanism combines blue-detuned (repulsive) and red-detuned (attractive) fields, creating a 1D lattice of trapping sites.

**Key observables.** Peak optical depth ($\approx 15.1$), estimated atom number ($\approx 1400$), collective cooperativity ($\approx 28$), and trap lifetime ($28 	ext{ ms}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trap depths ($\sim 100 \mu	ext{K}$), cooling detunings (up to $22 	ext{ MHz}$), and the characteristic loss rate dominated by technical noise.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary limitation on lifetime is attributed to technical noise, not intrinsic recoil or vacuum processes. The calculation of single-atom optical depth might overestimate the true value due to wavefunction spreading.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the laser configuration and calculated potential energy landscapes. Figure 4 shows transmission measurements on resonance (left) and the exponential decay of optical depth versus absorbed probe photons (right).

**Paper structure.** The paper details the setup using a two-color dipole trap, describes the use of machine learning to optimize the cooling sequence parameters, presents characterization results like high optical depth measurement, and finally analyzes the lifetime decay to determine the limiting loss mechanism.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optical nanofibers provide a way of coupling quantum information in cold atoms across large distances, however, this coupling requires atoms to reside close to the nanofiber surface. Atoms can be trapped close to the surface using a two-color dipole trap. Here we present our experimental realization of a two-color dipole trap. We optimize the number of trapped atoms using a machine learning algorithm and measure the optical density via the transmission. We estimate the number of atoms in the trap to be approximately 1400 and the lifetime of the atoms in the trap to be 28 ms. Machine-learning optimization improved the on-resonance optical depth from 0.5 in the initial optimization stage to optical depths exceeding 15.

</details>

### [Continuous-time quantum control across an exponentially small bottleneck in a frustrated Ising ring model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07168v1)

**Authors:** Vincenzo Roberto Arezzo, Kiran Thengil, Giuseppe Santoro

**Summary.** This paper develops advanced quantum control techniques to prepare the ground state of a frustrated Ising ring model, which typically suffers from exponentially small energy gaps. By optimizing the annealing schedule continuously rather than using fixed ramps, the authors demonstrate that the required computation time scales only linearly with system size. This provides a significant theoretical breakthrough for simulating complex many-body systems trapped by spectral bottlenecks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses fundamental limitations in quantum simulation algorithms by providing a concrete method to achieve polynomial scaling for ground state preparation in systems with known spectral bottlenecks.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is overcoming exponentially long annealing times required by standard Quantum Annealing (QA) when the system encounters an exponentially small spectral gap bottleneck.

**Main result.** By optimizing continuous-time control schedules, the required annealing time scales linearly with system size rather than exponentially, successfully bypassing the bottleneck mechanism.

**Method.** The authors use quantum optimal control theory to optimize smooth, continuous-time annealing schedules using a dressed-CRAB approach and gradient optimization techniques.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on a frustrated Ising ring model, whose dynamics are governed by an interpolating Hamiltonian between a driving field ($\hat{H}_x$) and the target problem ($\hat{H}_z$).

**Key observables.** Residual energy ($\epsilon$), threshold annealing time ($	au^*$), and population distributions across instantaneous eigenstates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size ($N$), minimum spectral gap ($\Delta_{\min}$), and the optimization schedule parameters (Fourier coefficients).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on optimizing smooth, continuous-time schedules; counter-diabatic corrections are analyzed but found not to improve results beyond optimal scheduling.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the frustrated Ising ring structure, show population dynamics near bottlenecks, and compare residual energies for optimized vs. fixed annealing schedules across varying system sizes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QA problem on the frustrated Ising ring, details the continuous-time control optimization using dressed-CRAB, analyzes the resulting nonadiabatic dynamics (population transfer), compares performance against standard ramps, and finally examines the role of counter-diabatic corrections.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Continuous-time Quantum Annealing (QA) is a strategy for preparing the ground state of nontrivial many-body systems. In its standard form, the dynamics is generated by a time-dependent interpolation between a simple driving Hamiltonian and the target problem Hamiltonian, usually implemented through a linear schedule. This approach faces the crucial bottleneck of small spectral gaps, which may require exponentially long annealing times to ensure adiabaticity. Here, we show how to implement quantum control over the annealing schedule in a frustrated Ising ring, one of the simplest models exhibiting an exponentially small bottleneck gap. By optimizing smooth continuous-time annealing schedules with a dressed-CRAB approach, and using a digitized representation of the dynamics to efficiently evaluate gradients, we construct protocols that strongly outperform standard fixed schedules. The optimized dynamics bypasses the bottleneck through a strongly nonadiabatic mechanism, leading to efficient ground-state preparation despite the exponentially small minimum gap. In particular, the annealing time required to reach a fixed residual-energy threshold is found to grow linearly with system size rather than exponentially. We further examine a lowest-order variational counter-diabatic correction and find that, once schedule optimization is allowed, it does not lead to any improvement.

</details>

### [Vortex dynamics in rotating dipolar supersolids across Josephson and self-trapping regimes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07266v1)

**Summary.** The paper explores how vortices behave in rotating dipolar supersolids by modeling the system as weakly linked condensates. It demonstrates that combining Josephson oscillation physics with self-trapping dynamics offers a powerful, tunable protocol to precisely trigger and track vortex nucleation, transport, and pair annihilation events within the lattice structure.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a detailed, controllable mechanism to study fundamental quantum phenomena like phase slips and vortex dynamics using ultracold atomic systems, which is highly relevant to understanding superfluidity in complex geometries.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates vortex nucleation and transport dynamics within a rotating dipolar supersolid across distinct physical regimes: Josephson oscillations and macroscopic self-trapping.

**Main result.** Local phase differences provide a predictive framework, showing that combining Josephson and self-trapping protocols allows for tunable control over triggering vortex nucleation, tracking transport, and observing pair annihilation mechanisms.

**Method.** The dynamics are modeled by analyzing the system as an array of weakly linked condensates, using local phase differences ($\varphi$) and population imbalances ($Z$) derived from the extended Gross-Pitaevskii equation (eGPE).

**Model / system.** The physical system is a dipolar Bose gas forming a supersolid arranged in a triangular droplet lattice. The theoretical framework utilizes the eGPE, incorporating dipole-dipole interactions and Lee-Huang-Yang corrections.

**Key observables.** Vortex position ($Y_n, Y_t$), phase differences ($\varphi$), population imbalance ($Z$), vortex/antivortex pair creation/annihilation events.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rotation frequency ($\Omega$), Josephson regime vs. Self-Trapping (ST) regime transitions, and the necessity of a three-droplet description near lattice vertices.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on approximating the system using local phase differences between neighboring droplets; specifically, the two-droplet approximation fails near hexagon vertices, necessitating a three-droplet model for accuracy.

**Figures summary.** Figures schematically illustrate the triangular lattice structure and paths for vortex nucleation (solid green) and transport (dashed orange). Later figures compare eGPE simulations with approximations, highlighting pair creation/annihilation events during rotation ramps.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the physical model (dipolar supersolid in a droplet lattice), develops theoretical tools based on local phase differences, analyzes dynamics separately in Josephson and Self-Trapping regimes, and culminates by showing how combining these two protocols allows for comprehensive control over topological vortex events.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate vortex nucleation and transport in a rotating dipolar supersolid arranged in a triangular droplet lattice, exploiting its description as an array of weakly linked condensates. By considering both Josephson and macroscopic self-trapping dynamics, we show that local phase differences between droplets provide a compact and highly predictive framework to explore a wide range of vortex behaviors. In particular, Josephson oscillations can be devised to induce vortex nucleation and motion near the vertices of the low-density hexagonal lattice (between droplets), while self-trapping dynamics induce running phases that enable directed vortex transport, which may be accompanied by vortex-antivortex pair creation and annihilation over finite time scales. Comparison with simulations based on the extended Gross-Pitaevskii equation demonstrates that a three-droplet description is essential to capture vortex motion near hexagon vertices. Together, Josephson and self-trapping dynamics provide a tunable protocol to trigger and track vortex nucleation, transport, and vortex-antivortex pair annihilation, revealing the microscopic topological mechanisms underlying phase slips in rotating dipolar supersolids.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06810"></a>
### [Cyclic ladder operators and hidden Weyl-Heisenberg structure in a Floquet system](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06810v1)

**Authors:** Tianao Wu, Li Ge  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06810v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06810_figures/2606.06810_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. (a) Schematics of a one-dimensional Floquet system with temporal period T and spatial period Λ. Couplings are marked by bars, and one unit cell is boxed by the dashed line. Sublattices A and B are marked by filled and open dots. **Summary.** This theoretical paper explores cyclic ladder operators in finite quantum systems, showing that if these systems obey a Weyl-Heisenberg commutation relation, their energy spectrum must be equally spaced. This principle is rigorously demonstrated using a one-dimensional Floquet lattice model, revealing a deep algebraic connection between the system's time evolution and its underlying discrete symmetries.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides deep connections between discrete spectral properties (equally spaced ladders) and fundamental algebraic structures (WH group), offering insights into the underlying symmetries governing dynamics in periodically driven quantum systems relevant to AMO physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates cyclic ladder operators in finite-dimensional quantum systems, particularly exploring how an underlying Weyl-Heisenberg (WH) commutation relation dictates the energy spectrum and dynamics.

**Main result.** It demonstrates that the existence of a WH structure forces the system to possess an equally spaced energy ladder, which is confirmed by showing the temporal evolution operator relates directly to the cyclic ladder operators in specific limits.

**Method.** The analysis involves deriving algebraic properties of generalized ladder operators for finite Hilbert spaces and applying these concepts to model dynamics using Floquet theory.

**Model / system.** The primary models are general quantum systems with discrete spectra, exemplified by a one-dimensional tight-binding lattice subjected to periodic driving (Floquet system).

**Key observables.** Equally spaced energy ladder ($E_n$), the temporal evolution operator $U(T)$, and the diagonal form of cyclic ladder operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of sites ($N$) in the lattice, the time period ($T$), and the coupling strengths ($	heta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the existence or emergence of an underlying Weyl-Heisenberg commutation relation to enforce spectral regularity.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate schematics of the 1D Floquet system, its band structures under varying driving conditions (including Dirac cone formation), and particle movement trajectories on a finite lattice.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces cyclic ladder operators in general finite systems, establishing the link to WH algebra. It then applies this framework to a specific one-dimensional Floquet lattice model, analyzing how the time evolution operator $U(T)$ manifests these algebraic symmetries.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ladder operators, found in the quantum harmonic oscillator and other quantized systems, provide an elegant approach to solving or understanding otherwise intricate physics problems. In this Letter, we discuss cyclic ladder operators in both Hermitian and non-Hermitian systems with a finite Hilbert space, with the highest (lowest) level directly descending (ascending) to the lowest (highest) level via a single raising (lowering) operation. We show that an equally spaced energy ladder emerges when these systems have an underlying Weyl-Heisenberg commutation relation, with the cyclic ladder operators and the temporal evolution operator behaving as the generators of the Weyl-Heisenberg group. We further illustrate such a system using a one-dimensional Floquet lattice, where the cyclic ladder operators become diagonal and the temporal evolution simplifies to a permutation matrix after a Floquet period. Our findings reveal a hidden relation between non-trivial dynamics and algebraic principles in Floquet systems, which may exist for other quantum numbers as well besides the energy levels.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.07111"></a>
### [Failure of the Quench Action Formalism for Mott Insulator Initial States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07111v1)

**Authors:** Garry Goldstein  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07111v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07111_figures/2606.07111_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
