**Summary.** This theoretical paper proves that the Quench Action Formalism fails to accurately describe quantum system evolution after a sudden change (quench) into an integrable model. Using explicit calculations on systems like Mott insulators quenched to Lieb-Liniger gases, the authors show that the true overlaps are highly singular, contradicting the formalism's assumption of smooth dependence.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for theorists studying non-equilibrium dynamics in quantum many-body systems, as it invalidates a widely used simplifying assumption (the quench action) for predicting thermalization or relaxation pathways.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper challenges the validity of the Quench Action Formalism, a theoretical tool used to predict quantum system relaxation after a quench into an integrable model.

**Main result.** By explicit counterexamples involving Mott insulator quenches to Lieb-Liniger gas and XXZ models, the authors demonstrate that the overlap between initial states and eigenstates exhibits highly singular behavior, violating the smooth functional dependence assumed by the formalism.

**Method.** The analysis involves calculating quantum overlaps using bosonic creation/annihilation operators in specific limits (e.g., Tonks-Girardeau limit) and comparing these results against the predicted exponential form of the quench action.

**Model / system.** The study examines quenches between Mott insulators and Lieb-Liniger gases, as well as overlaps within the XXZ model with crystal states. The underlying physics involves integrable quantum many-body systems.

**Key observables.** The primary observable is the overlap $\langle k_{1},...k_{N} | \Psi_{0} angle$ between the initial state and the eigenstates of the final Hamiltonian.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Key parameters include the coupling constant $c$ (for Lieb-Liniger gas) and anisotropy $\Delta$ (for XXZ model), analyzed in specific limits like leading order in $1/c$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The paper critiques the core assumption of the Quench Action Formalism: that the overlap must depend smoothly on the quasiparticle density $ho(\mathbf{k})$.

**Figures summary.** Not specified by notes, but calculations involve comparing derived overlap expressions (e.g., Eq. 29 vs Eq. 6) to show discrepancies.

**Paper structure.** The paper first introduces the formalism and then presents explicit counterexamples using two distinct physical systems: Mott insulator $	o$ Lieb-Liniger gas, followed by an appendix detailing similar failures in the XXZ model with crystal states.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The quench action formalism relies on the assumption that the overlap between a generic initial state $\left|Ψ_{0}\right\rangle $ and an eigenstate of an integrable model - defined through the rapidities $\left|k_{1},...k_{N}\right\rangle $ - can be written as: \begin{equation} \left\langle k_{1},...k_{N}\midΨ_{0}\right\rangle =\exp\left(-S_{Ψ_{0}}\left(ρ\left(k\right)\right)\right),\label{eq:Exponential} \end{equation} where $ρ\left(k\right)$ is the quasiparticle density of the state $\left|k_{1},...k_{N}\right\rangle $ and $S_{Ψ_{0}}$ is some smooth function of $ρ\left(k\right)$ that depends on $Ψ_{0}$. In particular the quench action formalism assumes the overlap depends smoothly on the quasiparticle density $ρ\left(k\right)$. In this work, by explicit counter example, we show that this is not the case. We consider the quench between a Mott insulator and a Lieb Liniger gas. We show that the overlap between the ground state of the Mott insulator and arbitrary eigenstates of the Lieb Liniger gas has a highly singular behavior and no expression like Eq. (1) applies. We do so within the Tonks Girardeau limit of the Lieb Liniger gas and to leading order in the $1/c$ expansion for the overlap (with $c$ being the coupling constant of the Lieb Liniger gas). In the Appendix we show similar results for overlaps in the XXZ model with crystal states.

</details>

### [Fluctuation-induced and quantum effects in nanofluidic transport](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06693v1)

**Summary.** This research addresses nanoscale fluid transport by developing theoretical frameworks that incorporate electronic fluctuations, leading to the concept of fluctuation-induced quantum friction. By unifying hydrodynamics and electronics into a single matrix, it provides quantitative predictions for coupled transport coefficients relevant to energy applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it tackles the interface between fluid dynamics and condensed matter physics using advanced quantum statistical tools (Keldysh formalism), providing a framework for understanding non-equilibrium transport phenomena in confined geometries.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is understanding fluid transport at nanoscale dimensions where liquid flow becomes critically sensitive to the wall's internal electronic degrees of freedom, necessitating a unified description beyond classical continuum models.

**Main result.** Fluctuation-induced quantum friction effects are predicted to couple hydrodynamic and electronic transport into a single hydro-electronic matrix. The theory establishes quantum fluctuation-dissipation theorems linking structural correlation functions to response functions.

**Method.** The work combines theoretical derivations using Keldysh formalism (Quantum Field Theory) with analysis of experimental results from nanofluidic measurements on various solid substrates.

**Model / system.** The system involves liquid confined in nanoscale pores (e.g., CNTs, 2D materials) interacting with solid walls whose electronic structure dictates transport properties. Models range from the PNPS framework to full quantum treatments incorporating electron-phonon and fluid-electron coupling.

**Key observables.** Hydrodynamic friction coefficient ($\lambda$), ionic conductance ($G_i$), critical confinement length for freezing, and measurable electric currents generated by fluid motion (e.g., phonon drag).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nanoscale dimensions ($\lesssim 10$ nm), temperature ($T$), quantum mechanical parameters ($\hbar\omega/k_B T$), and the electronic nature of the confining wall (metallic vs. semiconducting).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes that mean-field descriptions fail at small scales, necessitating the inclusion of fluctuating quantum degrees of freedom; approximations are made in deriving analytical formulas from complex field theories.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate relationships such as water friction coefficient vs. CNT radius, and conceptual diagrams showing the Superionic state or energy transfer mechanisms between electrons and water molecules.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first identifying the failure points of classical models (PNPS) at small scales, then introducing quantum effects like fluctuation-induced friction via QFT formalism, and finally benchmarking these theories against diverse experimental measurements on specific nanostructures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The hydrodynamic wall has traditionally been considered a featureless object, whose only role is to provide a boundary for fluid flow. Yet, there is now ample evidence that at nanometer scales, liquid flows are sensitive to the wall's internal -- in particular, electronic -- degrees of freedom. Here, after reviewing the experimental evidence for nanoscale liquid-electron couplings, we present the theoretical advances that have allowed for their quantitative understanding. We discuss how a quantum description of the liquid-solid interface reveals the influence of electron dynamics on classical fluid transport, in the form of the fluctuation-induced quantum friction effect. Quantum friction is at the root of liquid-electron coupled transport phenomena, that may be combined into a hydro-electronic transport matrix. We present analytical formulas for the hydro-electronic transport coefficients, that allow for their quantitative estimation in practical cases; we further outline the potential consequences of coupled liquid-electron transport for the water-energy nexus. Fluctuation-induced and quantum effects at liquid-solid interfaces represent an emerging interface between fluid dynamics and condensed-matter physics, and a largely uncharted territory for both theory and experiment.

</details>

### [Experimental Demonstration of Free-Space Unidimensional Continuous-Variable Quantum Key Distribution Under High Detector Noise](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07206v1)

**Summary.** The authors experimentally realized Unidimensional CV-QKD over free space, proving its feasibility despite significant detector electronic noise. They found that secure key generation is only possible when assuming the detector is trusted, achieving a rate of 270 kbps. This work underscores that practical quantum communication systems must account for and mitigate realistic noise sources like detector electronics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a concrete, high-noise experimental benchmark for quantum communication protocols, directly addressing practical limitations (detector noise) that often plague theoretical models in quantum optics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper experimentally demonstrates Unidimensional Continuous-Variable Quantum Key Distribution (UD-CVQKD) in a free-space setting while operating under challenging conditions of high detector electronic noise.

**Main result.** Secure key generation was achieved using the Trusted Detector model, reaching a maximum secret key rate of 270 kbps at an optimal modulation variance. The study highlights that detector trust is critical, as the Untrusted Detector model failed to yield positive rates under these noisy conditions.

**Method.** The security analysis employed worst-case assumptions over unknown correlation parameters ($C_p$) using established key rate formulas derived from mutual information and Eve's accessible information.

**Model / system.** The system uses polarized coherent states transmitted through a free-space channel. The protocol is simplified to modulate only one quadrature (UD-CVQKD) for implementation simplicity.

**Key observables.** Secret key rate ($K_{TD}$), measured quadratures ($\hat{X}, \hat{P}$), and the secure region in parameter space $(V_{B_p}, C_p)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** High detector electronic noise (1.4 shot-noise units), modulation variance ($V_A$), and channel transmittance ($T_x$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis critically depends on the assumption of a Trusted Detector model to ensure positive key rates; performance is limited by low-loss channels.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the physical and secure operational regions in parameter space for both trusted (TD) and untrusted (UTD) detector models, showing how high noise shrinks the secure region significantly.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from describing the physical setup and measurement techniques to deriving security bounds under different noise assumptions. It then numerically analyzes the key rate dependence on modulation variance and channel loss, culminating in experimental validation of feasibility.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Continuous-variable quantum key distribution (CV-QKD), which uses quadratures of the electromagnetic field, enables practical quantum communication using standard telecommunication technologies. Unidimensional CV-QKD (UD-CVQKD) simplifies the implementation by restricting modulation to a single quadrature. In this work, we experimentally demonstrate a free-space Gaussian-modulated UD-CVQKD system operating under a high detector electronic-noise regime (1.4 shot-noise units). The system employs polarized coherent states with signal and local oscillator co-propagating in the same spatial mode in orthogonal polarizations, ensuring stable interference. System security is analyzed under both untrusted (UTD) and trusted (TD) detector noise models. While no positive secret key rate is obtained under the UTD model, the TD model enables secure key generation over a finite range of modulation variances, highlighting the critical role of detector trust in high-noise conditions. A maximum secret key rate of 270 kbps is achieved at an optimal modulation variance of 11.57. Furthermore, secure operation requires high-transmittance (low-loss) channels under such noise conditions. This study demonstrates the practical feasibility of free-space UD-CVQKD in realistic high electronic-noise detection constraints and highlights detector electronic noise as a key limiting factor in practical systems.

</details>

### [Riemann Rarefaction Waves in a Strongly Interacting Fermi Gas](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06659v1)

**Summary.** This work uses strongly interacting Fermi gases to study rarefaction waves in an expanding gas into vacuum. The authors demonstrate that the expansion exhibits strong self-similarity matching Euler theory at unitarity. By analyzing deviations away from this limit, they probe the role of viscosity and thermal effects on nonlinear hydrodynamics in a highly controllable quantum system.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a highly controllable platform—ultracold atoms—to test fundamental concepts of nonlinear fluid dynamics, allowing direct comparison between ideal continuum theories (Euler) and dissipative reality (Navier-Stokes).

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates nonlinear hydrodynamics, specifically rarefaction wave dynamics, when a strongly interacting Fermi gas expands into vacuum.

**Main result.** Self-similarity in the density profile is robustly observed across various interaction regimes, showing excellent agreement with ideal Euler solutions at unitarity but exhibiting deviations due to viscosity away from it.

**Method.** The authors combine theoretical modeling using 1D hydrodynamics (Euler and Navier-Stokes equations) with experimental measurements of density profiles via polarization rotation imaging.

**Model / system.** The system is an ultracold, degenerate Fermi gas (e.g., $^{6}	ext{Li}$) studied across the BEC-BCS crossover, expanding in a 'shock tube' geometry. The dynamics are governed by conservation laws for mass, momentum, and entropy.

**Key observables.** Normalized density profile ($	ilde{ho}$), scaled coordinates ($	ilde{\xi} = x/t$), sound diffusivity ($D$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Unitarity limit, BCS side interactions, viscosity ($\eta$), thermal conductivity ($\kappa$), and expansion time ($t$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The ideal case assumes isentropic conditions leading to Euler dynamics; deviations require including viscous terms from the full Navier-Stokes equations.

**Figures summary.** Figures show density profiles collapsing onto self-similar curves when plotted against scaled coordinates, and later figures quantify the deviation from this ideal profile due to viscosity.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the ideal, scale-invariant unitary limit (Euler solution) to testing deviations by incorporating finite viscosity and thermal effects across the BEC-BCS crossover using both theory and experiment.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the expansion of a homogeneous, strongly interacting Fermi gas released into vacuum in a ``shock tube'' geometry. At unitarity, where the gas is scale invariant and nearly inviscid, we find that the resulting rarefaction wave dynamics are self-similar and in excellent agreement with Riemann's solution of the Euler equation for all temperatures probed. Probing interactions away from unitarity within the BEC-BCS crossover, we observe increasing deviations from the Riemann solution as viscosity increases. However, even on the BCS side, where the sound diffusivity is increased twenty-fold, self-similarity is still approximately preserved. This may reflect how 1D Navier-Stokes rarefaction flows approach Euler self-similar solutions at long times. Our work demonstrates the utility of strongly interacting Fermi gases for the study of nonlinear hydrodynamics in a highly controllable setting.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07274"></a>
### [Topological Anderson insulators and reentrant topological transitions in a quasiperiodic long-range Su-Schrieffer-Heeger model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07274v1)

**Authors:** Fang-Ming Meng, Qi-Bo Zeng  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07274v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `analog quantum simulation` **4/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `Kondo & dissipative impurity` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

The blue and orange circles rep- resent the two sublattices of each unit cell, while the red lines denote the dominant hopping amplitudes. Panels (a)–(d) cor- respond to the phases with winding numbers W = 0, 1, −1, and 2, respectively. The number and sublattice character of the unpaired boundary sites determine the topological invari- ant and the corresponding zero-energy edge modes.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07274_figures/2606.07274_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 4. The black dashed line marks the cut at t1 = 0.55, while the white dashed line corresponds to λ = 2.3, which exhibits multiple reentrant transitions in the system. (b) Phase diagram for t2 = 0.7, t3 = 1.1, and t4 = 0.9. The black and white dashed lines indicate the cuts at t1 = 0.1 and λ = 1.5, respectively, along which additional reentrant topological transitions occur.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 4 presents two additional phase diagrams in the t1-λ plane for different parameter sets, revealing even richer disorder-induced topological phenomena. Along the black dashed line at t1 = 0.55 in Fig. 4(a), the wind- ing number evolves according to W = 2 →1 →2 → 1 →0 as the disorder strength increases. **Summary.** This paper theoretically explores the complex topology of a 1D SSH model subjected to quasiperiodic disorder. It demonstrates that this combination leads to Topological Anderson Insulators (TAIs) characterized by multiple winding numbers and exhibits rich reentrant phase transitions. The findings highlight how disorder can induce quantized, staircase-like changes in topological invariants.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study provides a concrete theoretical framework for understanding how long-range interactions combined with incommensurate potentials (quasiperiodicity) can stabilize exotic topological states that are robust even when spectral gaps close, which is highly relevant to engineered quantum materials and lattice simulations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates topological Anderson insulators (TAIs) and complex reentrant topological phase transitions within a quasiperiodic long-range Su-Schrieffer-Heeger model.

**Main result.** The interplay between long-range hopping and quasiperiodic disorder generates a rich landscape featuring TAI phases with multiple winding numbers, alongside observable staircase-like and reentrant topological transitions.

**Method.** Analysis relies on calculating the real-space winding number (a topological invariant) and monitoring the Lyapunov exponent ($\gamma_{LE}$) to characterize phase boundaries in both clean and disordered limits.

**Model / system.** The system is a one-dimensional long-range Su-Schrieffer-Heeger model incorporating third-nearest-neighbor hopping, subjected to quasiperiodic disorder applied to its hopping amplitudes. The Hamiltonian structure allows for the emergence of multiple topological invariants ($W=-1, 0, 1, 2$).

**Key observables.** Real-space winding number (W), Lyapunov exponent ($\gamma_{LE}$), and bulk energy gap ($\Delta E$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength ($\lambda$), quasiperiodic modulation parameter ($\alpha$), and various hopping amplitudes ($t_1, t_2, t_3, t_4$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** All hopping amplitudes are assumed to be real; the analysis uses the transfer-matrix formalism.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams showing regions for different winding numbers in the clean limit (Fig. 1), and demonstrate reentrant transitions, staircase changes, and spectral gap evolution as disorder strength ($\lambda$) is varied (Figs. 3, 4, 5, 6).

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically introduces the model and characterization methods, analyzes the clean limit topology, investigates TAI phases under quasiperiodic disorder, and concludes by summarizing the rich landscape of disorder-induced topological phenomena.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a one-dimensional long-range Su-Schrieffer-Heeger model with third-nearest-neighbor hopping and subject to quasiperiodic disorder. In the clean limit, the model hosts phases characterized by winding numbers $W=-1,0,1$ and $2$. The introduction of quasiperiodic disorder profoundly modifies the phase diagram and induces a series of topological phase transitions. Owing to the competition between topological dimerization and localization, topological Anderson insulating (TAI) phases with different winding numbers emerge and can persist even when the spectral gap becomes nearly closed in the strong-disorder regime. In addition, we uncover multiple reentrant topological phase transitions induced by varying either the quasiperiodic disorder strength or the hopping amplitudes. Remarkably, the system exhibits staircase-like topological Anderson transitions, where the real-space winding number evolves through successive quantized steps with increasing disorder strength. Our results demonstrate that the interplay between long-range hopping and quasiperiodic disorder generates a rich landscape of disorder-induced topological phases and reentrant topological transition phenomena.

</details>

### [Topologically Enforced Lifshitz Multicriticality in One Dimension](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07380v1)

**Summary.** The paper introduces a novel class of Lifshitz Multicritical Points driven purely by changes in the topology of neighboring quantum critical lines. Using entanglement spectroscopy and energy spectrum analysis, the authors prove that these topologically enforced points exhibit a breakdown of the Li–Haldane correspondence. This mismatch between bulk topological signatures and boundary modes offers a new theoretical handle for characterizing exotic quantum criticality.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful new diagnostic tool—the comparison between PBC entanglement spectra and OBC edge modes—to probe topological phases in systems where standard invariants fail, which is highly relevant for understanding boundary physics in condensed matter theory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To investigate novel Lifshitz Multicritical Points (MCPs) whose existence is dictated solely by changes in the topology of adjacent quantum critical lines, rather than traditional changes in critical exponents.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that at these topologically enforced MCPs, a breakdown of the Li–Haldane bulk-boundary correspondence occurs, evidenced by a mismatch between topological signatures found in the entanglement spectrum (PBC) and the absence of protected zero modes in the physical energy spectrum (OBC).

**Method.** The analysis employs advanced theoretical diagnostics including calculating the bulk entanglement spectrum using correlation matrices and comparing these results against single-particle OBC energy spectra derived from low-energy effective Hamiltonians.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on one-dimensional chiral-symmetric fermionic systems, modeled by generalized hopping structures ($\alpha$ chain models) that allow for tuning between different critical lines to induce the Lifshitz MCPs.

**Key observables.** Topological degeneracy (midgap entanglement levels), central charges ($c$), dynamical exponent ($z$), and the comparison count of midgap pairs vs. OBC zero modes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The hopping parameters ($\alpha, \alpha'$), the coupling range width ($R$), and the relative shift parameter ($s$) are crucial for determining the topological nature of the MCP.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is primarily conducted in the Renormalization Group (RG) limit, treating the system via low-energy effective theories derived from free-fermion models.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams showing the transition to topologically enforced MCPs; comparisons show PBC spectra with midgap levels but OBC spectra lacking corresponding zero modes at these novel points.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically constructs the Hamiltonian, analyzes the topological invariants (winding numbers) associated with adjacent critical lines, and then uses entanglement/energy spectrum diagnostics to prove the breakdown of the Li–Haldane correspondence specifically at the topologically enforced MCPs.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Recent advances have revealed that topology can further enrich the universality classes of quantum phase transitions, thereby extending beyond the traditional paradigms of statistical and condensed matter physics. However, multicriticality between topologically distinct quantum critical lines remains insufficiently explored. In this Letter, we systematically construct and investigate a novel class of topologically enforced Lifshitz multicritical points in one dimensional chiral symmetric fermionic systems. Such multicriticality is driven solely by changes in the topology of neighboring critical lines, beyond previously recognized multicritical points that are typically induced by changes in critical exponents. More importantly, the topologically enforced multicriticality identified here can host robust topological degeneracies while surprisingly exhibiting a breakdown of the Li Haldane bulk boundary correspondence-a phenomenon we elucidate through a simple physical picture.

</details>

### [Fate of the Ising universality class under nonreciprocal interactions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06981v1)

**Authors:** M. Akritidis, A. Garcés, A. Vasilopoulos, M. Carosi, D. Levis, N. G. Fytas  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06981v1>

**Summary.** The paper examines the stability of the 2D Ising critical point when subjected to nonreciprocal interactions that break detailed balance. Using a combination of Monte Carlo simulations and Renormalization Group theory, the authors demonstrate that while these NR terms cause measurable deviations in scaling ratios, they do not alter the fundamental asymptotic critical exponents. This highlights the remarkable robustness of equilibrium universality classes.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a rigorous theoretical framework for understanding how microscopic violations of time-reversal symmetry or detailed balance affect macroscopic phase transition universality classes, which is crucial for non-equilibrium statistical physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how the critical behavior of a two-dimensional Ising model is affected by nonreciprocal (NR) interactions that violate detailed balance.

**Main result.** The asymptotic critical exponents remain robustly consistent with the equilibrium 2D Ising universality class, indicating stability against these directional perturbations. However, dimensionless quantities show systematic deviations from their equilibrium values.

**Method.** The research employs extensive Monte Carlo simulations, finite-size scaling analysis, and Renormalization Group (RG) techniques to analyze critical exponents and scaling corrections.

**Model / system.** The model is a 2D Ising system on a square lattice subjected to nonreciprocal vision-cone interactions. The dynamics are governed by Glauber dynamics, leading to an effective Hamiltonian that lacks global reciprocity.

**Key observables.** Magnetization ($m$), Susceptibility ($\chi$), Binder cumulant ($U_4$), Correlation-length ratio ($\xi/L$), and the critical temperature shift $T_c(\lambda)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The nonreciprocal coupling strength $\lambda$ is varied across a broad range, while the reciprocal coupling $J$ sets the baseline equilibrium case.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Analysis relies on fitting data to specific scaling forms and RG calculations are performed at one-loop order using momentum-shell procedures.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate finite-size scaling analysis of dimensionless quantities like $U_4$ and $\xi/L$, showing their dependence on the NR coupling strength $\lambda$. Other figures show the behavior of energy-like observables ($e$) at criticality.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from analyzing the lattice model using MC simulations to performing detailed RG calculations in momentum space. The structure builds by first establishing the stability of critical exponents, then quantifying the deviations in scaling ratios, and finally deriving flow equations for the couplings.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the critical behavior of a two-dimensional Ising model with nonreciprocal vision-cone interactions, which explicitly violate reciprocity and detailed balance. Extensive Monte Carlo simulations and dynamic renormalization-group analysis show that the asymptotic critical exponents remain fully consistent with the equilibrium Ising universality class over a broad range of nonreciprocal coupling strengths $λ$. In contrast, dimensionless quantities such as the Binder cumulant and the correlation-length ratio display pronounced anisotropic nonequilibrium corrections and systematically deviate from their equilibrium Ising values. The renormalization-group flow further demonstrates that the nonreciprocal perturbation is irrelevant at the Wilson-Fisher fixed point while generating a finite shift of the critical temperature proportional to $λ^2$. Our results demonstrate the remarkable robustness of two-dimensional Ising criticality against this class of directional interactions.

</details>

### [Complex Temperature-dependent Thermal Conductivity in a Sawtooth Chain Magnet Fe$_\mathrm{2}$SiSe$_\mathrm{4}$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06858v1)

**Authors:** Kunya Yang, Feihao Pan, Liran Wang, Chenglin Shang, Ying Zhu, Xiancai Hu, Sanjiang He, Xinrun Mi, Long Zhang, Aifeng Wang, Yisheng Chai, Frederic Hardy, Christoph Meingast, Peng Cheng, Mingquan He

**Summary.** This work investigates the thermal conductivity of $	ext{Fe}_2	ext{SiSe}_4$, showing that its temperature dependence is strongly dictated by magnetic ordering. The material exhibits a characteristic double-peak structure in $\kappa(T)$, which arises from resonant phonon scattering when the magnet is partially ordered, and a highly enhanced peak below the lower transition temperature due to the suppression of this spin-phonon coupling.

**Why it may be interesting.** The direct measurement of how magnetic ordering modifies phonon transport provides a clear experimental signature of spin-lattice coupling, which can be modeled using concepts from open quantum systems or coupled Hamiltonian approaches in condensed matter theory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how spin fluctuations interact with lattice vibrations to influence thermal transport in a geometrically frustrated magnet.

**Main result.** Thermal conductivity exhibits a pronounced double-peak structure, demonstrating that resonant phonon scattering by magnetic excitations causes a broad maximum at higher temperatures, while the suppression of this coupling below the ordering temperature leads to a significantly enhanced low-temperature peak.

**Method.** The research relies on comprehensive measurements of thermal conductivity ($\kappa(T)$) alongside magnetization and specific heat capacity across various magnetic phases.

**Model / system.** The system is the triangular sawtooth-lattice olivine magnet $	ext{Fe}_2	ext{SiSe}_4$, which undergoes successive magnetic transitions at $T_1=110 	ext{ K}$ (antiferromagnetic) and $T_2=50 	ext{ K}$ (ferrimagnetic). The analysis models phonon heat transport using the Debye–Callaway model.

**Key observables.** Thermal conductivity $\kappa(T)$, magnetic transition temperatures ($T_1, T_2$), and the temperature dependence of magnetization $M(T)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The resonant scattering gap ($\Delta \approx 5.2 	ext{ meV}$), the enhancement factor of $\sim 5$ between the two peaks, and the transition temperatures ($110 	ext{ K}, 50 	ext{ K}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Phonons are assumed to dominate thermal conductivity, and the scattering mechanisms (conventional vs. resonant spin-phonon) can be separated and modeled independently.

**Figures summary.** Figures show magnetization curves revealing magnetic transitions at $T_1$ and $T_2$, and $\kappa(T)$ plots exhibiting a distinct double-peak structure corresponding to different magnetic regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper analyzes the thermal conductivity across three temperature regions defined by magnetic ordering: paramagnetic ($T>T_1$), single-$q$ ordered ($T_2 < T < T_1$), and double-$q$ ordered ($T<T_2$). Each region is analyzed using the Debye–Callaway model to quantify different scattering rates.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Geometrically frustrated magnets provide an ideal platform for exploring the interplay between lattice geometry and spin degrees of freedom. Here, we investigate the interactions between lattice and spin via thermal-transport measurements on the triangular sawtooth-lattice olivine magnet Fe$_\mathrm{2}$SiSe$_\mathrm{4}$, which exhibits successive magnetic transitions at $T_1 = 110$ K (antiferromagnetic) and $T_2 = 50$ K (ferrimagnetic). Although phonons dominate the thermal conductivity, its temperature dependence displays a pronounced double-peak structure arising from spin-phonon coupling. In the intermediate temperature range between $T_1$ and $T_2$ , resonant scattering of phonons by magnetic excitations around 5 meV produces a broad maximum around 60 K. Below $T_2$, the resonant spin-phonon scattering is strongly suppressed, leading to a rapid increase in thermal conductivity upon cooling and a pronounced low-temperature peak near 11 K, characteristic of heat transport governed by conventional phonon scattering mechanisms. Notably, this low-temperature peak is enhanced by a factor of $\sim 5$ compared to the broad maximum at higher temperatures. These results demonstrate the strong sensitivity of thermal transport to spin-lattice interactions and highlight spin-phonon scattering as an effective mechanism for tailoring thermal conductivity in geometrically frustrated magnets.

</details>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5. Comparison of SDE stability under optical and electrical biasing. (a) SDE vs bias current curves under optical (red dots)</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper presents an improved optical biasing technique for SNSPDs using a cryogenic photodiode as the local current source. By effectively shielding stray photons, they achieved ultra-low dark count rates while maintaining high detection efficiency and low jitter compared to electrical methods. This establishes PD-based optical biasing as a viable path toward building all-photonic quantum photonics systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses hardware requirements for quantum photonics by providing a scalable, low-noise method to bias superconducting detectors essential for quantum communication and computing applications.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving low-noise and low-jitter single-photon detection is critical for quantum information processing, as conventional electrical biasing introduces noise and thermal load.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated that using a cryogenic photodiode (PD) for optical biasing provides an alternative to electrical methods, achieving performance comparable in efficiency and jitter while maintaining ultra-low intrinsic dark count rates after proper shielding.

**Method.** They experimentally compared the performance metrics of Superconducting Nanowire Single-Photon Detectors (SNSPDs) biased optically versus electrically, focusing on noise suppression techniques.

**Model / system.** The core system is an SNSPD operating at cryogenic temperatures (~2.3 K). The novel element is using a local InGaAs-InP photodiode as a stable optical bias source to replace traditional electrical biasing circuits.

**Key observables.** System Detection Efficiency (80.7%), background dark count rate (32.6 cps, intrinsic DCR of $1 	imes 10^{-4}$ cps), and minimum timing jitter (57.5 ps).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cryogenic temperature ($\sim 2.3$ K); stability metrics like Coefficient of Variation (CV) for photocurrent fluctuations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary limitation identified is that residual optical power fluctuations remain a dominant noise source, necessitating ultra-stable light sources and improved coupling methods.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare the SNSPD performance ($	ext{SDE}$, $	ext{DCR}$, jitter) under optical versus electrical biasing across different operating points, and illustrate how stray photon leakage from connectors increases background counts.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a structure of identifying the noise limitations of existing bias methods, proposing the PD-based optical biasing scheme, demonstrating its stability and low dark count rate through shielding, and finally comparing its achieved performance metrics against conventional electrical biasing.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We experimentally demonstrate an improved optical biasing scheme for superconducting nanowire single-photon detectors (SNSPDs), which employs a cryogenic InGaAs-InP photodiode (PD) as a local bias source. It is found that, under illumination from a stable external light source, this PD generates a stable photocurrent in a cryogenic environment (~2.3 K), with fluctuations in the photocurrent primarily attributed to fluctuations in the incident optical power. Furthermore, by screening and effectively blocking stray photons leaking from the PD, which give rise to background dark counts, we have achieved an SNSPD exhibiting an ultra-low intrinsic dark count rate of 1e-4 cps. Utilizing this improved optical biasing technique, our SNSPD achieved performance comparable to that obtained under conventional electrical biasing: a system detection efficiency of 80.7%, a background dark count rate of 32.6 cps, and a minimum timing jitter of 57.5 ps. These results indicate that cryogenic-PD-based optical biasing serves as a viable, low-noise, and low-jitter alternative to traditional electrical biasing. Moreover, this work offers useful design guidance for the future development of PD-based low-noise bias sources and for the construction of all-photonic SNSPD systems tailored for high-precision quantum photonics applications.

</details>

### [Coherent versus stochastic error injection on a repetition-code logical qubit in superconducting hardware](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07377v1)

**Summary.** This work investigates whether superconducting qubits exhibit a theoretical 'coherent-stochastic gap' when subjected to different types of noise during Quantum Error Correction (QEC). By comparing simulations and experiments on repetition codes, the authors find that while theory predicts a clear difference, hardware imperfections—such as qubit frequency drifts—appear to convert coherent errors into stochastic ones, effectively closing the predicted gap.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides critical empirical constraints on the fundamental assumptions underlying QEC theory by showing that realistic hardware noise can mask theoretically predicted differences between noise types. This has direct implications for designing fault-tolerant quantum computation protocols.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to experimentally and theoretically determine if a measurable 'coherent-stochastic gap' exists in quantum error correction (QEC) when comparing the performance degradation caused by coherent versus stochastic noise.

**Main result.** Experimentally, no significant difference was observed between coherent and stochastic errors for $d=3$ or $d=5$ codes; this discrepancy is hypothesized to be due to baseline noise acting as a dephasing mechanism that effectively stochastifies the injected coherent errors.

**Method.** The authors combine experimental measurements on superconducting transmon processors with advanced simulations, including free-fermion and density-matrix methods, to model error channels.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a superconducting quantum processor implementing bitflip repetition codes ($d=3$ and $d=5$). The analysis models noise using both unitary (coherent) and probabilistic (stochastic) Pauli error channels.

**Key observables.** Logical fidelity ($\mathcal{L}$ or $P_L$), detection probability, and the coherent-stochastic gap ($\Delta_L$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code distance ($d=3, 5$), injected error probability ($p_{inj}$), number of QEC rounds ($R=10$), and qubit frequency drift ($\delta f$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The discrepancy between simulation and experiment is attributed to residual coherent phase errors (like frequency drifts) in the baseline noise that effectively turn coherent errors into stochastic ones.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare logical infidelity curves for $d=3$ and $d=5$ under varying noise types, showing simulations predicting a gap where experiments observe none. Other figures detail noise characterization circuits and simulation methodologies.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the theoretical expectation of a gap to characterizing baseline hardware noise using multiple dedicated circuits, then simulating both coherent and stochastic error injections on the repetition code, culminating in comparing these results against experimental measurements.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The performance of quantum error correction (QEC) codes is limited by the underlying physical noise. Theoretical studies show that coherent and stochastic noise have different effects when performing QEC with either surface or repetition codes. We use the bitflip repetition code, realized in a transmon quantum processor, as a testbed to experimentally study the impact of injecting coherent versus stochastic errors on the logical performance. We adapt a scalable free-fermion simulator to simulate the experiments and we modify a subset sampling technique to efficiently sample stochastic noise in the quantum circuit. In the experiment, we do not observe the difference in logical fidelity predicted by simulation for either the distance-3 or distance-5 repetition codes. We hypothesize that this discrepancy could be explained by small drifts in qubit frequencies, which introduce phase-coherent noise that `stochastifies' the injected coherent errors. Our work contributes to advancing an understanding of how coherent errors affect experimental QEC.

</details>

### [How Similar Can Fractional Chern Insulators Be to Fractional Quantum Hall States? Moiré-Enhanced Gaps and Excitation-Spectrum Correspondence](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07323v1)

**Summary.** This theoretical study develops a framework to understand how periodic density modulations in moiré lattices affect the excitation spectrum of Fractional Chern Insulators (FCIs). It shows that these modulations can significantly enhance the energy gap by rescaling the system's low-energy physics, making FCIs highly tunable and predictable relative to ideal Quantum Hall states.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides a powerful, quantitative design principle showing how engineered lattice modulations can robustly enhance topological gaps in correlated electron systems, offering direct guidance for materials science applications.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is to establish a theoretical principle linking periodic electron-density modulations (from moiré patterns) to the excitation spectrum of Fractional Chern Insulators (FCIs), comparing them to established Fractional Quantum Hall (FQH) states.

**Main result.** Density modulations are not detrimental; instead, large reciprocal lattice vector components can enhance the FCI gap arbitrarily, rescaling the entire low-energy spectrum to mimic the FQH problem structure.

**Method.** Theoretical derivation using Fourier analysis of density modulations ($	ilde{w}_G$) and projecting interacting Hamiltonians onto effective flat bands derived from continuum models or moiré lattices.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on FCIs realized in lattice bands, particularly those formed by moiré superlattices (e.g., twisted bilayer graphene, tMoTe2). The physics is modeled by comparing the system to ideal Landau Level (LL) physics.

**Key observables.** Charge-neutral gap size ($\Delta$), anyon dispersion spectrum, and the gap enhancement factor ($M_2$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Screening length ($d_s$), moiré periodicity/modulation strength ($	ilde{w}_G$), filling fraction ($
u=1/3$ or $2/3$), and LL hybridization weight ($t$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on approximations such as the first harmonic approximation for the potential, neglecting band dispersion in some theoretical limits, or assuming specific symmetries (e.g., near-vortexable bands).

**Figures summary.** Figures compare FCI spectra to LL spectra, showing spectral collapse upon rescaling by $M_2$. Specific examples include gap enhancement plots vs. modulation strength and comparisons between $
u=1/3$ and $
u=2/3$ states in tMoTe$_2$.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops a theoretical principle relating density modulations to spectral properties, applies this to moiré systems (like graphene), analyzes the role of different Fourier components ($G$), and concludes by testing the robustness of gap enhancement using material examples like tMoTe$_2$ while noting discrepancies arising from band dispersion.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fractional Chern insulators (FCIs) realize fractional quantum Hall topology in lattice bands, but their excitation spectra remain far less understood than their ground states. Here we establish a theoretical principle relating the periodic electron-density modulations of flat Chern bands to the many-body gap and excitation spectrum of FCIs. Contrary to the conventional view that such density modulations are detrimental to fractional topology, we show that different reciprocal-lattice Fourier components play sharply distinct roles: components at smaller reciprocal lattice vectors suppress the FCI gap, whereas components at larger reciprocal lattice vectors enhance it. By suppressing the harmful small-wave-vector components and amplifying the beneficial large-wave-vector components, the gap enhancement can, in principle, be made arbitrarily large within the projected flat-band theory. Moreover, the same enhancement factor rescales the full low-energy spectrum, making the FCI excitation spectrum predictable from the corresponding Landau-level problem. We further generalize this correspondence to non-Abelian states. Applying this principle to moiré Chern bands, we identify these reciprocal-lattice density components as practical diagnostics for robust FCIs.

</details>

### [Suppression of Quasiparticle Poisoning to $10^{-11}$ Levels in Superconducting Qubits via Infrared Shielding](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07339v1)

**Authors:** Wei-En Lin, Chen-Hsun Ma, Erh-Hsiang Yeh, Wei-Lun Peng, Yu-Sen Wei, Hsi-Sheng Goan, Cen-Shawn Wu, Chung-Ting Ke, Yung-Fu Chen, Chii-Dong Chen

**Summary.** The authors investigated quasiparticle poisoning in superconducting qubits using various infrared (IR) shielding methods. By measuring parity switching rates over extended periods, they demonstrated that improved shielding can suppress decoherence sources by many orders of magnitude. This work establishes a new low benchmark for quasiparticle density, proving the necessity of meticulous environmental control for scalable quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** This research provides crucial experimental benchmarks on environmental noise mitigation in superconducting qubits, directly impacting the feasibility of scaling quantum processors.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Quasiparticle poisoning severely limits coherence times and scalability in superconducting qubits.

**Main result.** Implementing advanced infrared (IR) shielding techniques suppressed the quasiparticle switching rate by over four orders of magnitude, achieving a record low density estimate of $1.88 	imes 10^{-11}$ per Cooper pair.

**Method.** The study measured quasiparticle-induced parity switching rates ($\Gamma_p$) over long cooldown periods and characterized qubit temperature ($T_q$).

**Model / system.** Floating transmon superconducting qubits are used, which are susceptible to decoherence from environmental IR radiation and stray quasiparticles.

**Key observables.** Parity-switching rate ($\Gamma_p$), effective qubit temperature ($T_q$), quasiparticle density per Cooper pair ($x_{qp}$), and energy relaxation time ($T_1$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Qubit frequency $\approx 3$ GHz; achieved $x_{qp} \approx 1.88 	imes 10^{-11}$; effective temperature down to $17	ext{ mK}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The remaining quasiparticle population is likely dominated by sporadic phonon bursts from mechanical stress or the environment.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the experimental setup, time evolution of $\Gamma_p$ showing decay over days, and plots comparing $T_q$ to the phonon bath temperature ($T_{MX}$).

**Paper structure.** The work systematically compares three IR shielding configurations (A, B, C) by measuring parity switching rates ($\Gamma_p$) and characterizing thermalization effects ($T_q$) over time to determine the optimal environment for high-coherence operation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quasiparticle poisoning bottlenecks superconducting qubits, limiting coherence and the scalability of quantum processors. In this work, we systematically investigate quasiparticle poisoning in superconducting qubits under three infrared (IR) shielding configurations, ranging from a dedicated multi-layer design to a simplified implementation. By measuring quasiparticle-induced parity switching, we demonstrate a suppression of the switching rate by over four orders of magnitude via the implementation of improved shielding. In the best configuration, the rate decreases over time following cooldown and reaches 0.069$\,$Hz on day 34, corresponding to an anticipated quasiparticle density per Cooper pair of $1.88\times10^{-11}$. To our knowledge, this represents the lowest quasiparticle density reported in the literature to date. The remaining quasiparticle population is likely dominated by sporadic phonon bursts stemming from mechanical stress release in the on-chip films, as well as from the surrounding environment. The effective qubit temperature follows the phonon bath down to 17$\,$mK, enabling initialization errors of $\sim 0.01\%$ for 3$\,$GHz qubits. These results demonstrate that proper IR shielding and thermalization are essential for suppressing quasiparticle poisoning and enabling high-coherence, scalable superconducting qubit systems.

</details>

### [Non-Abelian braiding in Abelian Fractional Quantum Hall Phases from realistic interactions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07517v1)

**Summary.** The research demonstrates that non-Abelian braiding statistics can be engineered in the seemingly simple Laughlin FQH state at $
u=1/3$. By applying localized trapping potentials, the system forces the fractionalized quasiholes to behave as non-Abelian anyons, providing a pathway to realizing topological quantum computation using realistic electron interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses how topological order, typically associated with exotic Hamiltonians like the Moore-Read model, can emerge robustly within a simpler, Abelian phase ($
u=1/3$) by engineering local potentials. This has direct implications for designing experimental quantum simulators.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper proposes a method to realize non-Abelian braiding of fractionalized quasiholes within the Abelian Laughlin Fractional Quantum Hall (FQH) phase.

**Main result.** Non-Abelian statistics can be realized in $
u=1/3$ by inducing fractionalization of Laughlin quasiholes using carefully designed one-body trapping potentials, without requiring fine-tuning of realistic electron-electron interactions.

**Method.** The study employs numerical simulations (Exact Diagonalization and variational energy calculations) on spherical geometry to analyze the low-lying excitations relative to both two-body pseudopotentials ($\hat{V}_{2bdy}$) and three-body terms ($\hat{V}_{3bdy}$).

**Model / system.** The system is the Fractional Quantum Hall state at $
u=1/3$, modeled using concepts derived from the Laughlin and Moore-Read Hamiltonians. The analysis focuses on quasihole excitations, which are shown to fractionalize into non-Abelian anyons ($\sigma$-type) when subjected to external trapping potentials.

**Key observables.** Low-lying excitation energies, ground state degeneracy (comparing Abelian vs. non-Abelian expectations), and the ratio of energy gaps used to diagnose topological order.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Filling factor $
u=1/3$, pinning strength ($\lambda$), and the nature of the trapping potential (Gaussian vs. orbital).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on mapping complex interactions onto effective Hamiltonians in the Lowest Landau Level (LLL) basis, sometimes using idealized 'toy model' potentials to simulate realistic effects.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the energy spectra evolution as pinning strength increases, showing phase transitions marked by gap closing and changes in ground state degeneracy, confirming fractionalization.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the theoretical framework (Laughlin vs. MR models) to applying numerical techniques using external potentials ($\hat{V}_{1bdy}$) to induce quasihole fractionalization, culminating in evidence for non-Abelian braiding signatures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a method of realizing non-Abelian braiding of fractionalized quasiholes in the Laughlin fractional quantum Hall phase at $ν=1/3$ with realistic two-body interactions within the lowest Landau level. It is numerically shown that low-lying gapped excitations near $ν=1/3$ are contained almost entirely within the null space of the three-body Moore-Read model Hamiltonian. They are thus quantum fluids of non-Abelian quasiholes that are in principle physically accessible. In particular, Laughlin ground state can be described as a fluid of ``$ψ$-type" quasiholes formed by binding a magnetic flux with a Majorana fermion (MF), and the Laughlin quasiholes are described by the ``$1$-type'' quasiholes, which are magnetic fluxes without a MF attached. Within the Laughlin phase, Laughlin quasiholes can be locally fractionalized into non-Abelian quasiholes, when the strong attraction between them is overcome by properly designed one-body electronstatic trapping potentials. Extensive numerics with proper finite-size scaling corroborate this physical picture, and our study points to the possibility of realizing non-Abelian braiding within an Abelian topological phase in experiment without the need for fine-tuning realistic electron-electron interaction.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.07472"></a>
### [Driving Exchange Interaction in Spin Qubits with Quasi-Zero Pulses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07472v1)

**Authors:** Julian D. Teske, Remy L. Delva, Shobhan Kulshreshtha, Yuval Baum, Florian Luthi, Fahd A. Mohiyaddin, Rostyslav Savytskyy, Thomas Watson, Pranav S. Mundada  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07472v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07472_figures/2606.07472_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Illustration of an atomic barrier pulse as a function of time. A rectangular exchange pulse would correspond to an amplitude ratio of γ = 0, while a net-zero pulse would correspond to γ = tpos/tneg. Quasi-zero pulses correspond to other values of 0 &lt; γ &lt; tpos/tneg. In the following, we assume tpos = tneg. Inset: picture of the Tunnel Falls device adopted from Ref. [3].</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07472_figures/2606.07472_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Pulse perturbation of net-zero and reference pulses. (a) Experiment description: The qubit is initialized, Np rep- etitions of N(ψ) pulses are applied and the qubit is read out. Each N(ψ) pulse is tuned to rotate the qubit an angle ψ around the n-axis. (b, c) Measured fringe pattern of the ref- erence pulse and the net-zero pulse. We plot the excitation probability as a function of the rotation angle ψ and number of pulse repetitions Np which are incremented in multiples of 2. The teal circles mark the center of the fringe pattern for different repetition counts and black dashed lines are included as guide to the eye to spot the curvature of the fringes.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07472_figures/2606.07472_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Comparison of fringe curvature induced by reference, net-zero and quasi-zero pulses. The plot displays the simulated ground-state fidelity F|0⟩following the multi-pulse sequence outlined in Fig. 2 (a). The teal dots are the peaks along the central fringe and the black dashed line marks the calibrated amplitude to implement the N(π/2) gate. We observe that the net-zero pulse bends the curvature of the central fringe and over-corrects the curvature on the considered time-scale. The optimal amplitude relation γ of a quasi-zero pulse is found by maximizing the alignment of the teal dots along the central fringe to a straight line.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper tackles the challenge of controlling spin qubit exchange interactions accurately despite imperfections in control pulses. The authors introduce and validate 'quasi-zero' pulse designs as an efficient alternative to complex filtering methods. This approach significantly reduces the number of tunable parameters needed for high-fidelity gate operations, making quantum device calibration faster and more scalable.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses practical limitations in solid-state quantum computing hardware by providing a robust, low-complexity pulse shaping technique that simplifies the calibration overhead required for large-scale devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is achieving high-fidelity quantum gates for spin qubits by accurately controlling exchange interactions while mitigating systematic errors introduced by pulse distortions.

**Main result.** Quasi-zero pulses provide a low-complexity method to achieve gate fidelities comparable to highly optimized, multi-parameter filtering techniques, requiring fewer tuning parameters.

**Method.** The authors designed and benchmarked quasi-zero pulses—a generalization of net-zero pulses—to cancel out linear-dynamical pulse distortions during the evolution of exchange-only qubits.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on semiconductor spin qubits confined in quantum dots, specifically tested on Intel's Tunnel Falls six-dot device. The system dynamics are governed by an effective Hamiltonian involving tunable exchange interactions ($J_{i}(v_i)$).

**Key observables.** Gate fidelity (measured via randomized benchmarking), pulse duration, and the curvature of Rabi fringes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The amplitude ratio ($\gamma$) for quasi-zero pulses is critical; optimal performance was found by balancing noise sources like hyperfine and charge noise.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that control signal distortions are linear-dynamical. Furthermore, the negative pulse segments are assumed to have a negligible effect on the exchange interaction due to its exponential dependence on voltage.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the structure of atomic barrier pulses and compare fringe patterns, showing systematic shifts in reference pulses versus vanishing shifts with net-zero/quasi-zero pulses. Table I summarizes fidelity metrics for various pulse settings.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first identifying the need to correct pulse distortions; it then introduces and analyzes net-zero and quasi-zero pulse designs, systematically benchmarking these against reference methods using randomized benchmarking to quantify performance gains in terms of both fidelity and parameter count.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The implementation of high-fidelity quantum gates for spin qubits requires accurate control of exchange interactions between electrons confined in quantum dots, but pulse distortions can limit this control accuracy. Although linear-dynamical distortions can be compensated for by appropriately convolving the control signal, determining the necessary convolution requires detailed knowledge of the distortion's transfer function, and therefore the calibration of numerous parameters. Alternatively, control pulses can be designed to have a net-zero time integral canceling out linear-dynamical pulse distortions. We generalize net-zero pulse designs to quasi-zero pulses allowing net-positive but reduced time integrals. Using these pulse designs, we systematically develop complete gate sets for exchange-only qubits, and study the resulting tradeoffs between pulse duration, fidelity, and the required number of tunable parameters, both in simulation and experiment. We benchmark the optimized gate pulses on Intel's Tunnel Falls six-dot device and show they achieve fidelities similar to those obtained with a full filtering approach, with identical pulse durations and fewer tuning parameters. This reduction in complexity opens the door to fast and easily automated calibration schemes compatible with large-scale commercial quantum devices.

</details>

### [Measurement circuit ansatz: Naimark versus quantum neural-network measurements](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07376v1)

**Summary.** The paper develops methods to build quantum circuits that perform general measurements (POVMs) by comparing the traditional Naimark extension against modern Quantum Neural Network (QNN) ansatzes. It demonstrates that while Naimark circuits are theoretically sound, QNN-based measurements offer superior optimization efficiency and hardware compatibility for state discrimination tasks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses resource estimation and hardware feasibility for quantum measurement protocols, which is crucial for any practical application of quantum computation or sensing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is constructing quantum circuits (ansatze) capable of implementing general Positive-Operator-Valued Measures (POVMs) for tasks like state discrimination.

**Main result.** Quantum Neural Network (QNN) measurements are shown to be more efficient and effective than traditional Naimark circuit constructions, especially on near-term hardware, although they may not reach the theoretical optimum.

**Method.** The work compares three measurement approaches: standard Naimark extensions using universal gates, hybrid Naimark-QNN circuits, and fully QNN measurements. Optimization is performed classically to determine parameters for state discrimination tasks (ME/MC).

**Model / system.** The model involves quantum systems requiring the realization of $l$-outcome POVMs via ancillary qubits. The comparison focuses on circuit complexity, specifically counting CNOT gates and assessing optimization convergence.

**Key observables.** Guessing probability ($p_{	ext{guess}}$) for ME discrimination, Maximum Confidence (MC) values, and the required number of CNOT gates to implement the measurement circuit.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of desired outcomes ($l$), the minimum required ancillary qubits ($\lceil \log_2 l ceil$), and the optimization cutoff parameter ($\delta_c$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that current quantum hardware favors shallow-depth circuits, which biases the comparison toward QNN approaches over deep Naimark structures.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the construction of POVMs using ancillary qubits and binary modules; comparisons show convergence plots for different measurement types on simulated/real hardware; and circuit diagrams detail block-encoding and unitary transformations.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically develops from the Naimark extension formalism to constructing $l$-outcome POVM circuits. It then introduces QNN replacements (hybrid and full) to mitigate optimization difficulties inherent in the original structure, culminating in performance comparisons for state discrimination tasks.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this work, we present constructions of quantum circuits to implement general measurements on quantum hardware. Firstly, we investigate a quantum circuit ansatz by following the Naimark extension with a universal set of gates, such as controlled-NOT and single-qubit gates; we call it a Naimark quantum measurement. We present a circuit ansatz framed by the Naimark extension, leaving single-qubit gates with parameters, and apply a classical optimizer to determine their parameters to approximate a desired quantum measurement. Secondly, we relax the Naimark measurement with quantum neural-network (QNN) circuits, employing parameterized quantum circuits. We present hybrid Naimark-QNN measurements by incorporating QNN circuits into Naimark measurements. Thirdly, we also consider fully QNN measurements with shallow parameterized circuits. Then, we compare the constructed measurement circuits, Naimark, hybrid Naimark-QNN, and fully QNN measurements, for strategies of state discrimination, such as minimum-error and maximum-confidence measurements. We demonstrate that QNN circuits can efficiently and effectively achieve near-optimal quantum measurements with fewer training iterations.

</details>

### [Contacting Josephson Junctions via Airbridges in Superconducting Circuits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06951v1)

**Authors:** Prakiran Baidya, Momčilo Milosavljević, Murali Krishna Kurmapu, Thomas Fösel, Harshanth Ram Murugesan, Victor Kemme, Mojahed Jaber, Markus Sondermann, Christopher Eichler

**Summary.** This paper introduces a novel, streamlined fabrication technique for connecting superconducting circuit elements using only airbridges. By eliminating complex 'bandage' contacts, the method maintains high device coherence and low loss while drastically simplifying manufacturing steps for transmon qubits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses hardware limitations in superconducting quantum processors, which is central to realizing scalable quantum computation architectures.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is developing reliable, low-loss electrical interconnects for superconducting quantum circuits that avoid the complexity and damage associated with conventional 'bandage' contacts.

**Main result.** The authors successfully demonstrated a single-step airbridge fabrication process that maintains high coherence in transmon qubits (up to 290 $\mu$s) while significantly simplifying device manufacturing.

**Method.** They fabricated interconnects using varying-sized airbridges via grayscale electron-beam lithography and characterized performance by measuring qubit relaxation times ($T_1$) and resonator quality factors ($Q$).

**Model / system.** The system involves superconducting circuits, specifically transmon qubits coupled to Coplanar Waveguide (CPW) resonators. The interconnects are the airbridges themselves, which act as tunable elements affecting circuit parameters.

**Key observables.** Energy relaxation time ($T_1$), internal quality factor ($Q_i$), frequency shift ($\Delta f$), and room-temperature conductance ($G$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Airbridge dimensions (widths $0.5 \mu	ext{m}$ to $4 \mu	ext{m}$, lengths $5 \mu	ext{m}$ to $40 \mu	ext{m}$), and the comparison between intrinsic loss and airbridge-induced loss.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The process assumes that using only airbridges eliminates parasitic interfaces, though solvent choice (e.g., acetone vs NMP) is critical for successful lift-off.

**Figures summary.** Figures show SEM images of fabricated airbridges across various dimensions and structural profiles (linear vs parabolic). Other figures detail $T_1$ decay curves, resonator $S_{21}$ spectra showing frequency shifts, and comparisons of quality factors based on the number of airbridges.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from identifying the fabrication bottleneck (bandages) to proposing a single-airbridge solution. It then characterizes this structure electrically by measuring qubit coherence ($T_1$) and resonator response ($Q$, $\Delta f$), concluding with process optimization details.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconducting circuit devices require electrical interconnects between different circuit elements on the chip, for which conventional device architectures use a combination of two structural elements: \textit{airbridges} to connect non-adjacent elements in the base layer, and \textit{bandages} to connect the electrodes forming the Josephson junctions to the base layer. Bandages introduce unwanted parasitic material interfaces and increase the manufacturing complexity. Here, we overcome the limitations imposed by \emph{bandages} by establishing \textit{all} electrical interconnects with airbridges of varying size fabricated in a single step. The airbridges show a high yield and mechanical stability over a wide range of sizes from $0.5\,μ\mathrm{m}$ to $4\,μ\mathrm{m}$ in width and from $5\,μ\mathrm{m}$ to $40\,μ\mathrm{m}$ in length, and show low loss when integrated in coplanar waveguide resonators and transmon qubits. Measured relaxation times up to more than $250\,μ\mathrm{s}$ in standard transmon geometries show that the process achieves high coherence while substantially easing and accelerating device fabrication.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.06582"></a>
### [Fun with Graph States: Nonlocal Bell Pairs and the Arf Invariant](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06582v1)

**Authors:** Bartlomiej Czech, Yichen Feng, Xianlai Wu, Minjun Xie  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06582v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

<img src="2606.06582_figures/2606.06582_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 4: The complete graph K4 as a union of graph G (red) and π(G) (blue), where π = (1 2 3 4) permutes the vertices of G. We have drawn the figure such that π can be visualized as an anti-clockwise rotation by 90◦.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06582_figures/2606.06582_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 5: A k-replica multi-invariant of a q-partite quantum state |G′⟩A1A2...Aq involves a choice of q permutations πi, each acting on the k replicas of party Ai.</figcaption>

<img src="2606.06582_figures/2606.06582_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 6: Stabilizers O⃗x, which contribute to Z(3) n in summation (5.17) are in one-to-one correspondence with magic squares—solutions of equations (B.6-B.7).</figcaption>
</figure>

<img src="2606.06582_figures/2606.06582_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 7: Stabilizers O⃗x, which contribute to Z2n in summation (5.17) are in one-to-one correspondence with “magic annuli”—solutions of equations (B.13-B.15).</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work rigorously analyzes graph states by connecting their inner products and amplitudes to fundamental invariants from algebraic topology, specifically the Arf invariant. It establishes a nonlocal tensor factorization basis for these states, suggesting that quantum advantage in MBQC can be understood through topological structures on auxiliary manifolds.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful mathematical framework linking quantum resource states (graph states) directly to deep concepts in algebraic topology (Arf invariant, intersection forms), which is highly relevant for understanding the fundamental limits of computation.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the inner product $\langle G'|G''angle$ between graph states, relating its magnitude to algebraic invariants like the rank of the adjacency matrix over $\mathbb{F}_2$, and its phase to the Arf invariant.

**Main result.** Graph states can be factorized into a nonlocal tensor basis composed of Bell pairs and unentangled ancillae, providing deep connections between quantum information theory and algebraic topology.

**Method.** The analysis employs techniques from linear algebra over $\mathbb{F}_2$, utilizing the structure of stabilizer codes defined by graph adjacency matrices to compute partial amplitudes and expectation values.

**Model / system.** The system is based on graph states $|Gangle$ defined on $M$ qubits, which are a specific class of stabilizer states generated by CZ gates determined by the graph's adjacency matrix $A$.

**Key observables.** Inner product $\langle G'|G''angle$, magnitude $|\langle+|^{\otimes M} |Gangle|$, phase factor related to the Arf invariant ($\Delta$), and multi-invariants like Rényi entropy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The rank of the adjacency matrix $A$ over $\mathbb{F}_2$, and the Arf invariant $\Delta$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Calculations are performed rigorously within the framework of Pauli algebra on qubits, often assuming non-vanishing amplitudes to define topological invariants.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the factorization of graph states into effective Bell pairs and unentangled ancillae; other figures show structures related to counting solutions for multi-invariants (e.g., magic squares/annuli).

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from defining the inner product $\langle G'|G''angle$ using linear algebra over $\mathbb{F}_2$, establishing that magnitude depends on rank and phase on the Arf invariant. It then develops a nonlocal tensor factorization, applies this to compute multi-invariants (like Rényi entropy), and finally maps these results onto topological invariants of auxiliary manifolds.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study inner products and partial amplitudes of graph states--a commonly employed class of quantum states, which are specified by graphs. We find that the magnitudes of these quantities are simply related to the rank of the adjacency matrix of the graph over F_2 while the phase is determined by the Arf invariant of its quadratic refinement. These facts motivate a nonlocal tensor factorization of the Hilbert space, with respect to which all graph states are products of Bell pairs with unentangled ancillae. These results may illuminate the quantum advantage in the framework of Measurement-Based Quantum Computation and suggest that graph states can be usefully visualized in the language of algebraic topology. In addition, we develop a specialized technique for computing expectation values of qubit-wise permutations in graph states, which is useful for calculating multi-invariants.

</details>

### [Nonreciprocal optomechanical entanglement in an asymmetric Fabry-Perot cavity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06988v1)
