**Summary.** This paper theoretically investigates nonreciprocal quantum entanglement in asymmetric Fabry-Pérot cavities. By solving the system's dynamics using Quantum Langevin Equations, the authors demonstrate that asymmetry enhances entanglement compared to symmetric setups. The key finding is the realization of simultaneous classical and quantum nonreciprocities without requiring a positive correlation between their respective degrees.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it pushes beyond simple demonstrations of directional light control by quantifying the *quantum* nature of this directionality (nonreciprocal entanglement) in a well-established platform like optomechanics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to generate and characterize nonreciprocal quantum entanglement within an asymmetric Fabry-Pérot (F-P) cavity, specifically investigating the relationship between classical and quantum nonreciprocities.

**Main result.** Asymmetric F-P cavities are shown to enhance both the magnitude and robustness of optomechanical entanglement compared to symmetric counterparts. Crucially, the research demonstrates that classical and quantum nonreciprocities can coexist without exhibiting a positive correlation.

**Method.** The analysis employs solving Quantum Langevin Equations (QLEs) to determine system dynamics, followed by calculating steady-state covariance matrices via the Lyapunov equation for noise propagation.

**Model / system.** The physical platform is an optomechanical system realized in an asymmetric F-P cavity. The model uses a Hamiltonian describing light-mechanical coupling and evolves operators using QLEs under approximations like strong driving conditions.

**Key observables.** Logarithmic negativity (for entanglement), classical nonreciprocity degree ($\eta_c$), quantum nonreciprocity degree ($\eta_q$), and transmission spectra ($T_f$ vs $T_b$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Asymmetry parameters like reflectivity ($r_L$) and the mechanical spring constant ($K$), as well as driving power ($P$) and temperature ($T$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis utilizes approximations such as the strong-driving condition ($\vert\Omega\vert \gg \kappa_{tot}$) to separate mean values from quantum fluctuations.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate nonreciprocal transmission spectra dependence on cavity parameters, and show entanglement ($E_{N,\varsigma}$) vs. detuning/power/temperature, comparing asymmetric versus symmetric cavity performance.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the theoretical framework using QLEs to model the system. It then analyzes nonreciprocal transmission spectra, proceeds to quantify quantum entanglement via logarithmic negativity, and finally compares classical and quantum nonreciprocities across various parameter regimes (e.g., varying reflectivity or temperature).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonreciprocal transmission (classical nonreciprocity) in optomechanical systems based on asymmetric Fabry-Perot (F-P) cavities has been theoretically proposed and experimentally demonstrated. However, nonreciprocal quantum effects, particularly nonreciprocal quantum entanglement, remain unexplored in such systems. Here, we propose to generate nonreciprocal optomechanical entanglement in an asymmetric F-P cavity and discuss the connection between the nonreciprocal transmission and nonreciprocal quantum entanglement. We reproduce the nonreciprocal transmission spectra by solving the quantum Langevin equations, and then discuss the optimal parameters to achieve nonreciprocal optomechanical entanglement in the system. We show that a greater and more robust optomechanical entanglement can be approached in the asymmetric F-P cavities, in comparing with the symmetric cavities. Furthermore, we find that the degrees of classical and quantum nonreciprocities do not exhibit positive correlation as expected. Our work shows that the classical and quantum nonreciprocities can be realized simultaneously in the asymmetric F-P cavities, which provide a platform to explore the connection between classical and quantum nonreciprocities.

</details>

**Summary.** This theoretical study investigates the quantum critical properties of non-Hermitian XY spin chains. It reveals that defining these properties is highly ambiguous, as results depend critically on whether one uses standard or biorthogonal quantum mechanics and which specific state (e.g., minimal energy) is assumed. The paper provides detailed analysis using correlation functions and phase diagrams to map out the complex parameter space of non-Hermitian magnetism.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work directly addresses the complex, modern frontier of open quantum systems by showing how fundamental concepts like 'ground state' and 'criticality' break down or become ambiguous when moving from Hermitian to non-Hermitian Hamiltonians.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary challenge is characterizing the quantum critical properties of genuine non-Hermitian many-body systems because both the definition of the state and expectation values are ambiguous.

**Main result.** The critical properties (energy, magnetization, correlations) depend strongly on which formalism (standard vs. biorthogonal QM) and which specific state (minimal energy vs. steady state) is chosen for calculation.

**Method.** The study employs exact solutions, analyzing correlation functions using techniques like the Fisher–Hartwig theorem applied to Toeplitz determinants derived from quasi-particle excitations.

**Model / system.** The system under investigation is a 1D XY spin chain with an added magnetic field ($\lambda$), which is treated as non-Hermitian. The dynamics are analyzed using both standard quantum mechanics and biorthogonal quantum mechanics formalisms.

**Key observables.** Energy density (eVac), magnetization ($M$), and two-point spin correlation functions ($C_\alpha^r$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Exchange amplitude $J$, anisotropy $\gamma$, and magnetic field $\lambda$. Critical regimes are defined by the relationship between these parameters.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The authors must choose between different definitions of 'ground state' (e.g., minimal energy vs. steady state), which leads to differing physical predictions for critical behavior.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate phase diagrams showing transitions between FM, LL, and PM phases; they also plot the $r$-dependence of correlation functions on log-log scales to extract power-law decay exponents.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically compares results derived from different theoretical frameworks (standard vs. biorthogonal QM) and different physical states (minimal energy vs. steady state) to map out the phase diagram and critical scaling laws for the non-Hermitian XY model.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The characterization of the quantum critical properties of genuine non-Hermitian many-body systems remains ambiguous as neither the state considered nor the definition of expectation values is unique. In this work, we investigate the quantum critical properties of two models of non-Hermitian XY spin chains with magnetic field. Using exact solutions, we systematically investigate the parameter dependence of the energy, the magnetization as well as the long-distance asymptotic behavior of static correlation functions. We compute expectation values within the standard formalism of quantum mechanics as well as within biorthogonal quantum mechanics and take two different states which one might reasonably consider to be the analog of the ground state of a Hermitian model. The critical properties, including such fundamental characteristics as the phase diagram, depend on both the formalism used as well as the state considered. We provide arguments in favor of the use of standard quantum mechanics. Which state to be taken in computations, depends on the (hypothetical) experimental preparation of the system.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06716"></a>
### [Persistent currents in signed directed networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06716v1)

**Authors:** Davide Cipollini, Guido Caldarelli  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06716v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06716_figures/2606.06716_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. Persistent currents in looped networks. (a) Current as a function of q for a single-cycle network (inset), showing periodic behavior. (b) Total current for a network with two independent cycles. (c,d) Corresponding spectra of the signed magnetic Laplacian. (e,f) Decomposition of the current onto the two cycle modes at different τ, highlighting interference and multiscale contributions. Weights are color coded in blue (negative) and red (positive). Transparency codifies for am- plitude.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06716_figures/2606.06716_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. Hofstadter network. (a) Energy spectrum computed on a network with N = 400 nodes as a function of q, show- ing the butterfly-like fractal structure. (b) Representative signed directed network with spatially structured asymmetric weights, shown for a smaller system with N = 25 nodes for vi- sualization purposes. (c) Global persistent current computed on the full N = 400 network. (d) Cycle-resolved currents computed on the same N = 400 network.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work develops a generalized theory for persistent currents on arbitrary signed directed networks by treating the system thermodynamically. It maps quantum coherence onto fluxes defined over the network's cycle space, providing a topological signature of phase coherence. The framework is powerful enough to reproduce complex spectral features like the Hofstadter butterfly.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful topological tool (cycle space analysis) to characterize quantum coherence signatures in complex graph structures, linking spectral properties (Laplacian eigenvalues) directly to transport phenomena.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To generalize the concept of persistent currents from simple rings/lattices to arbitrary signed directed networks using a thermodynamic framework.

**Main result.** Persistent currents are shown to be divergence-free equilibrium currents that decompose onto the cycle basis, providing a topological signature of quantum phase coherence in complex networks.

**Method.** The analysis uses a Gibbs ensemble approach, defining persistent currents as thermodynamic responses (derivatives of the free energy) to gauge-invariant fluxes defined on the network's cycle space.

**Model / system.** The system is modeled by signed directed networks, where edge phases act as a discrete gauge field. The dynamics are governed by an effective Hamiltonian derived from the signed magnetic Laplacian, which is treated in a canonical ensemble.

**Key observables.** Global persistent current $J(q)$, cycle fluxes $\Psi$, and the response kernel $K(	au)$ relating currents to fluxes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inverse temperature/diffusion time ($	au$), flux parameters ($q$), and the network topology (cycle space geometry).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is performed in an equilibrium setting, using a free energy expansion around zero flux. The method successfully reproduces known results for rings and lattices.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate persistent currents in looped networks across different cycle numbers, showing the corresponding spectra of the signed magnetic Laplacian and the decomposition onto independent cycle modes.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops a thermodynamic framework by introducing gauge-invariant fluxes on the cycle space. It derives linear relations between current responses and these fluxes using the free energy expansion, culminating in an application to reproduce the Hofstadter butterfly spectrum.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Network theory can be fruitfully used to describe quantum coherence in physical systems. To that purpose we introduce persistent currents in signed directed networks by interpreting the signed magnetic Laplacian as an effective Hamiltonian and the associated edge phases as a discrete gauge field. In a canonical ensemble, persistent currents arise as thermodynamic responses to variations of gauge-invariant fluxes. We show that these fluxes are naturally defined on the cycle space of the network, and that the resulting currents are constrained to the divergence-free subspace and decompose onto independent cycles. This formulation provides a direct generalization of persistent currents from rings and lattices to arbitrary topologies. Detection of persistent currents provides a signature of the quantum phase coherence supported by the network, and a direct signature of the geometry of its cycle space. Such a mapping, not only allows a practical way to deal with quantum coherence for a variety of situations in the field of quantum technologies, but it also allows a physical interpretation of the importance of the Laplacian operator in graph theory, linking its role to the one of Hamiltonian (i.e. a tight-binding one) in physical systems. To test the power of the method, we construct a signed directed network that reproduces the Hofstadter butterfly spectrum.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06658"></a>
### [Capturing non-Markovian dynamics in non-equilibrium stochastic systems using flow matching](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06658v1)

**Authors:** Bhargav Sriram Siddani, John B. Bell, Alejandro L. Garcia, Ishan Srivastava  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06658v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06658_figures/2606.06658_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: The top row shows a subset of 100 independent one-dimensional realizations (along vertical direction) at time t = 5.1 × 10−3 simulated using Brownian random walkers, the regu- larized Dean–Kawasaki (DK) model, and the two flow matching (FM) models: Markovian and non-Markovian. The white patches denote negative values in the simulation. The bottom row com- pares the time evolution of the statistics of particle count in the target region around x = 0.7.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06658_figures/2606.06658_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 1 depicts independent realizations of an early-time state of the system. The regularized DK model results in high occurrence of nonphysical negative densities as compared to the ML models. While the DK model well-predicts mean particle densities in the target region, the non-Markovian ML model more accurately captures the spatial particle distribution and also the higher-order statistical moments (see bottom row), thus demonstrating significant non-Markovian effects at early times.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a generative flow matching method to overcome the limitations of coarse-grained equations in modeling particle systems exhibiting strong non-Markovian and non-Gaussian behavior. By directly learning from particle flux distributions, the model accurately captures short-time dynamics for Brownian particles, outperforming standard Markovian approximations.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical stochastic processes, the use of advanced machine learning techniques like Flow Matching to model complex non-equilibrium dynamics is highly relevant for simulating open quantum systems where memory effects are crucial.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is accurately modeling non-Markovian dynamics and highly non-Gaussian effects in stochastic particle systems, which standard coarse-grained equations fail to capture at short times or low densities.

**Main result.** A generative flow matching method successfully captures the short-time behavior and higher-order statistical moments of particle density distributions more accurately than established Markovian models like the regularized Dean-Kawasaki equation.

**Method.** The authors developed a generative flow matching (FM) technique that directly learns the probability distribution of fluxes by conditioning on the history of local states to incorporate memory effects.

**Model / system.** The study uses non-interacting Brownian particles governed by an external potential, comparing results against particle simulations and coarse-grained Stochastic Partial Differential Equations (SPDEs).

**Key observables.** Spatial particle distribution, higher-order statistical moments of the number density in a target region, and Kramers first passage time statistics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Short times ($t 	o 0$), low particle densities, and the memory length scale (captured by conditioning on previous states).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary limitation noted is that model accuracy degrades at later simulation times; the regularized DK equation's failure to capture history is also a key assumption being tested.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares realizations of system state and time evolution of particle count statistics across three models (Brownian random walkers, regularized DK, and two FM models) at early times.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the limitations of existing SPDEs for non-Markovian dynamics. It then introduces and details the generative flow matching framework, applying it to simulate a known physical problem (Kramers passage time) and comparing its results against established benchmarks.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hydrodynamic models of stochastic particle systems represented by coarse-grained stochastic partial differential equations (SPDE), such as the regularized Dean-Kawasaki (DK) equation, do not accurately capture the short-time system dynamics that is dominated by non-Markovian effects, and low particle density regimes where the distributions are highly non-Gaussian. We develop a generative flow matching method that directly models the probability distribution of fluxes from particle simulations that explicitly incorporates non-Markovian and non-Gaussian effects. As a demonstration, we use this method to simulate the Kramers first passage time problem for a system of non-interacting Brownian particles. We show the model accurately captures the short-time behavior and provides better predictions of the statistical moments of the number density when compared against the solution of the Markovian baseline, regularized DK equation.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.06626"></a>
### [Non-Hermitian Crystalline Braid Topology from Hermitian Projection: A Zero-Mode Resonance Mechanism](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06626v1)

The three panels follow the red trajectory in Fig. 7: the complex bands are unbraided before the first transition, acquire a nontrivial braid invariant after the first finite-frequency strand touching, and return to a trivial braid invariant after the next transition. In the first panel each band coincides with a separate strand and the loops do not interlink. In the second panel two exceptional points lie inside the Brillouin zone: each band traverses segments of both strands before closing, producing a nontrivial linking. In the...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a mechanism where non-Hermitian crystalline braid topology appears by projecting degrees of freedom from a simple Hermitian lattice. This process becomes singular (resonant) when eliminated zero modes couple to the remaining system, forcing the topological invariant into the finite-frequency response function. This offers a novel route to engineer complex quantum phases in physical realizations like circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a powerful theoretical framework showing that complex topological features can emerge purely from mathematical constraints (projection) rather than requiring explicit physical non-Hermiticity terms like gain/loss, which is highly relevant for designing novel quantum metamaterials and circuit elements.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To demonstrate that non-Hermitian crystalline braid topology can emerge solely from projecting a fully Hermitian, topologically trivial parent lattice without introducing explicit gain or loss.

**Main result.** The non-Hermitian topology is carried by the finite-frequency projected Green's function, arising specifically through a zero-mode resonance mechanism during projection. This results in a robust crystalline braid invariant that is immune to the non-Hermitian skin effect.

**Method.** The analysis employs the Schur complement technique to project degrees of freedom from a parent Hamiltonian onto a lower-dimensional brane subsystem. The key diagnostic tool is examining the singularity structure of the embedding self-energy at finite frequencies ($\omega$).

**Model / system.** The model uses a nearest-neighbor square lattice containing an embedded one-dimensional zig-zag brane, analyzed in momentum space. The effective non-Hermiticity arises from frequency-dependent coupling terms derived via projection.

**Key observables.** Finite-frequency projected Green's function, complex Berry phase (quantized to a braid count), transmission zeros, and admittance features at specific drive frequencies ($\omega$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The resonance condition depends on the parity of the eliminated complement ($N$ odd vs. even) and the tunable frequency parameter $\omega$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis distinguishes between regular projection (static limit) and resonant projection, which requires a zero mode in the eliminated complement to couple to the retained subsystem.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate energy band evolution, density of states comparisons for even vs. odd parity sectors, and complex spectra comparing analytical bulk solutions to numerical eigenvalues for both open and periodic boundary conditions.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically contrasts two projection regimes: the regular Schur complement route (recovering SSH-like physics) versus the resonant zero-mode projection. The core argument focuses on how the resonance forces topology into the finite-frequency response, culminating in the derivation of a crystalline braid invariant protected by symmetry.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Non-Hermitian topological phases are usually engineered through gain, loss, asymmetric couplings, or explicit environmental channels. Here we show that non-Hermitian crystalline braid topology can instead emerge from projection alone, starting from a fully Hermitian and topologically trivial parent lattice. The mechanism is zero-mode-resonant projection. When the eliminated complement is zero-mode free, projection has a smooth low-frequency limit and reduces to a static Schur complement, yielding conventional SSH-type descendants. When a complement zero mode couples to the retained subsystem, the embedding self-energy develops a pole, the zero-frequency limit becomes singular, and topology is carried by the finite-frequency projected Green's function-where frequency is a tunable parameter, the drive frequency in a circuit realization, for instance. We demonstrate this mechanism in an exactly solvable model, a trivial nearest-neighbor square lattice with an embedded one-dimensional zig-zag brane. Odd-parity periodic sectors are resonant: a sublattice-imbalance zero mode generates the singular self-energy, and the complex spectrum forms an abelian two-band braid whose transitions occur only at isolated finite frequencies. Although the internal class is $\text{AI}^†$ featuring only trivial phases, embedding parity induces conjugated pseudo-Hermiticity (CPH), quantizes the complex Berry phase, and identifies it with the braid count. The model is free of the non-Hermitian skin effect, making the invariant a genuine Bloch bulk quantity. In topolectrical realizations, the same finite-frequency braid transitions appear as transmission zeros and admittance features at the predicted drive frequencies.

</details>

### [Towards Engineering Material Neural Networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07262v1)

**Authors:** Charles de Kergariou, Hortense le Ferrand, Ali Momeni, Romain Fluery, Kunal Masania, Adam W Perriman, Fabrizio Scarpa

**Summary.** This paper introduces Engineering Material Neural Networks (EMNNs), a concept aiming to embed trainable intelligence directly into structural materials rather than relying on external electronics. It details a theoretical framework involving forward passes for inference and feedback passes for weight updates using physical error signals. The goal is to create fully autonomous, adaptive materials that perform computation through inherent material dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work proposes mapping abstract computational concepts (like gradient descent) onto physical laws governing materials. This bridges the gap between classical condensed matter physics/materials science and machine learning theory, which is highly relevant for developing novel physical computing paradigms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core problem is developing Engineering Material Neural Networks (EMNNs) capable of embedding trainable intelligence directly into load-bearing structural materials, moving beyond reliance on external digital computation.

**Main result.** The paper establishes a framework for EMNNs that approximate continuous functions through physical dynamics, proposing mechanisms like full physical feedback control to achieve autonomous adaptation.

**Method.** The methodology involves defining forward and feedback passes analogous to neural network training. Learning is achieved by calculating gradients based on physical error signals (loss) and using these to update material parameters.

**Model / system.** EMNNs are conceptualized as architected, load-bearing materials whose nodes possess trainable physical parameters (e.g., conductivity or stiffness). The system must exhibit interconnected adaptability, approximating a function $\phi$ through coupled material dynamics.

**Key observables.** Output temperatures ($T_o$), calculated loss ($L$), and updated physical parameters ($\lambda_{ij}$); the degree of reversibility and adaptability (PRAM vs. NPAM) are key diagnostics.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trainable physical parameters, input states ($T_i$), and material properties like stiffness or thermal conductivity; the regime shifts from in-silico to fully autonomous physical feedback control.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that complex computational functions can be mapped onto intrinsic, reversible, and energy-efficient physical processes within engineered materials. A major limitation noted is achieving true autonomous physical obtention of loss values.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the conceptual flow of EMNN operation through forward (FW) and feedback (FB) passes, comparing ideal vs. current training strategies across different levels of physical integration and reversibility.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from establishing the foundational concept of EMNNs to detailing the required mechanical/multifunctional properties. It then systematically reviews advanced learning techniques—from in-silico backpropagation to fully autonomous, material-embedded weight updates—culminating in a discussion of future biohybrid and permanently reversible systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Structures that capture functionality in the form of animate or intelligent machines have the potential to transform modern engineering applications. Animation and embedded intelligence are typically realised by integrating advanced capabilities such as reversibility, adaptive responses and learning directly into the materials themselves. Currently, the majority of adaptive material systems rely on predefined adaptive designs combined with in-service, electronics-based computing to dynamically modify the structural behaviour. However, structural configurations with interconnected adaptable nodes are able to approximate continuous functions, providing new possibilities and opportunities than classical metamaterials and computational materials. We discuss here the potential to design load-bearing engineering materials with trainable physical parameters and neural network-inspired morphologies, embedding intelligence directly into their structure, a concept we define as Engineering Material Neural Networks (EMNNs) as a subcategory of Physical Neural Networks. In this perspective, we first establish the foundational concept of EMNNs; we then detail the mechanical and multifunctional properties required for such structural configurations. Finally, we evaluate existing and emerging engineering materials that hold promise for enabling this innovative approach. Key material candidates for realising EMNNs include composites, architected, biological and engineering living materials. We also outline future directions in materials science and structural engineering for developing EMNNs.

</details>

### [Charge density waves and enhanced superconductivity in the electron-hole gas: a plausible, simple, physically intuitive model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06649v1)

**Authors:** Carl A Kukkonen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06649v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06649_figures/2606.06649_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Equilibrium density rₛ where the electron-hole gas has an energy minimum and the total pressure P = 0. The electron-hole gas becomes thermodynamically unstable when the bulk modulus B = 0. This point is known as the compressibility instability. Note that the rₛ of the B = 0 point is closely 1.25 that of the equilibrium rₛ where P = 0.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06649_figures/2606.06649_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 1 shows that the electron-hole gas has an equilibrium rₛ that varies from 4.19 (the same as the electron gas in a uniform background) when M/m = 1, and is asymptotic to rₛ = 1.63 for large M/m. The reason for the asymptotic behavior is that the kinetic energy of the holes becomes negligible for large mass, while the exchange energy is independent of mass and the correlation energy becomes independent of mass for large M. If there were no other factors, the electron-hole gas would sit at the equilibrium rₛ.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06649_figures/2606.06649_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 2. The electron-hole gas in this simple model has a unique minimum of its ground state energy at each mass ratio M/m; this is the equilibrium density. (a) Δ·(q/kᴹ)² at the equilibrium density for representative mass ratios. At small mass ratios the curves increase monotonically; for larger mass ratios the decrease at large q reflects the incipient charge density wave. (b) Δ·(q/kᴹ)² evaluated at the rₛ where the quantity first reaches zero, obtained by increasing rₛ from its equilibrium value. At small mass ratios the instability is at q = 0 (compressibility instability); at larger mass ratios it is at finite q (charge density wave).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06649_figures/2606.06649_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 3. The triple point at M/m = 4.97, rₛ = 3.036219. (a) Δ·(q/kᴹ)² at the triple point density, which appears close to zero over a wide range of q. (b) 100× magnification showing the two simultaneous zeros at q = 0 and q = 0.83kᴹ, confirming the coexistence of the compressibility instability and the charge density wave at a single density.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06649_figures/2606.06649_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 4. High sensitivity of the charge density wave instability to small changes in rₛ and M/m, illustrated for M/m = 7 (charge density wave at rₛ = 2.60779, q/kᴹ = 1.69). (a) rₛ varied by the amounts indicated. (b) M/m varied by the amounts indicated. A change of 0.1% in either variable is sufficient to trigger or suppress the charge density wave.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This theoretical work models an electron-hole gas to propose a unified mechanism explaining high-$T_c$ superconductivity, CDWs, and anomalous resistivity. By analyzing the system's response function $\Delta$, the authors show that instabilities inherent to the two-component plasma simultaneously enhance pairing attraction and drive charge ordering. The model provides quantitative estimates for $T_c$ approaching room temperature under specific mass ratio conditions.

**Why it may be interesting.** Although focused on condensed matter correlations, the methodology of linking multiple emergent phenomena (superconductivity, charge order) through a single mathematical instability parameter $\Delta$ is highly relevant for understanding complex many-body phase diagrams in quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates a potential common origin for high-temperature superconductivity, charge density waves (CDWs), and anomalous $T^2$ electrical resistivity in an electron-hole gas.

**Main result.** A single underlying instability mechanism within the two-component Coulomb gas simultaneously enhances all three phenomena, leading to crude estimates of room-temperature superconductivity ($T_c \approx 275 	ext{ K}$ at $M/m=9$).

**Method.** The analysis uses linear response theory applied to a simple, physically motivated model involving electron and hole interactions via Coulomb potentials.

**Model / system.** The system is a neutral degenerate plasma of equal numbers of electrons and positively charged fermions (holes), interacting purely via the Coulomb potential. The behavior is governed by a single diagnostic function $\Delta(q)$, which signals instabilities.

**Key observables.** Superconducting transition temperature ($T_c$), charge density wave instability wave vector ($q$), compressibility instability ($q=0$), and effective electron-electron interaction strength ($\lambda$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass ratio ($M/m$) and reduced Wigner-Seitz radius ($r_s$); the critical value $M/m = 4.97$ marks a triple point of instabilities.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key assumptions include approximating electron-hole correlation energy independence from density, assuming local field factors are those of independent uniform gases, and setting certain off-diagonal correlation terms to zero ($G_{12}=0$).

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the coexistence of instabilities (triple point at $M/m=4.97$), the strong enhancement of attractive interactions near instability points, and plots showing $T_c$ dependence on $M/m$.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by establishing the model using linear response theory, identifying the key diagnostic function $\Delta$, demonstrating how its zeros trigger CDW or compressibility instabilities, and finally applying these insights to estimate superconducting parameters ($\lambda, T_c$).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A neutral degenerate plasma of equal numbers of electrons and positively charged fermions (holes) -- the electron-hole gas -- is studied using a simple, physically motivated two-parameter model (density r_s and mass ratio M/m). Two simplifying assumptions are made but not proven: the electron-hole correlation energy is approximately independent of density and does not affect the pressure or bulk modulus, and the electron-hole contribution to the local field factors is zero, allowing use of the known electron-gas local field factors. With these assumptions the phase diagram, instabilities, and effective interactions can be calculated for all mass ratios and densities using simple formulae, reproducible on a laptop. The key new physics is the additional screening provided by the mobile holes. A single function Delta appears in the denominator of all effective interactions and response functions. Near the zeros of Delta at q = 0 (compressibility instability) and at finite q, three phenomena are simultaneously enhanced: charge density waves (for M/m >= 4.97), a large T^2 electrical resistivity from electron-hole scattering, and an attractive electron-electron interaction that is the purely electronic analog of BCS electron-phonon coupling. Crudely estimated superconducting transition temperatures approach room temperature, reaching approximately 275 K at M/m = 9. No claim is made that this model applies to any specific material. The aim is to provoke scrutiny of the assumptions, calculation of the unknown local field factors, and investigation of whether this minimal two-carrier framework maps onto real systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Regular and chaotic dynamics of nonlinear optomechanical systems controlled by modulated light](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06997v1)

**Summary.** This paper theoretically analyzes how nonlinearity influences the transition between regular and chaotic motion in optomechanical oscillators driven by modulated light. By mapping out phase space using Lyapunov exponents and Poincaré sections, the authors demonstrate that nonlinearity does not monotonically enhance chaos. The findings suggest controlling these systems near the edge of chaos for applications like sensing or random number generation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it applies nonlinear dynamics tools to a quantum optics platform (optomechanics), providing insights into chaos control and the engineering of non-classical states or noise sources using engineered potentials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the transition between regular and chaotic dynamics in nonlinear optomechanical systems subjected to modulated driving fields.

**Main result.** The system exhibits a non-monotonic dependence of chaos on nonlinearity order, and specific configurations (like membranes) show clear transitions from quasi-periodic motion to chaos, suggesting potential applications for sensing or random number generation.

**Method.** The analysis employs advanced tools from dynamical systems theory, including constructing bifurcation diagrams, calculating the largest Lyapunov exponent, and analyzing Poincaré sections.

**Model / system.** The model is an optomechanical system involving a mechanical resonator coupled to an optical field. The dynamics are governed by nonlinear interactions (linear, quadratic, cubic) between photons and mechanical displacements.

**Key observables.** Bifurcation diagrams ($\epsilon$ vs. dynamic regime), Largest Lyapunov exponent ($\lambda_{max}$), Power spectra, Phase portraits, and Poincaré sections.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Modulation amplitude/parameter ($\epsilon$), modulation frequency ($\Omega$), and the coupling strengths (linear $g$, quadratic $g'$, cubic $g''$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on the adiabatic elimination of the optical field variables, assuming the cavity decay rate is much larger than the mechanical resonance frequency.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate transitions between regimes: quasi-periodic motion (structured Poincaré points/discrete spectrum) vs. chaotic motion (randomly distributed points/continuous spectrum), particularly in membrane systems showing interwell chaos.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the dynamics by varying nonlinearity orders and control parameters ($\epsilon$), comparing results from different coupling terms, and detailing specific physical realizations like the membrane-in-the-middle configuration.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The nonlinear dynamics of a mechanical resonator in an optomechanical system with linear, quadratic and cubic photon-vibration interactions (with respect to mechanical displacements) in a modulated driving field under conditions of adiabatic elimination of the optical field is studied. Based on the constructed bifurcation diagrams of the mechanical coordinate and the largest Lyapunov exponent as a function of the modulation amplitude, as well as power spectra, phase portraits and Poincare sections, regions of regular and chaotic dynamics of the optomechanical system are identified. It is also shown that for a certain modulation amplitude in the presence of all three types of interactions, chaotic dynamics of the mechanical resonator (oscillator) is realized, which is replaced by quasi-periodic oscillations in the absence of cubic interaction, and the system returns to chaotic behavior if only linear interaction remains. This non-monotonic dependence of chaotic dynamics on the order of nonlinearity originates from the interplay between parametric driving and effective potential reshaping and manifests that nonlinearity does not always enhance chaos. For an optomechanical system in a membrane-in-the-middle configuration, where only quadratic photon-vibration interaction is present, it is demonstrated that at small modulation amplitudes the mechanical oscillator exhibits quasi-periodic motion in each of the wells of a symmetric two-minimum potential, whereas large modulation amplitudes lead to chaotic motion, involving interwell transitions.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07352"></a>
### [Tests of constructor theory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07352v1)

**Authors:** Chiara Marletto, David Deutsch, Vlatko Vedral  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07352v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07352_figures/2606.07352_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Information media and superinformation media: quantum systems can be proven to be a special case of superinformation media.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07352_figures/2606.07352_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 3: The entanglement-based witness of non-classicality. If M can entangle (by local means) the quantum probes Q and Q’, then it must be non-classical.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07352_figures/2606.07352_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 5: The quantum homogeniser used to model the constructor-based irre- versibility. The homogenizer consists of N qubits initialised in the target state, |ξ⟩. The system qubit is initialised in the input state |ρ⟩. At each step, the system qubit interacts with one of the qubits in the homogeniser, via the partial swap operation (parametrised by the angle η): 1 is the identity transformation, while Sα,β is the swap gate defined on two qubits α and β. As N →∞, the system qubit approaches the target state after N partial swaps.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07352_figures/2606.07352_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 6: The logic of self-reproduction with the replication-vehicle logic. An accurate self-reproducer (top) consists of the replicator R (in blue) and the vehicle V (in green) containing the copier C and the constructor B. In the copy phase, C copies the replicator R. In the construction phase, B executes the recipe in R to build a vehicle from generic resources N. Bottom: the copy of R and the newly constructed vehicle form the offspring.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Constructor Theory, a foundational framework proposing that fundamental laws should be expressed as constraints on the possible transformations (tasks) achievable by physical systems. It generalizes concepts like non-cloning and irreversibility to abstract information media, offering potential tests applicable even in regimes where standard quantum mechanics might break down.

**Why it may be interesting.** It offers a meta-theoretical framework that attempts to unify disparate physical concepts—information, computation, thermodynamics, and biology—under a single set of possibility constraints, which is highly relevant for foundational physics beyond current quantum field theories.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a foundational theory of physical principles that constrains what tasks are possible or impossible, extending beyond current quantum theories by focusing on task feasibility rather than specific dynamics.

**Main result.** Constructor Theory provides a framework where fundamental laws (like irreversibility and non-cloning) emerge from general constraints on information processing capabilities. It suggests that physical laws should be described as necessary conditions for the possibility of tasks.

**Method.** The theory uses an abstract concept of 'tasks' (input/output transformations) defined over generalized 'information media,' allowing statements to be made independent of specific dynamical formalisms like Hamiltonians or Hilbert spaces.

**Model / system.** The core model is the 'universal constructor,' a hypothetical machine capable of performing all physically possible tasks. Testing involves analyzing hybrid systems (e.g., quantum probes interacting with unknown media) and biological self-reproduction.

**Key observables.** Possibility/impossibility of transformations (tasks), non-classicality witnesses, fidelity of self-reproduction, and correlations in hybrid quantum-classical measurements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Scale independence, information capacity, and the degree of correlation between local interactions and global entanglement.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes principles like locality and interoperability of information; it aims to supplement existing laws rather than replace them entirely.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the replicator-vehicle logic for self-reproduction, the structure of composite information media, and entanglement witnesses derived from local interactions with an unknown medium.

**Paper structure.** The paper reviews constructor theory by first establishing its foundational principles (tasks, possibility), then applying it to specific domains like thermodynamics, quantum information (non-cloning/entanglement witnesses), and the emergence of life. It contrasts this approach with standard axiomatic reconstructions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Constructor theory is a proposal to extend quantum information theory beyond both quantum theory and computation, to cover more general machines than programmable computers -- called constructors. It consists of newly conjectured physical principles that can be expressed as constraints on what tasks are possible, what are impossible, and why. These principles also determine the repertoire of the universal constructor, which is a programmable machine that can perform all physically possible tasks. The principles of constructor theory have novel physical content that supplements current dynamical laws, leading to new predictions for experimental tests. In this paper, we review the main experimental proposals to test the principles of constructor theory and discuss their implications for existing theories of physics and for their successors.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a novel machine learning framework using Convolutional Neural Networks (CNNs) to pinpoint the critical temperature in anisotropic spin systems. By training on correlation matrices from Monte Carlo simulations, the method significantly reduces computational overhead while maintaining high accuracy compared to traditional thermodynamic calculations. This demonstrates a powerful synergy between deep learning and condensed matter physics for studying phase transitions.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on spin systems, the core methodology—using deep learning to extract complex physical parameters from high-dimensional simulation data—is highly relevant for analyzing quantum many-body dynamics or phase transitions in simulated quantum materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to evaluate the critical scaling behavior and identify the critical point ($T_c$) in anisotropic spin systems.

**Main result.** The machine learning approach provides estimates for $T_c$ that show very good agreement with reference Monte Carlo simulations while achieving a computational cost reduction of about 10x compared to classical methods.

**Method.** A Convolutional Neural Network (CNN) is trained on correlation matrices derived from Monte Carlo simulations. The critical temperature is then estimated using multiple inference techniques applied to the CNN's probabilistic output.

**Model / system.** The system modeled is a three-dimensional Heisenberg spin system exhibiting easy-axis anisotropy, described by a specific Hamiltonian involving exchange interactions ($J$) and anisotropy ($\Delta$).

**Key observables.** Correlation function $C_i(T)$, averaged output probabilities $P(T, L)$, deviation functions $E_r(T, L)$, and correlation-length-like quantities $\xi$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The critical exponent ($
u$) is determined to be approximately 0.58(1). The analysis depends on the system size ($L$) and temperature discretization.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The accuracy of the ML method relies heavily on the quality and density of the training data, particularly requiring fine temperature sampling near the critical region.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate correlation matrix changes across temperatures, the CNN architecture, and how varying the temperature sampling grid affects the sharpness of the transition observed in probability curves.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical problem, details the ML/CNN methodology using correlation matrices as input, presents multiple estimators for $T_c$ (crossing points, peak positions), validates results against classical Monte Carlo simulations, and concludes with finite-size scaling analysis to determine critical exponents.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Computational models adequately representing phase transitions and evaluating the critical system parameters are essential for the understanding of the properties of a wide range of materials. Here we propose a machine learning (ML)-based approach to the identification of the critical point in anisotropic spin systems. Our approach implies training of a convolutional neural network (CNN) model from the correlation matrices obtained by Monte Carlo simulations. Next, the pretrained model is employed as a fast estimator of the critical temperature, which can be extracted in several complementary ways from the CNN model inference, this way improving the robustness of the analysis. The ML-based estimates obtained in this study are in very good agreement with the reference Monte Carlo simulation results, while computational costs are about 10x lower compared to the classical thermodynamic approach.

</details>

### [Demystifying Objectivity with Operator Algebra Quantum Error Correction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06588v1)

**Authors:** Marin Girard, Gong Cheng, ChunJun Cao

In addition to their strong con- nection to existing QD concepts, the algebraic formula- tion provides precise tracking of both the type and the distribution of information — given f, one can efficiently determine the logical operators supported on it using our algorithm presented in the End Matter. With this im- proved precision, we can easily classify different strengths of objectivity and characterize them along our two cri- teria: classicality and redundancy. We highlight a few interesting classes and draw connections with existing lit- erature on QD.</figcaption>

The blue/pink hue also encodes ℓand ¯q. Different regimes of algebraic objectivity occupy distinct regions of this diagram, separated schematically by dashed lines. **Summary.** This work develops an algebraic framework to precisely define objectivity by linking it to the local recoverability properties within quantum error-correcting codes. By analyzing the structure of logical Pauli algebras on system fragments, the authors unify existing measures and provide quantitative criteria (SAO, LAO) for how classical information emerges from quantum entanglement.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep mathematical link between fundamental concepts of quantum foundations (objectivity/classicality) and the highly structured tools of Quantum Error Correction Codes, which is crucial for understanding decoherence dynamics in complex systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to precisely characterize the emergence of 'objectivity' and 'classicality' from quantum mechanics, moving beyond partial descriptions like Quantum Darwinism.

**Main result.** Objectivity is rigorously identified with the algebraic local recoverability of quantum codes, providing a unified and computable classification scheme for classicality and redundancy.

**Method.** The authors connect Quantum Darwinism to Operator Algebra Quantum Error Correction (QECC), using tools like analyzing the center of recoverable logical algebras ($Z(M_f)$) on stabilizer codes.

**Model / system.** The analysis is performed within the framework of quantum error-correcting codes, specifically utilizing stabilizer codes defined by Pauli subgroups. The dynamics are simulated via 'brickwork circuits' modeling system-environment interaction.

**Key observables.** Objectivity/Classicality Measure ($Z(M_f)$ non-triviality), Quantum Mutual Information (QMI), Classical bits ($c$), and Quantum qubits ($q$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Strong Algebraic Objectivity (SAO), Localized Algebraic Objectivity (LAO), Quantum Doped Objectivity (QDO); plateau widths related to code distances.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis heavily relies on the mathematical structure of stabilizer codes and uses Poisson approximations for calculating probabilities in random subsystem fragments.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate QMI plateaus vs. fragment size, depict different algebraic regimes (SAO, LAO, QDO), and show heatmaps mapping classical ($c$) versus quantum ($q$) information content over simulated time steps.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the problem using QD to establishing the connection with QECC algebra. It then develops theorems quantifying recoverability ($Z(M_f)$) and mutual information, culminating in proving equivalences between physical conditions (sQD) and algebraic structures (SAO).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum Darwinism extends the decoherence formalism to explain how classicality and objectivity emerge from quantum mechanics. However, existing approaches often capture only partial aspects of objectivity, leading to its mischaracterization and making it difficult to pin down precisely. By connecting quantum Darwinism to operator algebra quantum error correction, we show that the emergence of objectivity can be identified with the algebraic local recoverability of quantum codes. Applying this algebraic framework to stabilizer codes, we show that it yields a far more precise characterization of classicality and redundancy, unifies the traditional measures of objectivity, enables efficient classification via coding-theoretic tools, and supports large-scale Clifford simulations of decoherence dynamics.

</details>

### [Light-tunable quantum metric non-linear Hall response in Berry dipole semimetals](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06999v1)

**Authors:** Debashree Chowdhury, Awadhesh Narayan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06999v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

**Summary.** This theoretical work demonstrates that applying light to Berry dipole semimetals allows for active, tunable control over their nonlinear Hall response. By inducing an asymmetry in the quantum metric via optical driving, researchers show that the direction of the resulting current can be flipped simply by increasing the light intensity past a certain threshold.

**Why it may be interesting.** The concept of using light as a direct stimulus to control fundamental quantum geometric responses like the Hall effect is highly relevant for designing novel optoelectronic topological materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how light influences the intrinsic nonlinear Hall conductivity (NLHC) mediated by quantum metrics within Berry dipole semimetals.

**Main result.** Light induces a tunable asymmetry in the off-diagonal part of the quantum metric, leading to a controllable NLHC whose direction can reverse when the light amplitude exceeds a critical threshold.

**Method.** The authors used theoretical calculations involving Floquet theory (Floquet-Magnus expansion) and derived expressions for the Quantum Metric Dipole (DQM) under high-frequency optical driving.

**Model / system.** The system modeled is a Berry dipole semimetal (BDS), characterized by a quadratic band structure arising from two Weyl nodes at a mirror-symmetric interface. The analysis uses an effective Hamiltonian derived via optical driving.

**Key observables.** Nonlinear Hall conductivity (NLHC), Quantum Metric Dipole (QMD), and the off-diagonal components of the quantum metric ($G^{\Omega}_{xy}$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Light amplitude ($A_0$), light frequency ($\Omega$), and chemical potential ($\mu$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on the high-frequency approximation for optical driving, and specific limits (like $\Omega 	o 0$) require careful handling.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the BDS band structure, confirm the dipole nature of Berry curvature, show the emergence of light-induced asymmetry in quantum metric components ($G^{\Omega}_{xy}$), and plot the sign reversal of the DQM as a function of light amplitude.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the physical system (BDSs) and its Hamiltonian, through calculating the relevant geometric quantities (Berry curvature, Quantum Metric), applying optical driving via Floquet theory to derive effective Hamiltonians, and finally analyzing the resulting nonlinear transport response (NLHC/DQM).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the effect of light on quantum metric-mediated intrinsic nonlinear Hall conductivity in Berry dipole semimetals. We discover that light induces a tunable asymmetry in the off-diagonal part of the quantum metric, which is manifested by an asymmetry in the quantum metric dipole. We show that the nonlinear response can be tuned directly by the light amplitude. In particular, we note that the direction of the nonlinear Hall signal changes when the light amplitude is increased beyond a threshold value. Light thus emerges as a promising stimulus to control the quantum geometric response in topological semimetals.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07043"></a>
### [Exact noise characterization of entanglement distribution in star networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07043v1)

**Authors:** Kenneth Goodenough, Xiaonan Chen, Patrick Emonts  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07043v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

Solid lines are the case with binning (explained in the main text), dashed lines are without binning. The dotted, grey, vertical lines show the difference between factory and the piecemaker protocol.</figcaption>

**Summary.** This work analyzes the noise accumulation when distributing multipartite entanglement over star network topologies. By comparing two protocols—Factory and Piece-maker—the authors derive exact analytical expressions for the average noise and its distribution under memory decoherence. The findings strongly advocate for the piececemaker protocol due to its superior performance in maintaining high fidelity across multiple users.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides rigorous analytical tools for quantifying resource degradation in quantum communication channels due to realistic decoherence mechanisms, which is crucial for designing fault-tolerant quantum networks.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper characterizes the noise incurred during multipartite entanglement distribution across star networks, focusing on how memory decoherence affects the final state fidelity.

**Main result.** It derives closed-form expressions showing that the piecemaker protocol significantly outperforms the factory protocol in terms of achievable users and key rates for a given level of noise.

**Method.** The analysis uses analytical derivations, including generating functions and Pauli basis expansions, to calculate the average noise parameter E[Lambda] and its full probability distribution.

**Model / system.** The system models quantum networking using star topologies where entangled states (like GHZ states) are distributed via a central node. The primary physical effect modeled is memory decoherence (dephasing or depolarizing noise) accumulated during stochastic waiting times.

**Key observables.** Average noise parameter E[Lambda], output fidelity F, and conference key agreement rate.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Decoherence rates ($\lambda$), link failure probability ($q$), number of users ($n$), and the global cut-off time ($T_c$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis primarily focuses on memory decoherence; gate/measurement noise and initial state infidelities are ignored. The results often rely on approximations for finite cut-offs.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare fidelity vs. number of users (n) and key agreement rate vs. cut-off time ($T_c$) for both protocols, demonstrating the superiority of the piecemaker method.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically derives noise metrics for two protocols (Factory and Piece-maker), extends the analysis from dephasing to depolarizing noise, calculates the full distribution of noise parameters, and compares performance using key rate metrics under finite cut-off conditions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multipartite entanglement forms the core of many networking applications. In the near-term future, it is expected that multipartite distribution will be achieved first through star topologies, making it important to understand the noise incurred during the distribution process. In such networks, elementary links are created stochastically and successful links must be stored while waiting for the remaining links, causing memory decoherence that depends on the random waiting times. We derive analytical expressions for both the average noise and its distribution, when distributing GHZ states under memory dephasing in star networks. We study and compare two distribution protocols: the factory and piecemaker protocol. Furthermore, we find expressions for the case of a global cut-off (allowing fast optimization of the cut-off without requiring Monte Carlo simulations) and extend the analysis for the factory protocol to depolarizing noise for arbitrary states.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.07425"></a>
### [Tomography of quantum states with bounded extent](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07425v1)

**Authors:** Srinivasan Arunachalam, Arkopal Dutt  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07425v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07425_figures/2606.07425_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Circuit for preparing the state |eβ⟩.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a general theoretical framework for performing quantum state tomography when the unknown state has a low-complexity decomposition over a structured model class. By boosting weak agnostic learners, the authors derive efficient algorithms that estimate the state with bounded error, providing significant advances over previous methods that required full access to the state preparation unitary.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work provides powerful tools to characterize and learn complex quantum states based on their underlying structure, which is crucial for understanding resource limitations in quantum computation protocols.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a general framework for quantum state tomography when the unknown state $|\psiangle$ is assumed to have a 'bounded extent' with respect to a structured class of states $C$. This addresses efficiently learning an unknown quantum state that admits a low-complexity decomposition over $C$.

**Main result.** The authors provide algorithmic protocols for tomography achieving $\epsilon$-error estimation, showing that the weak agnostic learner can be boosted into a strong algorithm. For stabilizer states, this is achieved in quasipolynomial time.

**Method.** The core method involves leveraging the existence of a weak agnostic learner for $C$ and applying an algorithmic decomposition theorem to reconstruct $|\psiangle$. This process uses techniques like ridge projection and boosting from computational learning theory.

**Model / system.** The system is general quantum states, specifically focusing on $n$-qubit systems. The structure is formalized by a class of states $C$ (e.g., stabilizer states, MPS states), and the complexity is measured by the 'bounded extent' $\xi$.

**Key observables.** Fidelity ($F_C(|\psiangle)$), trace distance ($\epsilon$), Stabilizer extent ($\xi$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of qubits ($n$), Extent bound ($\xi$), Error tolerance ($\epsilon$), Sample complexity, Time complexity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results rely on $|\psiangle$ admitting a low-complexity decomposition over $C$, and the best polynomial bounds assume the algorithmic PFR conjecture.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from general framework development (Chunk 1) to specific algorithms for structure learning and parameter estimation using ridge projectors (Chunk 2). It details protocols for various structured classes like stabilizer states and MPS (Chunks 3, 5, 6), while also providing rigorous mathematical analysis of the projection process and condition number bounds (Chunks 7, 8).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We give a general framework for tomography of states that have bounded-extent with respect to a structured class of states. Let $\textsf{C}$ be a family of $n$-qubit states such that: $(i)$ $\textsf{C}$ is succinctly representable and $(ii)$ there is a weak agnostic learner of $\textsf{C}$. We give a tomography protocol for an unknown state $|ψ\rangle$ that is promised to admit a decomposition of the form $|ψ\rangle = \sum_i c_i |φ_i\rangle$, where $|φ_i\rangle \in \textsf{C}$ with bounded $\ell_1$-norm of the coefficients (which we call extent). Our main contribution is to show that a weak agnostic learner for $\textsf{C}$ can be boosted into a tomography algorithm for states with bounded extent with respect to $\textsf{C}$.   Our reduction is black-box and applies broadly across model classes. As an application, when $\textsf{C}$ is the class of stabilizer states, we obtain tomography algorithms for states with stabilizer extent $ξ$ up to trace distance $\varepsilon$, in time $\textsf{poly}(n,(ξ/\varepsilon)^{\log(ξ/\varepsilon)})$, which is improvable to $ \textsf{poly}(n,ξ,1/\varepsilon)$ assuming the algorithmic polynomial Freiman-Ruzsa conjecture in the high-doubling regime. When the unknown state $|ψ\rangle$ is arbitrary, we give an algorithmic decomposition result in the spirit of a weak regularity lemma for quantum states with respect to $\textsf{C}$ and show that the structure in $|ψ\rangle$ that is explainable by $\textsf{C}$ can be efficiently learned. Our main conceptual message is that agnostic learning of a structured base class automatically yields learnability of its low-complexity linear span.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [On the true low-energy excitations of the three-dimensional spin glass](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07197v1)

**Summary.** This work uses large-scale Monte Carlo simulations to investigate the low-energy excitations of 3D spin glasses. By extrapolating results down to zero temperature, the authors find that Replica-Symmetry Breaking theory provides the best fit for key scaling parameters. The findings offer strong numerical confirmation of the overlap-equivalence hypothesis in disordered magnetic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical statistical mechanics, the rigorous numerical techniques used for extrapolation and phase boundary determination are highly relevant to understanding complex energy landscapes in quantum many-body systems, particularly those exhibiting glassy behavior.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to determine which theoretical framework (RSB, DM, CP, or TNT) correctly describes the low-energy excitations of three-dimensional spin glasses.

**Main result.** The simulations provide strong evidence supporting the Replica-Symmetry Breaking theory for the fractal dimension and confirm the overlap-equivalence hypothesis through smooth extrapolations to zero temperature.

**Method.** Large-scale Monte Carlo simulations are performed on lattices up to L=18, involving complex extrapolation techniques to reach the thermodynamic limit (L->infinity) and zero temperature (T->0).

**Model / system.** The system studied is a three-dimensional spin glass (Edwards-Anderson type), modeled by Ising spins ($\sigma_i = \pm 1$) interacting via random couplings ($J_{ij}$). The analysis focuses on the behavior of the spin overlap parameter $q$ and the link overlap $q_l$.

**Key observables.** Spin overlap ($q$), link overlap ($q_l$), energy density ($\langle H/V angle$), fractal dimension ($d_f$), and the probability distribution $P(q)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size L (up to 18), Temperature T (extrapolated down to zero), Dimension D=3.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Key assumptions include the commutativity of limits ($T 	o 0$ and $L 	o \infty$) and the overlap-equivalence hypothesis, which links link overlap to the spin overlap.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate Parisi-Toulouse scaling for $P(q)$ vs T, and show extrapolations of $E(q_l|q_2=0)$ versus system size L and temperature T using various fitting ansatze.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by defining the physical problem (low-energy excitations), detailing observables ($q, q_l$), presenting simulation results showing smooth extrapolations to $T=0$, and finally comparing these findings against predictions from established theoretical frameworks like RSB theory.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the low-energy excitations of the three dimensional spin glass through a large-scale Monte Carlo simulation on lattices up to $L=18$. We find smooth extrapolations down to zero temperature, which, in the case of the energy and of the link overlap, can be directly -- and favourably -- compared with previous investigations featuring ground states (i.e., at zero temperature). The best fit for the fractal dimension of the excitations is provided by Replica-Symmetry Breaking theory, but we also consider the alternative TNT description. The $P(q)$ is found to verify the Parisi-Toulouse temperature scaling. Our data provides a spectacular confirmation of the overlap-equivalence hypothesis.

</details>
