**Summary.** The paper theoretically investigates the control of circular dichroism observed during the ionization of 3-methylcyclopentanone using chirped laser pulses. By solving the time-dependent Schrödinger equation within sophisticated quantum models, the authors pinpoint that the interaction between sequential absorption steps is responsible for the chirp dependence of the measured anisotropy. This provides a detailed mechanism for controlling chiral signals in molecular physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it uses advanced quantum dynamics simulations (TDSE) to model coherent control in molecular spectroscopy, specifically demonstrating how sequential resonance pathways dictate measurable chiral properties like CDIY.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to theoretically elucidate how circular dichroism (CD) in the ion yield, resulting from a three-step ionization process ($1+1+1$), can be controlled by using femtosecond linearly chirped laser pulses.

**Main result.** The anisotropy factor's dependence on chirp is governed by the interplay between the first and second absorption steps; randomized models successfully reproduce the qualitative features of this chirp enhancement, confirming a sequential resonance mechanism.

**Method.** The authors numerically solve the time-dependent Schrödinger equation to track molecular excitation dynamics through multiple absorption steps using both minimal and randomized quantum models.

**Model / system.** The system is 3-methylcyclopentanone (3MCP) undergoing $1+1+1$ ionization. The Hamiltonian governs the time evolution, incorporating field-free molecular terms and dipole interactions for sequential excitation from ground state to A-band vibronic states, then to Rydberg states.

**Key observables.** Circular Dichroism in Ion Yield (CDIY), Anisotropy Factor ($g$), population distributions in Rydberg states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Linearly chirped laser pulses characterized by Group Delay Dispersion (GDD); central wavelengths ($\lambda_c$); temperature (for conformational averaging).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The second absorption step is modeled using effective approaches, and rotational degrees of freedom are sometimes neglected or simplified to a linear rotor for estimation.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the $1+1+1$ ionization scheme; subsequent figures show how the anisotropy factor changes with GDD (chirp) in both minimal and randomized models, and how conformational averaging improves quantitative agreement.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical framework using the time-dependent Schrödinger equation for multi-step excitation. It develops simplified 'minimal' and complex 'randomized' models to isolate physical mechanisms. Finally, it applies these models to analyze the dependence of CDIY on laser chirp and molecular conformation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We theoretically investigate circular dichroism in the ion yield following $1+1+1$ ionization of 3-methylcyclopentanone using femtosecond linearly chirped laser pulses, inspired by recent experiments by Das et al. [Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys. 27, 8043 (2025)]. To this end, we numerically solve the time-dependent Schrödinger equation and evaluate the total population in the Rydberg states at the end of the second absorption step. The A-band transition in the first absorption step is treated using state-of-the-art quantum-chemical calculations, whereas the second absorption step is described via an effective model. Within our framework, we identify the interplay between the first and second absorption step as the key explanation for the experimentally observed chirp dependence of the anisotropy. By elucidating this mechanism for the chirp-enhanced signal, our findings contribute towards the development of improved control schemes for chiral molecules.

</details>

### [Field-rigid Ising antiferromagnetism with giant spin-flip fields in Van der Waals UOTe](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06678v1)

**Summary.** This work investigates UOTe, a Van der Waals antiferromagnet that combines metallic conduction with strong magnetic anisotropy and quasi-2D Ising order. Through extensive measurements up to 73 T, the authors demonstrate that the material maintains its field-rigid Ising character even when entering a complex metamagnetic state at high fields. This establishes UOTe as a unique platform for studying correlated magnetism in low dimensions.

**Why it may be interesting.** The combination of extreme magnetic field studies (up to 73 T) on a correlated electron system within a van der Waals structure is highly relevant. The persistence of Ising rigidity into the high-field regime provides insights into quantum phase transitions in strongly interacting, low-dimensional magnets.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To characterize a Van der Waals antiferromagnet exhibiting field-rigid Ising behavior alongside metallic, correlated electronic structure.

**Main result.** UOTe establishes a unique platform where high-temperature c-axis order, quasi-2D Ising criticality, and Kondo hybridization coexist, with the magnetic rigidity persisting into a broad, high-field metamagnetic regime.

**Method.** The study employs a combination of techniques including neutron diffraction, temperature/angle-dependent susceptibility measurements, torque magnetometry, and pulsed-field magnetization up to 73 T.

**Model / system.** The physical system is UOTe, a compensated Van der Waals material. It exhibits strong magnetic anisotropy, quasi-two-dimensional Ising antiferromagnetism below $T_N \approx 150$ K, and metallic transport linked to Kondo-associated uranium 5f hybridization.

**Key observables.** Magnetic susceptibility ($\chi(T)$), critical exponent $eta$, torque ($	au$), pulsed magnetization ($M(H)$), and angle dependence of the metamagnetic instability field component ($H_c(	heta) \propto \cos	heta$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Critical exponent $eta \approx 0.14$ (near 2D Ising value); high-field regime onset near 50 T; $T_N \simeq 150$ K.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the magnetic response can be modeled by uniaxial antiferromagnetism and that the high-field instability is governed by the field component along the ordered moment direction.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate crystal structure, temperature dependence of magnetic Bragg peak intensity showing 2D Ising scaling, $\chi(T)$ anisotropy, angular cuts through torque data confirming uniaxiality, and $M(H)$ curves revealing a broad, hysteretic metamagnetic reconstruction rather than a simple spin flip.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically establishes the material's magnetic anisotropy via susceptibility measurements, confirms 2D Ising critical behavior using neutron diffraction, verifies the uniaxial nature of the ordered state with torque magnetometry, and finally probes the high-field stability and metamagnetic reconstruction using pulsed-field magnetization and angle-dependent PDO measurements.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Van der Waals antiferromagnets provide a route to thickness-controlled magnetic order, but few combine high-temperature Ising order with conducting, correlated, and topological electronic structure. Here we show that UOTe realizes this combination. Magnetic susceptibility reveals a strongly anisotropic paramagnetic response, while neutron diffraction establishes c-axis antiferromagnetic order below $T_N \simeq 150$ K with an order-parameter exponent $β= 0.14$, close to the two-dimensional Ising value. Torque magnetometry further shows that the ordered state remains well described by a uniaxial antiferromagnet below the high-field transition. Pulsed-field magnetization up to 73 T shows that the ordered state survives to very large fields applied along the c axis before entering a broad metamagnetic regime that begins near 50 T, and remains unsaturated at the highest measured field. Angle-dependent proximity detector oscillator measurements show that the metamagnetic instability is set by the field component along the ordered moment direction, providing direct evidence for Ising-like field rigidity. UOTe therefore establishes a field-rigid Ising antiferromagnet with giant spin-flip fields in a compensated Van der Waals metal, where high-temperature c-axis order, quasi-two-dimensional magnetic criticality, Kondo-associated uranium 5f hybridization, metallic transport, and symmetry-enabled topology coexist in a single material.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper models supply chain resilience by studying how random production capacity fluctuations and input substitutability affect the system's critical demand. It finds that managing volatility (synchronization) and diversifying suppliers are crucial for maintaining stability, even more so than simply increasing average resource availability.

**Why it may be interesting.** While applied to economics/operations research, the mathematical framework involves stochastic processes and network flow constraints that share conceptual parallels with analyzing energy or information flow bottlenecks in complex quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how capacity volatility and input substitutability affect critical demands (resilience) within stochastic supply chains, extending the Feld-Barthelemy framework.

**Main result.** Reducing production-capacity volatility is often more effective than increasing average capacity in long chains, while structural diversification via substitute suppliers significantly enhances resilience by dispersing shocks.

**Method.** The analysis uses theoretical modeling based on Directed Acyclic Graphs (DAGs) to derive expressions for critical demand ($r^*$) under various stochastic assumptions.

**Model / system.** The system is a supply chain modeled as a DAG where production capacity $m_i(t)$ is random. The core dynamics involve order propagation, constrained production via a Leontief-type function, and stock updates.

**Key observables.** Critical Demand ($r^*$), which represents the maximum sustainable demand rate of the root node.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Capacity volatility ($\sigma$), average capacity ($\mu$), supply chain length ($N$), and degree of input substitutability (number of suppliers $z$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes no stock capacity ($s=0$) for key comparisons, and it builds upon the Feld-Barthelemy model assumptions.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate order/delivery processes in 1D chains, showing how volatility ($\sigma$) impacts $r^*$, and demonstrate that increasing supplier diversification ($z$) raises critical demand in tree structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper develops the mathematical framework for stochastic supply chain flow, first analyzing the impact of capacity distribution (e.g., truncated normal) on $r^*$ in non-substitutable chains, then extending this to incorporate input substitutability via a modified Leontief function.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Supply chains are intrinsically vulnerable to stochastic shocks due to their sequential production dependencies. Building on the Feld-Barthelemy framework, we investigate how capacity volatility and input substitutability determine critical demands in stochastic supply chains. By modeling production capacity with a truncated normal distribution, we show that in long supply chains, reducing capacity volatility is often more effective than increasing average capacity, emphasizing the need for firm-level synchronization. Furthermore, introducing a modified Leontief-type production function reveals that input substitutability effectively disperses stochastic shocks. Supplier diversification inherently raises critical demands, even under fixed maximum capacities, by introducing the effect of network topology that independently enhances the resilience of physical stock. Our findings demonstrate that mitigating capacity volatility and structurally diversifying supply routes are just as crucial to supply chain resilience as traditional inventory expansion.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07002"></a>
### [Phase lag enhances synchronization in coupled oscillators with inertia](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07002v1)

**Authors:** Sudo Yi, Cook Hyun Kim, Heetae Kim, B. Kahng  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07002v1>

**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Frenkel-Kontorova` **1/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper analyzes how controlled external perturbations, specifically applying a symmetry-breaking phase lag, can significantly boost global synchronization in coupled oscillators that possess inertia. By forcing the primary cluster to incorporate secondary frequency clusters, the method reveals a mechanism for actively controlling and improving collective coherence in complex dynamical systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on classical oscillator dynamics, the concept of using an engineered non-conservative perturbation (phase lag) to stabilize or enhance collective order in a complex, multi-cluster state has parallels with controlling quantum phase transitions or stabilizing exotic many-body states.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how applying a controlled phase lag can enhance global synchronization in coupled oscillators that possess inertia, especially when the system naturally exhibits multiple synchronized clusters.

**Main result.** Controlled phase lags can enhance global synchronization by steering the primary cluster to merge with higher-order clusters, demonstrating a mechanism for improving entrainment in inertial oscillator networks.

**Method.** The analysis uses mean-field theory and quasi-static methods, examining the system's response to varying coupling strengths (K) while incorporating phase lags ($\alpha_i$) on a subset of oscillators.

**Model / system.** The model is based on the second-order Kuramoto model with inertia, described by an equation of motion involving second derivatives and damping terms. This system is used as a proxy for physical systems like synchronous machines in power grids.

**Key observables.** Order parameter (R), group-based order parameter ($R_g$), mean angular velocity ($\langle\dot{\varphi}_iangle$) of different clusters, and cluster boundaries determined by pinning/depinning conditions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inertia magnitude ($m$), coupling constant ($K$), phase lag ($\alpha_0$), and the fraction of oscillators subjected to the lag ($\eta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis relies on a minimal mean-field demonstration, assuming the existence of primary and higher-order clusters initially. The use of specific approximations for drifting oscillators is also noted.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate how the order parameter $R$ changes with coupling strength $K$ for different inertia values, showing enhancement near transition points when phase lags are applied. Other figures map mean angular velocities versus intrinsic frequencies to determine cluster boundaries.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes that inertia lowers synchronization in the 2nd KM compared to simpler models. It then introduces the controlled phase lag perturbation and analyzes how this external control mechanism forces a dynamical reorganization, leading to enhanced global coherence by merging distinct frequency clusters.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The second-order Kuramoto model with inertia exhibits different dynamical behaviors than the first-order KM without inertia. A central difference is its lower synchronization due to the emergence of multiple synchronized clusters with different frequencies. We aim to investigate how such lowered synchronization can be improved by applying external perturbations to the system in a steady state, for example, a symmetry-breaking phase lag to a subset of oscillators. We find that this phase lag steers the primary cluster along a specific path and enables it to merge with higher-order clusters, thereby enhancing global synchronization. Our results reveal a mechanism by which controlled phase lag can improve entrainment in inertial oscillator systems, with possible implications for synchronization control in inertial oscillator networks.

</details>

### [Bures geodesics for non-faithful states and quantum speed limit](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06759v1)

**Authors:** Sergio Carrasco, Dominique Spehner  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06759v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06759_figures/2606.06759_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: (a) Geodesic arcs connecting two faithful non-commuting qubit states, represented on the hypersphere S3</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06759_figures/2606.06759_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2: Geodesic arcs joining the perturbed states ρ1(ε1) and ρ2(ε2) of two non-faithful states ρ1 and ρ2 in the regularization method (plain red and blue curves). <figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06759_figures/2606.06759_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4: Complex-plane representation of the limit limε→0 z(cm,n) 21 (ε, ε) for the regularization curves (B2) with r = rm and ϕ = ϕn as in (B3). The colored circles and gray rays indicate resp. the radial index m and phase index n. The distribution of points shows that the limiting values fill the closed unit disk. The value r = 1 leads to Fubini geodesics, given by (44) with |z21| = 1 and arbitrary phases ϕ; the values 0 &lt; r &lt; 1 lead to elliptic rank-2 geodesics, and r = 0 corresponds to the rank-2 straight diameter geodesic.</figcaption>

</div>

**Summary.** This paper rigorously determines the geodesic arcs connecting non-faithful quantum states in the space of density matrices using the Bures metric. It establishes necessary and sufficient conditions for the uniqueness of these shortest paths by analyzing the overlap between state supports and kernels. The results have direct implications for understanding the minimum time required for quantum evolution, thus refining the Quantum Speed Limit.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides rigorous geometric tools to analyze the fundamental limits of quantum evolution (QSL) by characterizing the shortest paths between arbitrary mixed states, which is crucial for understanding decoherence and process characterization.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The central problem is determining the explicit mathematical form of Bures geodesic arcs connecting two non-faithful quantum states, which relates directly to establishing the Quantum Speed Limit (QSL).

**Main result.** The authors provide the general framework for these geodesics, showing that while a unique shortest path often exists under specific conditions related to state supports/kernels, infinitely many optimal paths can exist otherwise.

**Method.** The work employs geometric methods on the manifold of density matrices (Bures metric), utilizing techniques like regularization and subspace restriction to analyze boundary behavior for non-invertible states.

**Model / system.** The system is general quantum state evolution described by density operators ($ho$) in a finite-dimensional Hilbert space $H$. The analysis focuses on the geometry of this state space, particularly when states are mixed (non-faithful).

**Key observables.** Bures distance ($d_B$), Fidelity ($F$), Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), and the rank of the geodesic path.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension of the Hilbert space ($n$), the ranks of the initial/final states ($r_1, r_2$), and regularization parameters ($\epsilon$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on approximating boundary geodesics by taking limits from faithful (invertible) states or restricting the path to subspaces spanned by the supports of the end-states.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate geodesic arcs connecting qubit states, showing differences between unique shortest paths and cases with infinitely many optimal arcs when states are orthogonal. Tables summarize properties based on state ranks for specific dimensions like qutrits and qubits.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from the known pure-state (Fubini-Study) case to general mixed states, developing techniques involving regularization and subspace analysis to handle non-faithful states ($	ext{rank} < n$). It culminates in explicit conditions for geodesic uniqueness based on kernel/support intersections.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The quantum speed limit establishes a bound on the minimal time required for a quantum system to evolve from a given initial state to a final state. When the mean energy variance is fixed this limitation is captured by the Mandelstam--Tamm bound. The fastest quantum evolution saturating this bound follows a geodesic arc connecting the two states. Such geodesics in the manifold of quantum states are explicitly known when the states are pure (Fubini-Study geodesics) and when they are mixed and given by faithful density matrices (Bures geodesics). In this article we obtain the explicit form of the Bures geodesic arcs joining two non-faithful density matrices, which may have different ranks. For pure states one recovers the Fubini-Study geodesics. A necessary and sufficient condition for the uniqueness of the shortest geodesic arc is given. When the condition is not fulfilled there are infinitely many such arcs, all having length equal to the arccos Bures distance between the two states, in analogy with the arcs of great circles connecting the two poles of a sphere. We discuss the implications of our results for the quantum speed limit.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07349"></a>
### [Direct High-Magnetic-Field Coupling to Stripe Order in a Cuprate Superconductor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07349v1)

**Authors:** Leonardo Martinelli, Izabela Biało, Jens Oppliger, Julia Küspert, Orion Gerguri, Sandro Brunner, Benedikt Eggert, Mark H. Fischer, Jochen Geck, Marein Rahn, Ellen Fogh, Jaewon Choi, Atsuhiko Miyata, Oleksandr Prokhnenko, Zahirul Islam, Fernando Igoa Saldaña, Martin v. Zimmermann, Rachel Nickel, Kurt Kummer, Nicholas B. Brookes, Adheena Painganoor, Paola Caterina Forino, Rasmus Toft-Petersen, Niels Bech Christensen, Xunyang Hong, Qisi Wang, Tohru Kurosawa, Naoki Momono, Migaku Oda, Dmitri V. Novikov, Azat Khadiev, Thomas Herrmannsdörfer, Ana Kurtanidze, Katharina Ollefs, Zuzana Konôpková, Michał Andrzejewski, Minxue Tang, Ulf Zastrau, Alexander Pelka, Hauke Höppner, Jolanta Sztuk-Dambietz, Vratko Rovensky, Torsten Laurus, Erik Brambrink, Björn Näser, Marcin Sikora, Cornelius Strohm, Carsten Baehtz, Shingo Yamamoto, Johan Chang

**Summary.** This work uses high-field XFEL diffraction to probe the normal state of cuprate superconductors under extreme magnetic fields. The key finding is a linear enhancement of charge stripe order that persists even when superconductivity is suppressed, indicating a direct coupling between the field and the spin component of the stripe order. This challenges simple models based solely on superconducting phase competition.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on solid-state magnetism/superconductivity, the concept of field-induced quantum phase transitions and emergent order parameters in strongly correlated systems has parallels with understanding non-equilibrium dynamics in AMO or open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how high magnetic fields couple directly to the stripe order within the complex normal state of cuprate superconductors.

**Main result.** A linear enhancement of charge order amplitude and correlation length is observed far above the vortex melting transition, suggesting a direct coupling mechanism independent of superconductivity suppression.

**Method.** The research combines X-ray Free Electron Laser (XFEL) diffraction with intense magnetic field pulses (up to 44 T), complemented by magnetostriction measurements.

**Model / system.** The system is the cuprate superconductor $	ext{La}_{2-x}	ext{Sr}_x	ext{CuO}_4$, which exhibits intertwined orders including charge stripes, spin order, and superconductivity. The analysis contrasts field effects with theoretical models of phase competition.

**Key observables.** Charge order (CO) amplitude and correlation length; magnetostriction ($\Delta c/c$); intensity of stripe order reflections in the $(h, k, 30.5)$ plane.

**Important parameters / regimes.** High magnetic fields (up to 44 T); temperatures above the vortex melting transition ($B_m$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The linear field dependence observed is incompatible with standard phase competition models based on superconductivity suppression.

**Figures summary.** Figures show intensity measurements of charge order peaks vs. magnetic field, demonstrating a persistent linear increase in amplitude and correlation length above $B_m$.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the complex normal state physics by using high-field XFELs to probe stripe order, showing that the observed enhancement is a direct coupling mechanism rather than merely being an artifact of superconducting pair breaking.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconductivity in cuprates emerges out of a complex normal state that hosts density waves, pseudogap physics, and strange metal properties. Here, we access this normal state by synchronizing free-electron laser x-rays with high-magnetic-field pulses up to 44 T. We observe a linear increase in charge order amplitude and correlation length that persists far above the vortex melting transition. This behavior is incompatible with standard phase competition between charge order and superconductivity. By means of conventional hard x-ray diffraction and magnetostriction, we show that applied fields also enhance monoclinic lattice distortions. However, this magnetoelastic response is weaker and an epiphenomenon of the stripe order enhancement. Combined with recent observations of field-linear spin freezing, our results point to a direct coupling between magnetic field and the spin component of stripe order in the high-field normal state -- a mechanism independent of superconductivity suppression that has so far remained hidden from scattering probes.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.07331"></a>

**Authors:** Jacob Lamers, Guy Verschaffelt, Guy Van der Sande  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07331v1>

**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

**Summary.** This work rigorously analyzes classical adiabatic annealing applied to Ising machines for solving hard combinatorial optimization problems. The authors propose and test a hybrid annealing strategy designed to navigate the system's energy landscape by avoiding mathematical singularities (bifurcations). Ultimately, they conclude that while theoretically motivated, this advanced technique offers only marginal practical gains over simpler, established simulation methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** Although focused on classical optimization, the underlying mathematical machinery—analyzing dynamical systems stability (bifurcations) during a controlled parameter sweep—is highly relevant to understanding non-equilibrium dynamics in complex quantum or classical many-body systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper analyzes the performance of classical adiabatic annealing (CAA) for solving combinatorial optimization problems mapped onto Ising machines.

**Main result.** While hybrid CAA shows marginal improvements over simpler techniques like Regular Annealing, its practical advantage diminishes when compared against enhanced baselines such as the spin sign method.

**Method.** The authors use continuation methods to analyze the fixed points of the system's evolution equation while gradually transforming the Ising Hamiltonian from an initial solvable state to the target problem Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The model is an Ising Machine (IM) whose dynamics are governed by a time-dependent Hamiltonian. The process involves adiabatically evolving the coupling matrix and external fields using an interpolation parameter F, which is analogous to quantum adiabatic annealing.

**Key observables.** Spin amplitudes ($s_i$), ground state energy, Time-to-Target (TTT), and Success Rate (SR).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Annealing parameters like the rate of change in $F$ ($v_F$) and $eta$ ($v_eta$), noise strength ($\gamma$), and the initial coupling strength ($eta_{start}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the system can follow the instantaneous fixed point during annealing, though this is challenged by saddle-node bifurcations (SNs) which necessitate a hybrid two-stage approach.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the evolution of spin amplitudes and energy during CAA, showing how SNs limit direct adiabatic following. Later figures compare TTT across various annealing schedules (hybrid vs. regular) for MaxCut and Beasley instances.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by first introducing the theoretical framework of CAA on IMs, then detailing the necessary hybrid strategy to avoid bifurcations, followed by extensive benchmarking comparing this hybrid approach against simpler methods using metrics like TTT across various problem types.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ising machines are a promising approach to solve combinatorial optimization problems. They map these problems onto the Ising model and search for low-energy configurations. However, navigating the rugged energy landscapes of these systems remains difficult. To improve this navigation, classical adiabatic annealing has been proposed in the literature as a heuristic optimization method for classical Ising machines. Using this technique, the Hamiltonian of the Ising machine is gradually transformed from an easily solvable Hamiltonian to the target Hamiltonian. However, its purported effectiveness is primarily motivated by an analogy to quantum adiabatic annealing, and systematic benchmarking has remained limited.   In this work, we analyze the classical adiabatic annealing technique using continuation methods. Motivated by insights from this analysis, we propose an optimized annealing strategy we refer to as hybrid classical adiabatic annealing. We benchmark our proposed strategy using MaxCut instances with up to 800 spins and problems with external fields, for which it achieves a marginal improvement for a limited set of problems. We conclude that, although theoretically motivated and occasionally beneficial, the hybrid strategy does not offer a sufficient practical advantage over simpler, existing techniques.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.06689"></a>
### [Computational Superiority of Non-Markovian Kerr Feedback in Continuous-Variable Quantum Reservoir Computing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06689v1)

**Authors:** Daniel Soh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06689v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

The fixed linear Gaussian register is realized by an MZI mesh (Hmix) between DBR high reflectors forming the main cavity, which preserves the quantum state (contraction set by intrinsic loss, Hypothesis 2). A leaky right cavity wall (partial mirrors) couples each mode both to a weak homodyne tap—continuously reading the quadrature moments ⟨qn⟩that form the degree-d...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.06689_figures/2606.06689_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Connected cross-time witness Wrms versus Kerr coupling χ, reconstructed from the exact master equation (RMS over 64 input realizations). The witness vanishes at χ = 0 (Lemma 1) and activates with χ; the data follow a quadratic law (free-fit exponent p = 1.97, R2 = 0.999) rather than linear, consistent with dominance by the higher Kerr vertices (Appendix A).</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

(B) The cliff: ν⋆versus g/κ for several shot budgets—raising the budget barely helps. (C) The free 2D tuning at fixed high g/κ; the optimum sits on the echo-state contraction boundary, not in the interior, so the optimal operating region is boundary-bounded rather than a single point.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This work proves that adding Kerr nonlinearity to a time-delayed quantum reservoir dramatically increases its computational power compared to purely linear Gaussian systems. The advantage is quantified by showing that the achievable cross-time correlation rank scales with feedback depth rather than mode count, establishing an unbounded resource separation in theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous theoretical framework for quantifying how nonlinearity fundamentally changes the computational capacity of quantum memory elements, moving beyond simple linear mappings into complex nonlinear feature extraction.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To determine the fundamental computational advantage of incorporating a nonlinear Kerr element into a time-delayed quantum reservoir compared to purely linear Gaussian reservoirs.

**Main result.** The inclusion of Kerr feedback allows the system to achieve cross-time nonlinear correlations whose rank scales with the feedback depth (D), providing an unbounded resource separation from any finite-mode linear reservoir.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis using cumulant expansions, Volterra series, and spectral kernel calculus to compare the reachable feature space ranks of both Gaussian ($\chi=0$) and Kerr ($\chi 
eq 0$) models.

**Model / system.** Continuous-Variable Quantum Reservoir Computing (QRC) implemented with a time-delayed feedback loop containing a $\chi^{(3)}$ Kerr element. The dynamics are analyzed using the master equation formalism for open quantum systems.

**Key observables.** Reachable cross-time nonlinear kernel rank, connected cumulants ($\kappa^{(3)}$), and the mixed second difference witness $W(a, b)$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Kerr coupling strength ($\chi$), feedback depth ($D$), number of linear modes ($N$), and the Kerr-to-loss ratio ($g/\kappa$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often assumes specific spectral properties (e.g., distinct eigenvalues) or operates in weak-coupling regimes where perturbative expansions are valid.

**Figures summary.** Figures confirm that a structural witness of nonlinearity is zero for the Gaussian baseline ($\chi=0$) but non-zero and quadratic in $\chi$ when Kerr feedback is active, though operational advantage in weak coupling was not found.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from establishing the limitations of linear systems to proving an unbounded resource separation via kernel rank analysis. It contrasts structural theorems (existence) with empirical tests (task performance), concluding that while structurally superior, the operational gain is limited by noise/readout depth in weak regimes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A linear optical medium can delay, mix, and superpose light, but never make two pulses multiply: multiplication is nonlinear, and a linear system has no such operation. This roots a sharp limit on continuous-variable quantum reservoir computers (QRCs) built from Gaussian optics. Within the reservoir they cannot form genuine products of the input at different past times, the cross-time nonlinear correlations many temporal computations require; they can only fake them by storing each past input separately and multiplying in the readout, forcing an exponentially harder high-order measurement. We show that a single Kerr (intensity-dependent phase) element in a time-delayed feedback loop removes this limit. The Kerr effect makes phase depend on intensity, a true multiplication inside the medium; feedback makes the light revisit that element repeatedly, so one mode mixes its own history against itself once per round-trip. Feedback turns time into space: D passes through one nonlinear mode replace D parallel linear modes. We prove an unbounded resource separation (Theorem 3, Corollary 2): an N-mode Gaussian reservoir reaches cross-time nonlinear rank at most 2N, a hardware ceiling, while a single Kerr mode reaches rank equal to its feedback depth D, costing no extra modes. For every N, one Kerr mode performs a computation no N-mode linear reservoir can. Loss is the counterintuitive ingredient: each round-trip dims the light, so the nonlinear phase differs pass to pass, giving every echo its own fingerprint; without loss the passes would be redundant. We confirm activation on an exact open-system simulation and ground the separation in nonlinear channel equalization. Achievable D is 30 to 230 on integrated platforms, so one nonlinear mode replaces up to about 100 linear ones, at the price of measurement time.

</details>

### [Frequency Detuning and Interference-Induced Bohmian Chaos in a Two-Dimensional Anisotropic Harmonic Oscillator](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07011v1)

**Authors:** Umair Abdul Halim, Nurisya Mohd Shah, Chan Kar Tim, Ahmad Hazazi Ahmad Sumadi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07011v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `interference shaping light` **1/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This paper explores how quantum interference dictates the onset and spatial extent of chaotic motion in a 2D anisotropic harmonic oscillator using Bohmian mechanics. The key finding is that the ratio between the interference beating time and the particle's transport time ($\chi$) acts as a diagnostic: slow beating leads to extended chaos, while rapid decorrelation confines it locally.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides an interference-based mechanism for quantum chaos in a simple Hamiltonian system, linking macroscopic transport properties (chaos) directly to microscopic phase coherence dynamics, which is highly relevant for understanding non-equilibrium quantum dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how chaotic Bohmian trajectories emerge in a quantum system due to interference effects, rather than external randomness.

**Main result.** Chaotic transport transitions from localized to spatially extended regimes based on the temporal coherence of the interference-induced phase field. This coherence is quantified by a dimensionless parameter comparing beating time scales to transport times.

**Method.** The analysis uses Bohmian mechanics, deriving particle velocity from the wavefunction's phase gradient ($
abla S$), and employs Poincaré sections and Lyapunov exponents to characterize chaotic regions.

**Model / system.** The system is a two-dimensional anisotropic harmonic oscillator subjected to a three-mode superposition of its ground and first excited states. The dynamics are governed by Bohmian equations derived from the quantum wavefunction's phase structure.

**Key observables.** Bohmian velocity field ($\dot{\mathbf{r}} \propto 
abla S$), Lyapunov exponents, Poincaré sections, and the interference-induced phase structure.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimensionless coherence parameter $\chi = T_b / T_{tr}$, which compares the beating time scale ($T_b$) to the characteristic transport time ($T_{tr}$). The frequency ratio ($\omega_x : \omega_y$) controls this parameter.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Bohmian velocity is assumed finite and sampled away from exact nodes of the wavefunction. The coherence parameter $\chi$ is proposed as a diagnostic for chaos in this specific model.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare trajectories and Poincaré sections for strongly detuned vs. near-resonant cases, showing that slow beating (large $\chi$) leads to broader, sustained irregular dynamics compared to localized fluctuations seen under strong detuning (small $\chi$).

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the theoretical framework using Bohmian mechanics in a multi-mode oscillator, introduces the coherence parameter $\chi$ as the key diagnostic, and then analyzes specific frequency ratios ($\omega_x : \omega_y$) to demonstrate how $\chi$ dictates the spatial extent of chaos.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the emergence of chaotic Bohmian trajectories in a three-mode superposition of the ground and first excited states of a two-dimensional anisotropic harmonic oscillator. The analysis focuses on the interference-induced phase structure of the wavefunction, which determines the Bohmian velocity field through its phase gradient. We show that the spatial extent of chaotic motion is controlled by the temporal coherence of the interference pattern, set by the detuning between oscillator modes. Near resonance, slow beating generates long-lived phase-gradient structures that repeatedly stretch and fold nearby trajectories, leading to more spatially extended chaotic regions. In contrast, strong detuning produces rapid temporal decorrelation of the phase field and confines chaotic dynamics to localized regions of configuration space. To quantify this behavior, we use a dimensionless coherence parameter comparing the beating time scale with a characteristic transport time. The results identify temporal coherence of the interference-induced phase field as a useful diagnostic for chaotic transport in low-dimensional Bohmian systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2606.07485"></a>
### [Quantum correlations in QBism's reconstruction program](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07485v1)

**Authors:** Sachin Gupta, Jacques Pienaar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07485v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07485_figures/2606.07485_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: This illustrates one geometric configuration of the centered vectors that maximizes the</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2606.07485_figures/2606.07485_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2: One geometric configuration of the centered correlation vectors maximizing the I2233</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper explores bipartite correlations using qplex theories, a probabilistic framework inspired by QBism. By mapping joint expectations onto inner products of C-vectors, the authors show that while this geometry correctly predicts the Tsirelson bound for two outcomes (CHSH), it fails to constrain three-outcome tests (CGLMP), suggesting that additional principles are needed to fully reconstruct quantum theory.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel, non-Hilbert space geometric approach to understanding the fundamental constraints on correlations, which is relevant for foundational work in quantum information theory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates bipartite quantum correlations within a geometric framework called qplex theories, which are probabilistic models inspired by QBism.

**Main result.** Qplex geometry successfully reproduces the Tsirelson bound for two-outcome Bell tests (CHSH) but fails to constrain three-outcome inequalities (CGLMP), allowing for superquantum correlations.

**Method.** The authors use a geometric formulation, expressing joint expectation values as inner products between specially defined C-vectors, and study Bell inequalities as optimization problems over these qplex-compatible assignments.

**Model / system.** The model is bipartite quantum correlation testing using generalized probability structures (qplexes) derived from QBist principles, rather than standard Hilbert space formalism. The analysis covers two-outcome (CHSH) and three-outcome (CGLMP) scenarios.

**Key observables.** CHSH quantity ($I_{2222}$), CGLMP inequality ($I_{2233}$), and the inner product $\langle C(\mathbf{R}|A\alpha), C(\mathbf{R}|Beta) angle$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Tsirelson bound ($2\sqrt{2}$) and the algebraic maximum of 4 for $I_{2233}$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes repeatable measurements and utilizes the inner-product structure derived from qplex constraints, which are shown to be insufficient alone.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates optimal geometry for CHSH with orthogonal vectors; Figure 2 shows a configuration maximizing $I_{2233}$ where Bob's vectors are separated by $4\pi/3$.

**Paper structure.** The paper establishes the qplex framework, derives joint expectation values as inner products of C-vectors, and then applies optimization techniques to test CHSH (two outcomes) and CGLMP (three outcomes), culminating in a comparison between derived bounds and quantum limits.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

QBism recasts quantum theory as a normative framework for an agent's probability assignments, with the Born rule taking the form of a consistency condition known as the Urgleichung. Motivated by this perspective, qplex theories provide a broader class of probabilistic models in which the sets of valid states and measurements are constrained by QBist-inspired geometric conditions. While qplexes have been extensively studied for single systems, their implications for bipartite correlations remain largely unexplored. In this work, we investigate bipartite correlations in qplex theories by expressing joint expectation values as inner products between suitably defined $C$-vectors. This geometric formulation allows Bell-type inequalities to be studied as optimization problems over qplex-compatible probability assignments. We first analyze the CHSH scenario and show that the shared inner-product structure of the $C$-vectors restricts the maximal value to the Tsirelson bound $2\sqrt{2}$. We then turn to the three-outcome CGLMP inequality $I_{2233}$ and find that the same qplex-derived norm and inner-product constraints allow the algebraic maximum of 4, thereby exhibiting superquantum correlations. These results show that qplex geometry captures enough structure to reproduce an important quantum bound in the two-outcome case, but not enough to recover the full set of quantum correlation constraints. The analysis therefore suggests that additional principles are needed to complete the QBist reconstruction of quantum theory.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Unified Framework for Functional Theories of Quantum Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06676v1)

**Authors:** Chih-Chun Wang, Julia Liebert, Markus Penz, Christian Schilling  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.06676v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **1/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **1/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

**Summary.** This paper introduces a unified mathematical framework for functional theories of quantum many-body systems by reformulating the problem using reduced variables derived from basic observables. It systematically analyzes the relationship between pure-state and ensemble-state descriptions ($	ext{B}_p$ vs $	ext{B}_e$) across various models, providing powerful structural theorems applicable to strongly correlated electron systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a highly abstract yet powerful mathematical machinery that unifies several advanced concepts in condensed matter theory, such as DFT, RDMFT, and geometric constraints, which is crucial for understanding strongly correlated materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** To develop a unified mathematical framework for density-functional theory (DFT) and its variants for quantum systems on finite-dimensional Hilbert spaces.

**Main result.** The work establishes that the structural properties of functional theories can be proven systematically once, applicable across a broad class of finite-dimensional models defined by their 'scope.'

**Method.** The framework uses reduced variables (expectation values of basic observables) to replace the full quantum state description, employing tools from functional analysis and symplectic geometry.

**Model / system.** The theory applies to general quantum systems, including lattice fermions (Hubbard model), spin systems (qubits), and bosons on periodic lattices. The scope is defined by a set of basic observables and a fixed Hamiltonian part.

**Key observables.** Reduced variables ($\mu$), ground-state energy functional $E(v)$, pure-state observable range ($	ext{B}_p$), and ensemble-state observable range ($	ext{B}_e$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension of the parameter space ($m=\dim V$) and the nature of interactions (e.g., repulsive vs. attractive $U$ in dimer models).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework is most rigorous in the finite-dimensional setting, allowing for systematic mathematical proofs regarding representability.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the geometric relationship between $	ext{B}_p$ (e.g., two spheres) and $	ext{B}_e$ (the convex hull filling the gaps) for specific models like the double qubit.

**Paper structure.** The paper builds from defining the general scope $S=(V, H, \iota, H_0)$ to analyzing the geometric properties of observable ranges ($	ext{B}_p$ vs $	ext{B}_e$), and finally applying these structural results to specific physical examples like lattice fermions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce and study a unified framework for density-functional theory and its variants for quantum systems on finite-dimensional Hilbert spaces. These theories seek to reduce the complexity inherent in the many-body quantum problem by describing ground states through reduced variables. The central ingredients of our unified framework are a generalized choice of basic observables, whose expectation values define precisely those reduced variables, and a fixed part of the Hamiltonian characterizing the class of quantum systems under consideration. It is this minimal structure, which we call the scope of a functional theory, that is necessary and sufficient for the formulation of a functional theory. In particular, it allows one to define the universal functionals, establish their convexity and differentiability properties, address representability questions, and prove a Hohenberg-Kohn-type uniqueness result. A purification construction also relates ensemble and weighted-ensemble functionals to the pure-state variant. Particular emphasis is placed on functional theories with Lie-algebra observable structures, connecting the variational framework to symplectic geometry. The result of this work is a systematic mathematical formulation in which structural results can be proved once and applied across a broad class of finite-dimensional functional theories.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper advances the many-body bootstrap method by introducing non-local 'string' operators to improve bounds on ground-state observables in quantum spin systems. By applying this technique to models like the TFIM and ANNNI, the authors demonstrate that these extended constraints can accurately map out phase boundaries and resolve issues related to domain-wall excitations in symmetry-broken phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** The development of methods that rigorously constrain ground state properties using extended, non-local operators is highly valuable for understanding quantum phase transitions in strongly correlated magnetic materials where simple local descriptions fail.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The primary goal is obtaining rigorous, tight bounds on ground-state observables for quantum spin systems in the thermodynamic limit.

**Main result.** By incorporating non-local, string-like operators into the bootstrap method, the authors significantly tighten existing bounds, especially in spontaneously symmetry-breaking (SSB) phases.

**Method.** They employ the many-body bootstrap technique, formulating it as a Semidefinite Program (SDP), and crucially extend the operator set with non-local 'string' operators to handle domain-wall excitations.

**Model / system.** The work applies to 1D quantum spin systems, including the transverse-field Ising model (TFIM), the axial next-nearest-neighbor Ising model (ANNNI), and the Z3 chiral clock model. The Hamiltonians describe various magnetic ordering phases.

**Key observables.** Ground-state observables, energy density ($\Delta E$), magnetization density, and correlation functions ($\langle Z_0 Z_r angle$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The transverse field strength ($g$), the next-nearest-neighbor coupling ($\kappa$), and the symmetry parameter ($\phi$) are key tuning parameters used to map out phase diagrams.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes that incorporating string operators allows for a formulation directly in the thermodynamic limit, overcoming limitations of local operator sets in SSB phases.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the tightening of bootstrap bounds (e.g., energy density vs. parameters) when comparing results from local versus string-operator constrained SDPs across different models like TFIM and ANNNI.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the limitations of local operators in SSB phases, then introduces non-local string operators to formulate the bootstrap in the thermodynamic limit. It applies this construction sequentially to 1D TFIM, then ANNNI, and finally generalizes it to the Z3 chiral clock model.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Bootstrap is a numerical many-body method that provides rigorous bounds on ground-state observables by imposing a set of necessary constraints on the expectation values of operators. The quality of the resulting bounds is sensitive to the choice of operators entering the constraints. In particular, bounds on ground-state correlations are often loose in spontaneous symmetry-breaking (SSB) phases, since local operator sets cannot exclude domain-wall excitations. In this work, we introduce non-local, string-like operators into the bootstrap and show that the program can be formulated directly in thermodynamic limit. We then apply our construction to several 1D spin models. First, we obtain a significant tightening of the bounds in the SSB phase of the 1D transverse-field Ising model. Using the 1D axial next-nearest-neighbor Ising model, we further show that this tightening allows for a quantitative estimate of the locations of phase boundaries. Finally, we generalize the string operators to the 1D $\mathbb{Z}_3$ chiral clock model and track the behavior of the bounds across the phase diagram. Our results broaden the class of constraints available to the bootstrap and open a route toward bootstrapping more general symmetry-broken and topological phases, where the relevant constraints may involve non-local or extended operators.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.06919"></a>
### [Scalable Quantum Algorithms for Gutzwiller Projection](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.06919v1)

**Authors:** Byungmin Kang, Hyunwoong Kwon, Vito W. Scarola, Kwon Park

The measurement-based projection algorithm measures double occupancy at every site and postselects the outcome with no double occupancy. UBCS is the unitary operator that creates the BCS state from the vacuum or the null state |0⟩= |0⟩⊗N. PG imple- ments the Gutzwiller projection by measuring double occupancy at every site using CCNOT gates. The amplitude amplification algorithm replaces PG with a series of unitary operations U(k) aa with k = 1, · · · , L, where L is determined by two factors: (i) the probability weight of the RVB state within the BCS state, W, and (ii) the desired tolerance,...</figcaption>

(a) Evolution of the success probability |αn|2 as a function of n/L with L = 200001, starting from the initial value, W = 6.5 × 10−11, to the targeting value, 1 −δ2 with δ = 0.4, which is illustrated by the dark curve. In the actual implementation of the amplitude amplification algorithm, W is not known precisely beforehand and must be estimated. Consequently, its true value, Wtrue, might differ from the estimated one. On the right side of the figure, the final success probability...</figcaption>

Panels (a), (b), and (c) show the phase diagram of the overlap between the RVB state and the exact ground state of the t-J model as a function of pairing amplitude ∆and hole doping concentration x for finite-size systems with Ns = 10, 18, and 20, respectively. **Summary.** The paper presents scalable quantum algorithms to prepare the Gutzwiller-projected BCS state, which serves as an essential input for simulating strongly correlated electron models like the $t$-$J$ model. By employing Amplitude Amplification (AAGP), the required computational resources scale quadratically better than previous measurement-based techniques. This breakthrough moves projected state preparation from a theoretical hurdle to a potentially deployable protocol for quantum simulation.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant as it develops a concrete quantum computational primitive—scalable state preparation—essential for simulating complex many-body phenomena in condensed matter physics that are intractable classically.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The core challenge is preparing physically accurate input states for quantum simulation of strongly correlated lattice models, specifically due to the non-trivial Gutzwiller projection.

**Main result.** The proposed Amplitude Amplification for Gutzwiller Projection (AAGP) algorithm achieves a quadratic speedup in required projection queries compared to measurement-based methods. This makes state preparation practically feasible at relevant finite system sizes.

**Method.** The authors develop and analyze the AAGP quantum circuit, which uses amplitude amplification techniques derived from Grover's search structure to estimate the overlap between the desired Resonating Valence Bond (RVB) state and the initial BCS ansatz. The full BCS state itself is constructed using Givens rotations applied after diagonalizing a Bogoliubov-de Gennes Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The work focuses on strongly correlated lattice models, particularly the $t$-$J$ model derived from the Hubbard model, relevant to high-temperature superconductivity. The target state is the Gutzwiller-projected BCS state $|\psi_{	ext{RVB}}angle = \mathcal{P}_G |\psi_{	ext{BCS}}angle$.

**Key observables.** The probability weight $W = |\langle\psi_{RVB}|\psi_{BCS}angle|^2$ is the key observable, and the required number of projection queries ($O(1/\sqrt{W})$ vs. $O(1/W)$) quantifies the algorithmic improvement.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (e.g., 100 sites), pairing gap ($\Delta$), hole doping concentration ($x$), and the desired success tolerance ($\delta$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that while the weight $W$ decreases exponentially with system size, the quadratic speedup of AAGP is large enough to provide a decisive practical advantage at finite sizes. The BCS state construction relies on mapping fermionic operators via Bloch-Messiah decomposition.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the workflow for Gutzwiller projection using amplitude amplification (AAGP), show the structure of the quantum circuit involving Givens rotations, and demonstrate the convergence of success probability versus iteration number.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically builds the necessary components: first, defining the physical problem and the BCS state construction; second, detailing the inefficient measurement-based projection method; third, introducing and analyzing the AAGP algorithm using amplitude amplification theory (including recursion relations solved via Chebyshev polynomials); and finally, calculating the overlap weight $W$ for specific models like the $t$-$J$ model.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum simulation requires highly accurate input states. Gutzwiller-projected Bardeen-Cooper-Schrieffer (BCS) states provide physically motivated input states for solving strongly correlated lattice models, but their preparation on a quantum computer is hindered by the non-trivial nature of the Gutzwiller projection. We construct scalable quantum algorithms for this task by combining a circuit construction for arbitrary BCS states with the amplitude amplification for Gutzwiller projection (AAGP) procedure. AAGP yields a quadratic reduction in the number of projection queries compared with measurement-based postselection and leads to substantially improved fault-tolerant resource scaling. For projected BCS states optimized for the square-lattice $t$-$J$ model, we find that the projected-state weight decreases exponentially with system size, but the quadratic improvement is still large enough at physically relevant finite sizes to make a decisive practical difference. In particular, for a 100-site benchmark, AAGP reduces the required number of projection queries by about seven orders of magnitude. These results establish AAGP as an enabling input-state preparation protocol for projected BCS states in quantum simulation.

</details>

**Summary.** This work uses Quantum Monte Carlo simulations on the extended chequerboard J-Q model to map its phase diagram. The authors demonstrate that the transition from the Antiferromagnetic (AFM) state to the Plaquette-Singlet (PS) solid is universally first-order for $\lambda < 1$. Crucially, they establish that this transition is governed by an emergent O(4) symmetry.

**Why it may be interesting.** The detailed study of emergent symmetries (O(4) vs O(5)) in a lattice model provides deep insight into how underlying microscopic interactions dictate macroscopic quantum behavior, which is fundamental to understanding strongly correlated materials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the ground state properties and phase diagram of an extended chequerboard J-Q model to understand the nature of the direct transition from the Antiferromagnetic (AFM) state to the Plaquette-Singlet (PS) solid state, relevant to materials like SrCu2(BO3)2.

**Main result.** The research concludes that for parameters $\lambda < 1$, the system undergoes a direct first-order phase transition between AFM and PS phases, exhibiting an emergent O(4) symmetry. No intermediate phase was found across the investigated parameter space.

**Method.** Large-scale Stochastic Series Expansion (SSE) Quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) simulations were employed to map the ground state phase diagram.

**Model / system.** The model is an extended chequerboard J-Q Hamiltonian, incorporating nearest-neighbor Heisenberg interactions ($J$) and four-spin couplings ($Q, \lambda Q$) on different plaquettes. It interpolates between known limits like the original CBJQ model ($\lambda=0$) and the $J-Q2$ model ($\lambda=1$).

**Key observables.** Binder ratios ($U_s, U_p, U_{VBS}$), order parameters ($	ext{m}_p, 	ext{m}_s$), and probability distributions $P(D_x, D_y)$ are used to diagnose phase transitions and symmetries.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The coupling constants $Q$ and $\lambda$, which define the parameter space $(Q, \lambda)$, are crucial. Specific limits include $\lambda=0$ (O(4) symmetry) and $\lambda=1$ (O(5) symmetry).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that Binder ratio crossing points provide a reliable method for locating phase transitions via finite-size scaling, although computational limitations sometimes restrict the focus to certain ratios.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the dependence of binder ratios ($U_{VBS}$) on $\lambda$ across various values. Other figures show order parameter distributions $P(D_x, D_y)$ confirming O(4) symmetry for $\lambda < 1$.

**Paper structure.** The paper systematically analyzes the model by first establishing the Hamiltonian and relevant physical context (AFM to PS transition). It then applies QMC simulations across varying coupling parameters ($\lambda$) using diagnostic tools like Binder ratios. The core of the work is mapping the phase diagram, confirming the nature (first-order) and symmetry (O(4)) of the primary transition.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The chequerboard $J-Q$ model was proposed to describe the direct phase transition from the antiferromagnetic (AFM) state to the plaquette-sin glet (PS) solid state observed in SrCu$_2({\rm BO}_3)_2$. In this paper, we present a Monte Carlo study of the ground state of an extended ve rsion of this model. For all parameters investigated, we find only a direct first-order phase transitions from the AFM to the PS phase, with no intermediate phase between them. On the transition line, the system exhibits an emergent $O(4)$ symmetry. Furthermore, we find that the Bi nder ratio of the columnar valence-bond solid state can be used to locate the phase transition. It exhibits a monotonic finite-size scaling b ehavior, allowing for a precise determination of the transition point.

</details>

### [Randomised mixed labyrinth fractals](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07241v1)

**Authors:** Janett Prehl, Ligia Loretta Cirstea, Daniel Dick  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2606.07241v1>

**Summary.** This work introduces randomised mixed labyrinth fractals to study their topological properties by randomly mixing generating patterns. The authors analyze how this randomness affects key metrics like arc dimension and shortest path scaling using advanced matrix methods. It provides a flexible theoretical framework for studying fractal dynamics relevant to physical systems like diffusion.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on geometry, the study of complex fractal dimensions and transport properties in disordered media (like random networks) shares mathematical techniques with understanding quantum localization or energy transport in disordered potentials.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper introduces and analyzes 'randomised mixed labyrinth fractals,' generalizing existing fractal structures to study how random pattern mixing affects their topological properties.

**Main result.** It demonstrates that isotropy extends to this randomised class, and various scaling behaviors (linear, nonlinear, maxima) for shortest path dimensions are identified based on the mixing probability.

**Method.** The analysis employs path matrices derived from labyrinth patterns, proposing an approximated path matrix as an efficient alternative to extensive iterative simulations.

**Model / system.** The model consists of labyrinth fractals, which are finitely ramified Sierpiński carpets constructed iteratively by combining randomly selected 'labyrinth patterns' with fixed probabilities.

**Key observables.** Arc dimensions, shortest path dimensions ($d_{\min}$), path lengths ($\ell_n(\gamma)$), and isotropy restoration.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The mixing probability $p$, the structure of individual labyrinth patterns (A and A'), and the iteration level $n$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis often relies on approximating complex iterative processes using combined path matrices, assuming convergence to a stable scaling factor $\lambda$ or dimension.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate examples of labyrinth patterns, mixed sets ($W_2$), and show the dependence of calculated dimensions ($\langle d_{\min} angle$) on the mixing probability $p$, revealing monotonic trends or maxima.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses by defining labyrinth fractals, detailing path matrix construction for self-similar cases, extending this to mixed/randomised systems using combined matrices, and finally analyzing the convergence and scaling of various dimension measures as a function of the mixing probability $p$.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this paper, the class of randomised mixed labyrinth fractals is introduced. It is a class of finitely ramified Sierpinski carpets that generalize mixed labyrinth fractals. The structures are generated by randomly selected labyrinth patterns with fixed selection probabilities at each iteration level, offering a flexible framework to study fractal topology, arc dimensions, and shortest path properties. Here, the focus lies on analysing how the randomised mixing of patterns - specifically their shape, symmetry, and path geometry - effects arc dimensions, path lengths, and isotropy restoration. The study reveals that isotropy, previously shown for self-similar fractals, extends to the randomised mixed class. Various scaling behaviours of shortest path dimensions with respect to the mixing probability are identified, including linear and nonlinear monotonic trends, as well as transitions with maxima. The approximated path matrix is proposed as an efficient alternative to extensive iterative simulations, reliably reproducing statistical results. The findings highlight the relevance of pattern properties in determining fractal structures and dynamics and suggest applications in physical systems such as diffusion, signal processing, and antenna design.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper surveys the rapidly advancing field of Machine-Learned Interatomic Potentials (MLIPs), which promise to bridge the gap between computational scale and accuracy in materials science simulations. It synthesizes current knowledge by defining a set of six fundamental, unanswered questions that researchers must tackle regarding model expressivity, data requirements, and physical generalization.

**Why it may be interesting.** While focused on condensed matter, the rigorous framework for analyzing model limitations (expressivity vs. data scaling) mirrors challenges in developing complex quantum simulation methods that rely on scalable neural representations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The field of Machine-Learned Interatomic Potentials (MLIPs) needs to define its fundamental boundaries by addressing several open questions regarding model design, data requirements, and physical scope.

**Main result.** The paper articulates six key open questions—covering expressivity, scalability, memorization, multimodality, and compositionality—that must guide future research in foundation MLIP models.

**Method.** A conceptual framework is developed by adapting general AI foundation model taxonomies to the specific physical constraints and mathematical structures of interatomic potentials.

**Model / system.** The focus is on Machine-Learned Interatomic Potentials (MLIPs) used to approximate the Born-Oppenheimer potential energy surface for molecular and materials systems. These models aim to replace traditional, physically parameterized force fields.

**Key observables.** Potential energy, atomic forces, stresses, thermodynamic properties, and prediction accuracy across various physical regimes (e.g., defect formation energies).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Model expressivity, dataset size/diversity, computational cost, and the incorporation of physical symmetries like $E(3)$-equivariance.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The primary assumption is that progress in MLIPs requires addressing fundamental theoretical limitations rather than just incremental architectural improvements or data scaling alone.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 maps general AI foundation model criteria (expressivity, etc.) to the six open questions for MLIPs. Other figures illustrate performance gains versus training set size using specific architectures like NequIP.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the context of MLIPs, defines a working concept of 'foundation' models, and then systematically explores and articulates the six most critical unanswered questions defining the cutting edge of research in this domain.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Machine-learned interatomic potentials (MLIPs) have had a profound impact on molecular modelling in recent years, promising to resolve the long-standing tension between the scale and accuracy of simulations. There has been a proliferation of new models and designs, and recently the paradigm of ``foundational'' MLIPs has become prevalent. Broadly speaking, foundation models are trained on large diverse datasets and promise to work well for new systems with minimal updates required. However, in such a new and fast moving field, there are many unanswered questions. In this article, we set out to articulate and explore what we see as the most important among these questions. We start by developing a working definition for foundational MLIPs and use this definition to frame the subsequent open questions. Despite the rapid progress in the field of MLIP models, we believe that these are fundamental questions which will continue to define cutting edge research in MLIPs in the years to come.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2606.07137"></a>
### [Inverse Melting of 3D Antiferromagnetic Order in Multi-sublattice Magnetic Perovskites](http://arxiv.org/abs/2606.07137v1)
